Additional Miracles: Character, Scriptures, and Unique Name
Sanusi enumerates additional miracles and signs confirming prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional miracles and signs of prophethood beyond the Quran, including the Prophet's perfect character, mention in previous scriptures, and the unique sign of his name.

Core Idea
The Prophet's truthfulness is confirmed by his character, ancient scriptures, and even his unique name.

Sanusi continues his discussion of miracles by presenting further proofs that establish the Prophet's truthfulness. These include his noble character, which itself was a sign, the mention of him in earlier scriptures, the testimony of Jewish and Christian scholars, and a remarkable divine sign regarding his name. Each of these adds layers of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage aloud
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the rhythmic listing of proofs.
2
Identify the four proofs
Look for four distinct signs: perfect character, mention in scriptures, scholars' testimony, and prohibition of his name. Count them.
The passage starts with 'then' indicating an addition.
3
Focus on the unique name sign
The last part says God prevented Arabs before him from using his specific name. Think about why this is a powerful sign: it removes doubt about identity.
The text cuts off, but the sense is clear.
4
Reflect on cumulative certainty
Ask yourself: how do multiple independent proofs strengthen belief in prophethood? Each proof addresses a different aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هذا إلى ما لهُ مِنَ المعجزاتِ التي لا تُحصى ، ثم إلى ما جُبِلَتْ عليهِ ذاتُهُ الكريمةُ مِنَ الكمالاتِ التي كادَتْ أنْ تُفصِحَ - بل أفصحَتْ - قبل مبعثِهِ برسالتِهِ خَلْقاً وخُلْقاً . ثم معَ ذلكَ كلّهِ : أَكَّدَ الله تعالى صدْقَهُ بذكرِهِ بِاسمِهِ وبجميع وصفِهِ في الكتبِ الماضية ، قال تعالى : ﴿ الَّذِينَ يَتَّبِعُونَ الرَّسُولَ النَّبِيَّ الْأُمّى ... ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٥٧] الآية وأطلق السنة الأحبار قريباً مِنْ مبعثه بجميع ذلك . حتى إِنَّهُ سبحانَهُ بفضلِهِ ممَّا أَكَدَ بهِ زوالَ اللَّبْسِ عن نبوّتِهِ أنْ منعَ العرب قبلَهُ مِنَ التسمّي باسمِهِ الخاص بهِ ، إِلا أُناساً
Then in addition to this, there are his innumerable miracles, and then the perfections that his noble essence was created upon, which almost expressed – rather, they did express – before his mission with his message, in form and character. Then, along with all that, God Almighty affirmed his truthfulness by mentioning him by his name and all his descriptions in previous scriptures. He said: 'Those who follow the Messenger, the unlettered Prophet...' (Quran 7:157) the verse. And the rabbis and priests spoke openly shortly before his mission about all that. So much so that He, glorified be He, out of His grace, from what He affirmed to remove doubt about his prophethood, He prevented the Arabs before him from naming themselves with his specific name, except for some people.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents additional proofs beyond the Quran itself. First, the Prophet had innumerable miracles (muʿjizāt). Second, his noble character and perfect qualities, both physical and moral, almost declared his prophethood before his mission. Third, God affirmed his truthfulness in previous scriptures by mentioning him by name and description, as in Quran 7:157. Fourth, Jewish and Christian scholars (aḥbār) spoke openly about his imminent advent. Finally, as a unique sign, God prevented any Arab before him from being named with his specific name (presumably 'Muhammad'), except for a few, thus removing any doubt about his identity.

Key Terms
المعجزات
الكمالات
الكتب الماضية
الأحبار
التسمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Prophet's virtues are exaggerated or subjective.
Correction: Sanusi presents these virtues as objective divine signs that God placed to confirm his prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's truthfulness is supported by numerous miraculous signs, including his character, scriptural mentions, and a unique divine sign regarding his name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Prophet's character 'almost expressed his prophethood before his mission.' Does your character—your habits, your speech, your reactions—clearly reflect your faith to those around you? What is one change you can make this week to let your character 'express' your belief?
?
The previous scriptures spoke of him, and scholars testified about his coming. If you have never read the biblical prophecies about the Prophet, what is stopping you from seeking them out? Will you commit to studying at least one such reference to strengthen your faith?

The Bee's Inspiration: Answering the Second Objection
Sanusi answers the objection that the bee is unaware of its action by invoking miracles and inspiration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the concept of kharq al-ada (breaking of habit) and divine inspiration to answer the objection that animals acting instinctively cannot be evidence for a Creator.

Core Idea
Even an animal's instinct can be a miracle guided by Allah.

This passage continues Sanusi's defense of the argument from design. The objector raises a second point: even if the bee's honeycomb is indeed its own action, the bee is unaware of the precision it achieves. Sanusi's answer is fascinating: he does not deny that the bee acts, but he insists that at that moment it is granted miraculous knowledge through divine inspiration. This shows that the precision comes from Allah, not from the bee's own reason. A Quranic example of the ant is cited.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
The objector concedes that the bee's action might be its own, but argues that the bee is unaware of the fine details. Recognise this as a second attempt to undermine the argument from design.
This objection is more subtle than the first; it grants the action but denies knowledge.
2
Identify the Answer's Structure
Sanusi uses a conditional acceptance: 'even if we grant... we do not grant...'. Notice that he does not reject the premise entirely; instead, he shifts the ground to miraculous inspiration.
This is a typical kalam debating technique: accepting a premise for the sake of argument.
3
Grasp the Key Terms
Focus on 'kharq al-ada' (breaking of the norm) and 'ilham' (inspiration). These terms are central to understanding how divine intervention occurs in the natural world.
Kharq al-ada is the standard term for miracles in the kalam tradition.
4
See the Evidence
The ant of Solomon is cited as a Quranic precedent for animals receiving knowledge by divine inspiration. Reflect on how this strengthens Sanusi's case.
Quran 27:18 provides direct scriptural support for animal inspiration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو سلَّمْنا أَنَّهُ مِنْ فعلها فلا نُسلّمُ أَنَّهَا غيرُ عالمةٍ بِهِ حِينَئِذٍ، خُرِقَتْ في حقها العادةُ، والهِمَتْ علمَ ذلك، وخُلِقَ لها كما خُلِقَ للنملة علم بسليمانَ عليهِ السلامُ وبجنودِهِ حتى قالَتْ : ﴿ يَتَأَيُّهَا النَّمْلُ ادْخُلُوا مَسَكِنَكُمْ ﴾ [النمل : ١٨] .
And if we grant that it is from its action, we do not grant that it is unaware of it at that time; a breaking of the norm (kharq al-ada) occurred in its case, and it was inspired with knowledge of that, and knowledge was created for it just as knowledge was created for the ant concerning Solomon, peace be upon him, and his armies until it said: 'O ants, enter your dwellings' (Quran 27:18).
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This is a second objection and its answer. The objector concedes that the action might be from the bee but argues that the bee is unaware of the fine details of what it does. The answer rejects this: even if we grant that the action is from the bee, we do not grant that it is unaware at that moment. Rather, a miracle (kharq al-ada) occurred in the bee's case: it was divinely inspired with the knowledge of that shape, and that knowledge was created in it. This is analogous to the ant in the Quran (27:18) that was given knowledge of Solomon and his army and warned the other ants. Thus, the bee's action, being miraculous, still points to the Creator's knowledge and power, not to the bee's own intellect.

Key Terms
خرق العادة
إلهام
علم
نملة
سليمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a non-rational animal cannot perform miraculous acts or receive divine knowledge.
Correction: The passage shows that Allah can inspire animals and break natural norms for them, as demonstrated by the ant that recognized Solomon and his armies.

Checkpoint
What does the author cite as an example of a non-rational animal being given knowledge by divine inspiration?
A) The bee building the honeycomb
B) The ant recognizing Solomon and warning others
C) The spider weaving a web
D) The hoopoe bringing news to Solomon

Key Takeaway
The bee's precise honeycomb, even if its own action, is a miracle because Allah inspires it, proving the existence of a powerful Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the intricate design of a beehive, do you recognize it as a direct act of Allah's inspiration or just a natural phenomenon? What would change in your heart if you truly saw it as a miracle?
?
If Allah can grant precise knowledge to a tiny bee, how much more can He guide you in your life? Are you actively seeking His inspiration through prayer and reflection, or are you relying solely on your own reasoning?
?
The ant was given knowledge of Prophet Solomon and his army. Are there events in your life that you dismiss as coincidence but might actually be signs from Allah? What would it take for you to start seeing them as divine messages?

Critique of First Response: Miracle Proof Requires Tawhid
Sanusi critiques the first response, arguing miracle's evidential value depends on tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why establishing tawhid is logically prior to using miracles as evidence for prophethood, and why the first response's appeal to rational indication alone is insufficient.

Core Idea
Rational indication of miracles depends on tawhid; it cannot be an independent proof.

In this passage, Sanusi continues his refutation of objections against the necessity of knowing tawhid before prophethood. He focuses on the first response, which claimed that the miracle's rational indication is enough without prior tawhid. Sanusi dissects this claim, showing that the miracle being an exclusive act of Allah is a foundational pillar (rukn) of that rational proof, and this pillar itself requires knowledge of Allah's unity. This lesson will walk through his critique and the supporting citations he provides.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the complete Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice the structure: Sanusi states the first response, then argues against it, citing Imam al-Razi and al-Muqattir.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the author's main claim
Sanusi argues that the first response fails because the miracle's rational indication is only valid if the miracle being Allah's act is NOT a pillar. But it IS a pillar, and that pillar depends on tawhid.
3
Understand the argument about the pillar
Sanusi explains that the imams made Allah's creation of the miracle a part of the proof. If you allow a partner in creation, you cannot attribute the miracle exclusively to Allah, so it cannot indicate His will to confirm the prophet.
This shows why tawhid is essential.
4
Examine the supporting citations
Sanusi quotes Imam Fakhr al-Razi and al-Muqattir to reinforce that the miracle being Allah's act is a pillar, and ignorance of this pillar is why some reject prophethood.
These citations strengthen the argument.
5
Grasp the conclusion: the error of making part an independent proof
Sanusi concludes that the source of error is 'making part of the proof on its own an independent proof.' The rational indication is only part; the full proof includes tawhid.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : ولا يخفى ضَعْفُ جوابيه معاً في غاية : أَمَّا الأَوَّلُ - وهو التمسُّكُ بقولِ الأستاذ في أنَّ دلالة المعجزة عقلية - : فلا يتم له ذلك إلا لو لم يكن كون الخارق فعلاً لله تعالى ركناً مِنَ الدليل ، أما إذا كانَ ركناً فيهِ ، وهو لا يتحقَّقُ إلا بمعرفة الوحدانية . . لم يصح ما ذكر ، وظاهر أنَّه ركن على كل قول في وجهِ دلالة المعجزة ؛ إذ معنى كون دلالتِها عقليةً عندَ مَنْ قالَ : أنَّ به خلْقَ اللهِ تعالى الخارقَ على وَفْقِ دعواه وتحديَهُ معَ العجز عن معارضته ، وتخصيصه بذلك .. يدلُّ على إرادة الله تعالى لتصديقه كما يدلُّ اختصاص الفعلِ بالوقتِ المعيَّنِ والمحل على إرادتِهِ تعالى لذلكَ بالضرورة ، هكذا قرَّرَهُ الأئمَّةُ رضي الله عنهم على ما يأتي فيهِ مِنَ البحث في موضعِهِ إِنْ شاءَ الله تعالى
فأنت ترى كيف جعلوا خلْقَ اللهِ تعالى للفعل جزءاً مِنَ الدليل وذلك لا يتم إلا بمعرفة الوحدانية له جلَّ وعلا ، ونفي أنْ يكونَ مَعَهُ شريك في ملكه ، وهو ظاهرُ ؛ لأنَّهُ لو جُوِّزَ أَنَّ ثَمَّ مَنْ يَشَارِكُهُ تعالى في اختراع الكائناتِ .. لم يتحقق حينئذ كونُ الخارقِ فعلهُ تعالى حتى يدلنا أنَّهُ تعالى أراد بإيجاده تصديق نبيه ، ولهذا قال الإمامُ الفخرُ في المعالم : ( إنَّ المنكرينَ للنبوَّةِ طعنوا في المعجزة مِنْ ثلاثة أوجه : أحدها : قالوا : لِمَ قلتُم : إِنَّ هذهِ المعجزاتِ فعلُ الله تعالى وخلقه ؟! فانظر إلى الطعن بهذا الوجه كيف هو صريح في أنَّ كونَ الخارقِ فعلاً للهِ جَلَّ وعزَّ ركن في دلالة المعجزة ، وإثباته متوقف على معرفة الوحدانية ، فوجب توقُفُ معرفة النبوَّة على الوحدانية ضرورة توقفها على دلالة المعجزة المتوقفة عليها وقد صرح المقترح في شرح الإرشاد بأنَّ كونَ الفعل مخلوقاً الله تعالى ركن في المعجزة؛ لأنَّهُ قالَ في فصل النبوَّاتِ : ( وما أُتِيَ أحدٌ مِنْ منكري النبوَّاتِ في جحدِ دلالة المعجزاتِ إِلا مِنْ وَجْهِ الجهلِ بأركانها ، فقد يجهلُ أنَّ الخارق للعادة فعل الله تعالى ثم قال : ( وقد يعتقدُ أَنَّهُ ليس خارقاً للعادة ، وأنَّهُ ممَّا يجوزُ التوصُّلُ إليهِ بالحيل والغوص في العلوم ، فأَمَّا مَنْ هُدِيَ لمسلكِ الحقِّ ، وعرفَ أنَّ الذي وقعَ بهِ التحدّي فعل الله تعالى ، وهو عالم بدعوى المتحدّي ، وأنَّهُ لا يَتوصَّلُ إليه بالحيل ، وأنَّه خارق للعادة ، فعلَهُ الله تعالى على وَفْقِ دعوى النبي إجابةً له . . لم يسترب في حصولِ العلم ، ولا يختص ذلك بصورةٍ ، ولا يفتقر في دلالته إلى مثال يُضرَبُ في الشاهدِ ( انتهى وبالجملة : فمثارُ الغلط في جوابِ صاحبنا عن إيراد شرف الدين . . جعلُ بعض الدليل على الانفرادِ دليلاً مستقلاً
I said: The weakness of both his responses is not hidden, to the utmost. As for the first—which is clinging to the master's statement that the indication of the miracle is rational—: it is not complete for him except if the fact that the extraordinary event is an act of Allah Most High were not a pillar of the proof. But if it is a pillar in it, and it is not realized except by knowledge of unity, then what he mentioned is not correct. And it is apparent that it is a pillar according to every view concerning the manner of the miracle's indication; because the meaning of its indication being rational, according to those who said that the creation by Allah Most High of the miraculous event in accordance with his claim and his challenge, along with inability to oppose it, and its specification thereby, indicates the will of Allah Most High to confirm him, just as the specification of the act to a particular time and place indicates His will for that necessarily. Thus the imams (may Allah be pleased with them) established it, as will come of discussion in its place, if Allah wills. So you see how they made the creation of Allah Most High of the act a part of the proof, and that is not completed except by knowledge of His unity and glory, and negation that there be with Him a partner in His dominion. And it is apparent; because if it were permitted that there be someone who shares with Him Most High in inventing existents, then the fact that the extraordinary event is His act would not be realized, so that it would indicate to us that He Most High willed by its bringing into existence the confirmation of His prophet. For this reason Imam Fakhr said in al-Ma'alim: 'Indeed the deniers of prophethood attacked the miracle from three aspects: one of them: they said: Why do you say that these miracles are the act of Allah Most High and His creation?!' So look at the attack in this aspect: how it is explicit that the fact that the extraordinary event is an act of Allah Almighty is a pillar in the indication of the miracle, and its proof depends on knowledge of unity. Thus knowledge of prophethood necessarily depends on unity, because it depends on the indication of the miracle which depends on it. And al-Muqattir clearly stated in Sharh al-Irshad that the fact that the act is created by Allah Most High is a pillar in the miracle; because he said in the chapter on prophethoods: 'No one among the deniers of prophethood came to reject the indication of miracles except from the aspect of ignorance of its pillars. For he may be ignorant that the extraordinary event is the act of Allah Most High.' Then he said: 'And he may believe that it is not extraordinary, and that it is something which can be achieved by tricks and delving into sciences. But as for one who is guided to the path of truth and knows that what occurred by way of challenge is the act of Allah Most High, and he knows the claim of the challenger, and that it cannot be achieved by tricks, and that it is extraordinary, Allah Most High did it in accordance with the claim of the prophet in answer to him—he does not doubt in obtaining knowledge. And that is not specific to any form, nor does its indication need an example to be given in the witnessed world.' (End.) In summary: the source of error in our companion's response to Sharf al-Din's objection is making part of the proof on its own an independent proof.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the first of two responses made by the author's opponent (called 'our companion') to an objection raised by Sharf al-Din. The passage is a refutation (naqd) of that response. The author begins by stating that both responses are weak, then focuses on the first. The first response had appealed to the claim of 'the master' (perhaps a previous authority) that the indication of a miracle is rational ('aqli). The author argues that this rational indication only stands if the miracle being an act of Allah is not a pillar (rukn) of the proof. However, it is indeed a pillar on every view of how miracles indicate. The meaning of 'rational indication' here is that Allah's creation of the miracle in accordance with the prophet's claim and challenge, combined with inability to replicate it, indicates Allah's will to confirm the prophet—just as the specification of an act to a particular time and place indicates the agent's will. This was the established position of the imams. The author then explains that the imams made Allah's creation of the act a part of the proof, and that part cannot be known without knowledge of tawhid (unity). If one permitted a partner in creation, then the miracle could not be attributed exclusively to Allah, and thus would not indicate His will to confirm the prophet. The author cites Imam Fakhr al-Razi from his work al-Ma'alim, who noted that deniers of prophethood attacked miracles from three angles, one of which was questioning why we say miracles are Allah's act. This shows that the miracle being Allah's act is a pillar, and its proof depends on tawhid. Hence knowledge of prophethood depends on tawhid because it depends on the miracle's indication, which depends on tawhid. The author then cites al-Muqattir (likely 'the proposer' or a commentator) from Sharh al-Irshad, who explicitly states that the act being created by Allah is a pillar of the miracle. The commentator elaborates that a person may be ignorant of this pillar or may think the miracle is not extraordinary. But once one knows that the act of challenge is Allah's act and is extraordinary, one obtains certain knowledge without needing an example from the witnessed world. The author concludes that the source of error in the first response is 'making part of the proof on its own an independent proof'—i.e., focusing solely on the rational aspect without including the necessary condition that the miracle is Allah's exclusive act.

Key Terms
معجزة
دلالة عقلية
خارق للعادة
فعل الله
وحدانية
ركن الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mere occurrence of an extraordinary event is sufficient evidence for prophethood without first establishing that only Allah can create such events.
Correction: The miracle being Allah's exclusive act is a pillar of the proof. Without tawhid, one cannot attribute the miracle to Allah alone, so it cannot indicate His will to confirm the prophet.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the first response weak?
A) Because it denies that miracles are rational evidence.
B) Because it treats the rational indication of the miracle as independent, without including the condition that the miracle is Allah's act, which itself depends on tawhid.
C) Because it relies on a statement from Imam Fakhr al-Razi, who is not considered reliable.
D) Because it confuses the miracle with ordinary events.

Key Takeaway
The rational evidence of miracles depends on first establishing tawhid; treating the rational indication as an independent proof is a logical error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever witnessed or heard of something you considered a miracle? Did you first confirm in your heart that only Allah could do that, or did you instinctively attribute it to a person or cause? What does your reaction reveal about your conviction in tawhid?
?
If you were to meet someone who claims to be a prophet but does not first call people to the oneness of Allah, would you accept their miracles as proof? Why or why not? How does this lesson change the way you evaluate such claims?
?
Sanusi argues that making part of the proof an independent proof is the root error. Where in your own faith might you be picking one aspect of Islam (e.g., a miraculous story) without tying it to the foundation of tawhid? Will you commit to re-centering your understanding of every proof on Allah's oneness starting today?

The Miracle: Definition and Linguistic Expansion
Definition of miracle and two aspects of linguistic expansion in the term mu'jizah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the formal definition of a miracle (mu'jizah) and the two-fold linguistic expansion discussed by Imam al-Haramayn.

Core Idea
The term 'mu'jizah' is used leniently, not implying literal incapability.

The definition of a miracle is foundational for proving prophethood. This lesson presents the precise definition from the Sanusi tradition and then examines a subtle theological-linguistic point raised by Imam al-Haramayn about the term 'mu'jizah' itself. You will see how careful scholars are with language when speaking about divine acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Carefully read the definition of mu'jizah: an act of Allah, extraordinary, accompanying the claim, challenged before occurrence, not disproven, incapacitating opponents.
Notice the key conditions: act of Allah, khāriq li-l-ʿādah, etc.
2
Identify the two expansions
Imam al-Haramayn notes two ways the term 'mu'jizah' involves linguistic expansion (tawassuʿ). Identify both from the text.
The second expansion is incomplete in the source text.
3
Understand the first expansion
The first expansion concerns the word's implication of literal incapability (ʿajz). The passage argues that literal incapability is not applicable: if the miracle is beyond human power, no one was capable; if within human power, the incapability is simultaneous with the act. So 'incapability' is used metaphorically.
Compare with 'ignorance' used for absence of knowledge.
4
Examine the second expansion (incomplete)
The second expansion concerns the active participle form 'mu'jizah' suggesting an agent. The text cuts off, but the point is that the term implies a doer, whereas the true agent is Allah.
This expansion is incomplete in the source; the lesson notes this limitation.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why scholars engage in such linguistic analysis. How does this precision protect correct belief about Allah's power and the nature of miracles?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتفضل سبحانه بتأييدهم بالمعجزة الدالة على صدقهم ؛ وهي فعل الله سبحانه ، خارق للعادة ، مقارن لدعوى الرسالة ، متحدى به قبل وقوعه ، غير مكذب ، يعجز من يبغي معارضته عن الإتيان بمثله

المعجزة - اسم فاعل - : مأخوذة من الإعجاز مصدر أعجز ؛ وهي لفظ أطلق على الآية الدالة على صدق النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وذكر إمام الحرمين أن في إطلاق لفظ المعجزة عليها توسعا من وجهين

(أحدهما : أن اللفظ يشعر بحقيقة العجز ، ولا يصح ثبوت العجز ؛ لأنه إن كانت الآية ليست من جنس مقدور البشر فلا يصح لفظ العجز حقيقة عما ليس بمقدور ، وإن كانت من جنس مقدور البشر فالعجز عندنا يقارن المعجوز عنه ، والمعارضة منتفية ، فلا يصح ثبوت عجز متعلق بها ، فقد تسومح وأطلق العجز على انتفاء القدرة ، كما يتسامح في الجهل ويطلق على انتفاء العلم

الوجه الثاني في التوسع : أن لفظ ( المعجزة ) يشعر بفاعل
And He, exalted, favored by supporting them with the miracle indicating their truthfulness; and it is an act of Allah, exalted, breaking the norm, concurrent with the claim of prophethood, challenged with before its occurrence, not disproving, incapacitating whoever seeks to oppose it from producing its like.

Al-Mu'jizah – active participle – derived from i'jaz, the verbal noun of a'jaza; and it is a term applied to the sign indicating the truthfulness of the Prophet, peace be upon him. And Imam al-Haramayn mentioned that in the application of the term mu'jizah to it there is an expansion (tawassu') in two ways:

(First: that the word suggests the reality of incapability, and the establishment of incapability is not valid; because if the sign is not of the type of human capability, then the word 'incapability' is not literally valid for what is not within capability, and if it is of the type of human capability, then incapability in our view accompanies the thing incapacitated, and opposition is nonexistent, so the establishment of incapability related to it is not valid. So leniency was taken and 'incapability' was applied to the absence of power, just as leniency is taken in 'ignorance' and it is applied to the absence of knowledge.

The second way of expansion: that the word 'mu'jizah' suggests the agent...
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage provides the formal definition of a miracle (mu'jizah) as an act of Allah that is extraordinary (khāriq li-l-ʿādah), accompanies the claim of prophethood, is challenged before its occurrence, is not disproven, and incapacitates anyone attempting to produce its like. It then discusses the etymology and terminology: the word is derived from iʿjāz (making incapable). Imam al-Haramayn notes that applying the term 'mu'jizah' to the prophetic sign involves a two-fold linguistic expansion (tawassuʿ). The first expansion addresses the issue of literal incapability: if the miracle is beyond human power, reference to 'incapacitating' is not literal (since no one was capable); if it is within human power, the opponent's incapability is simultaneous with the act, so 'incapability' is used metaphorically, like using 'ignorance' for absence of knowledge. The second expansion (incomplete) concerns the agent implied by the active participle, suggesting a doer, though it is actually Allah's act.

Key Terms
معجزة
إعجاز
خارق للعادة
توسع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'mu'jizah' literally means 'that which causes incapability' and that the miracle itself makes people incapable.
Correction: The passage explains that the term is used with leniency; literal incapability is not applicable. The true meaning is that the miracle is a sign of divine power and indicates the truthfulness of the prophet.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason Imam al-Haramayn gives for the expansion (tawassuʿ) in the term 'mu'jizah'?
A) The word suggests a doer, but Allah is the true agent
B) The word suggests real incapability, but this is not literally applicable
C) The word is derived from a non-Arabic root
D) The word was originally used for a different concept

Key Takeaway
The term 'mu'jizah' is used with linguistic leniency; it does not imply literal incapability but serves as a sign of divine power supporting the prophet's truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the word 'mu'jizah' is used leniently—it does not literally mean 'causing incapability' but is a sign from Allah. When you hear the word 'miracle' in daily life, do you unconsciously attach more power to the event itself than to Allah who brought it about? How can you train your heart to immediately refer every extraordinary event back to the Creator rather than marvel at the event alone?
?
The passage says that if a miracle is beyond human capability, no one could even attempt it, so 'incapability' is not literal. Reflect: Are there areas in your life where you believe you are 'incapable' of obeying Allah (e.g., quitting a sin, maintaining prayer on time)? How does the lenient use of language here remind you that your incapability is not an excuse—your inability is only from your own weakness, not from Allah's command being impossible?
?
Imam al-Haramayn uses an analogy: just as 'ignorance' is applied to the absence of knowledge, so 'incapability' is applied to the absence of power. Think of a time you excused yourself by saying 'I just can't do it.' Does that statement reflect a true impossibility (like a miracle), or is it a lenient use of language covering up a lack of effort or reliance on Allah? Commit today to rephrase at least one excuse this week as 'I don't have the power yet, but Allah can give it to me' and act accordingly.

The First Condition of a Miracle: It Must Be an Act of Allah
Sanusi explains the first condition of miracle definition and discusses two opinions on acquired miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first condition of a miracle (that it is an act of Allah) excludes the eternal and why some scholars argue it must not be an acquired act.

Core Idea
A miracle is purely divine; it cannot be an eternal attribute nor a human acquisition.

Sanusi clarifies an essential element of the definition of miracle: it must be an act of Allah alone. He explains the first reservation (ihtirāz) that excludes anything eternal, and then introduces a debate about whether miracles can be 'acquired' (muktasaba) by the prophet. This passage establishes the Ash'ari insistence that a miracle is solely a divine act, not a human skill.

Guided Path
1
Understand the metaphorical naming
Sanusi begins by noting that 'miracle' is metaphorically named from 'inability' (ʿajz) because it is accompanied by human inability. Recognize that the doer is Allah.
2
Identify the first reservation
The first condition 'it is an act of Allah' serves to exclude the eternal (al-qadīm). Find the phrase 'فاحترز بالأَوَّلِ مِنَ القديمِ' and note why the eternal cannot be a miracle.
The eternal cannot be exclusive to a specific challenger.
3
Distinguish two types of acts
Sanusi includes both acts attached to originated power (like the Prophet's recitation) and acts not attached (like reviving the dead). Some scholars specify only the second type is a true miracle. Grasp this nuance.
4
Examine the debate on acquired miracles
The passage mentions two opinions on whether a miracle must not be acquired (muktasaba). The author favors the second opinion. Relate this to the example of the Qur'an's inimitability.
Ibn Dihaq is cited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العجز ، والله تعالى هو فاعل العجز ، فسُمِّيَ ما فُعِلَ العجز عندَهُ معجزاً مجازاً . وأما قوله : ( وهي فعلُ الله سبحانَهُ . . . ) إلى آخرِهِ : فشرحُ هذا يستبين ببيان ما احترزَ منهُ بكل قيدٍ مِنْ تلك القيود ، وإليهِ أَشارَ بقوله : [ احترازات الحدّ ] فاحترزَ بالأَوَّلِ مِنَ القديمِ ، فليس فعلا الله تعالى ، فلا يكون معجزةً ، ودخل فيه الفعل الذي تعلقت القدرة الحادثة بهِ ؛ كتلاوة النبيِّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ القرآنَ ، فهو معجزةٌ لرسولِ اللهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ دون غيره ؛ إذْ غيرُهُ إذا تلاه إنَّما يحكيه ، وليس هو الآخذ له عن المَلَكِ ودخلَ ما لا تتعلَّقُ بهِ القدرة الحادثة ؛ كإحياء الموتى ، وتكثير الطعام ، وانقياد الشجرِ ، ونحو ذلك ، وعَيَّنَ بعضُ أصحابنا في المعجزة : أن تكونَ مِنَ النوع الثاني لا الأَوَّلِ فتكون معجزة القرآن على هذا في نظمه المخصوص ، وإطلاع النبيُّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ على ذلكَ دون سائر الناس ، وكلا الأمرين ليسَ مِنْ فعله ولا كسبه ، وهذا الثاني أظهرُ ، والله تعالى أعلم يعني : أنَّه احترز بالشرطِ الأَوَّلِ ـ وهو كونُ المعجزة فعلاً للهِ تعالى ـ ممَّا لا يكون فعلاً لهُ تعالى ؛ كالصفة القديمة ، وإنَّما لم يصح أن يكونَ القديمُ معجزةً ؛ لعدم اختصاص بعض المتحدِّينَ بهِ دونَ بعض ثم ذكرت قولين في اشتراط ألا تكونَ المعجزة مكتسبة ، وقد ذكرهما ابنُ دهاقٍ في شرح الإرشادِ ومثله بتلاوة النبي صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ القرآنَ ، ونظيرُ ذلك أيضاً المشي على الماء ، والتحليق في جوّ السماء إذا وقع التحدّي بهما ؛ فإنَّ تلك الحركاتِ
Inability, and Allah Most High is the doer of inability, so what is called 'inability at it' is metaphorically called a miracle. As for His saying: 'And it is the act of Allah, exalted be He...' to the end: the explanation of this becomes clear by clarifying what was avoided by each of those conditions, and to that he pointed by his saying: 'Reservations of the definition.' So he avoided by the first [condition] the eternal; it is not an act of Allah Most High, so it is not a miracle. And included in it is the act to which originated power is attached, like the recitation of the Prophet, peace be upon him, of the Qur'an; it is a miracle for the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and not for others, since others when they recite it only imitate it and are not those who took it from the angel. And included is that to which originated power is not attached, like reviving the dead, multiplying food, the submission of trees, and the like. And some of our companions specified concerning the miracle: that it be of the second type, not the first; thus the miracle of the Qur'an is in its specific composition and the Prophet's, peace be upon him, being informed of that to the exclusion of other people; both matters are not from his act nor his acquisition. And the second is more apparent, and Allah Most High knows best. Meaning: that he avoided by the first condition – which is that the miracle is an act of Allah Most High – what is not an act of Him Most High, like the eternal attribute. And it is not correct that the eternal be a miracle because of the lack of exclusivity of some challengers over others. Then he mentioned two opinions concerning the condition that the miracle not be acquired, and Ibn Dihaq mentioned them in Sharh al-Irshad, and its example is the recitation of the Prophet, peace be upon him, of the Qur'an, and similarly walking on water and soaring in the air of the sky if challenge occurs by them; for those movements...
Source pages p. 546

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the definition of miracle (muʿjiza), focusing on the first condition (al-qayd al-awwal) that the miracle is an act of Allah, and the reservations (ihtirāzāt) that follow from it. The author begins by clarifying the metaphorical naming: the miracle is called such because it is accompanied by human inability, but the actual doer of inability is Allah. Then he states that the first reservation excludes the eternal (al-qadīm) – i.e. any eternal attribute or essence – because the eternal is not an act of Allah, so it cannot be a miracle. He then includes two types of acts: (1) acts to which originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) is attached, like the Prophet's recitation of the Qur'an; this is a miracle for the Prophet alone because others merely imitate it. (2) Acts to which originated power is not attached, like reviving the dead, multiplying food, the submission of trees. Regarding miracles, some scholars specify that it must be of the second type (not acquired), so the miracle of the Qur'an is in its specific composition and the Prophet's knowledge of it exclusive of others, as both are not from his act or kasb. The author deems the second opinion more apparent. Then he explains that the first condition guards against what is not an act of Allah, like the eternal attribute, because the eternal cannot be exclusive to some challengers. Finally, he mentions two opinions on the condition that the miracle not be acquired (muktasaba), citing Ibn Dihaq in Sharh al-Irshad, with examples: the Prophet's recitation, walking on water, and soaring in the air when challenge occurs. The passage thus defends the Ash'ari view that miracles are purely divine acts, not acquired by the prophet.

Key Terms
معجزة (muʿjiza)
فعل الله (fiʿl Allah)
القديم (al-qadīm)
القدرة الحادثة (al-qudra al-ḥāditha)
كسب (kasb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that a miracle is an act of the prophet or can be acquired like human actions.
Correction: The passage clarifies that a miracle is solely an act of Allah; even the Prophet's recitation is not his acquisition but a miracle given by Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the first condition (ihtirāz), what does the definition of miracle exclude?
A) Acts to which originated power is attached
B) The eternal (al-qadīm)
C) Acts not attached to originated power
D) The Prophet's recitation of the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
A miracle must be an act of Allah alone and cannot be an eternal attribute or a human acquisition; this preserves its divine exclusivity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you genuinely believe that every good deed or extraordinary outcome in your life is entirely from Allah, or do you subtly attribute it to your own ability? What would it take for you to internalize that even your recitation of the Quran is not your own doing but a gift from Him?
?
How does the fact that miracles cannot be acquired affect your understanding of the Prophet's role? Does it challenge your tendency to see him merely as a human hero rather than a vessel of divine power?
?
If a miracle is purely divine and cannot be earned, how should that shape your expectation of Allah's intervention in your life? Are you waiting for a miracle on your own terms, or are you ready to submit to His exclusive act?

First Condition of a Miracle: Act of God
Sanusi defines the first condition of a miracle and excludes eternal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first condition of a miracle according to Sanusi: that it must be an act of God and not an eternal attribute, and why eternal attributes cannot serve as miracles.

Core Idea
A miracle must be a created act of God, not an eternal attribute.

Sanusi begins by stating the position of his school regarding miracles: they belong to the second type, meaning they are not acquired by the prophet. He gives the example of the Quran as a miracle. Then he explains the first condition: a miracle must be an act of God, thereby excluding anything that is not an act of God, such as an eternal attribute. This passage lays the foundation for understanding what qualifies as a true miracle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow: the scholars' view, the example of the Quran, then the explanation of the condition.
2
Identify the two types of miracles
The passage mentions 'the second type' and 'the first'. Understand that the first type would be something acquired by the prophet (kash), while the second is purely from God. The miracle is of the second type.
The distinction is crucial for the argument.
3
Understand the first condition
The first condition is that the miracle is an act of God. This excludes anything that is not an act of God, such as eternal attributes (like God's essence or knowledge).
4
Reflect on why eternal attributes are excluded
An eternal attribute is not specific to any individual; it cannot be exclusive to a prophet challenging others. A miracle must be a unique event that supports a specific claim.
This is the key rationale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصحابنا في المعجزة : أن تكونَ مِنَ النوع الثاني لا الأَوَّلِ فتكون معجزة القرآن على هذا في نظمه المخصوص ، وإطلاع النبيُّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ على ذلكَ دون سائر الناس ، وكلا الأمرين ليسَ مِنْ فعله ولا كسبه ، وهذا الثاني أظهرُ ، والله تعالى أعلم . يعني : أنَّه احترز بالشرطِ الأَوَّلِ ـ وهو كونُ المعجزة فعلاً للهِ تعالى ـ ممَّا لا يكون فعلاً لهُ تعالى ؛ كالصفة القديمة ، وإنَّما لم يصح أن يكونَ القديمُ معجزةً ؛ لعدم اختصاص بعض المتحدِّينَ بهِ دون بعض
Our scholars regarding the miracle: that it is of the second type, not the first, so the miracle of the Quran is in its specific composition, and the Prophet's (peace be upon him) being apprised of that to the exclusion of other people, and both matters are not from his act nor his acquisition. And this second (view) is more apparent, and God most high knows best. Meaning: that he guarded by the first condition – which is that the miracle is an act of God most high – against that which is not an act of Him most high, like the eternal attribute. And it is not correct for the eternal (attribute) to be a miracle, because of the lack of exclusivity of some challengers with it over others.
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the first condition of a miracle. The author begins by stating the position of his school (our scholars) that the miracle is of the second type, not the first. This refers to a classification likely explained earlier: the first type would be something that is acquired by the prophet, while the second is not. He gives the example of the Quran: its miracle lies in its unique composition and in the Prophet's knowledge of that composition, both of which are not acts or acquisitions of the Prophet. He then says the second view (that it is not acquired) is more apparent. Then he explains the meaning: the first condition of a miracle is that it be an act of God most high. This condition excludes anything that is not an act of God, such as an eternal attribute. He explains why an eternal attribute cannot be a miracle: because an attribute belongs to God essentially and cannot be exclusive to a specific challenger (i.e., the prophet) as opposed to others. A miracle must be a specific act that God creates to support a prophet's claim, and it must be unique to that prophet's challenge. An eternal attribute is not specific to any created being, so it cannot serve as a sign.

معجزة
الصفة القديمة
النوع الثاني
الشرط الأول

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any extraordinary event, even a divine attribute, can be a miracle.
Correction: The author clarifies that a miracle must be a created act of God, not an eternal attribute, because it must be exclusive to the challenger.

Checkpoint

B) It must be an act of God and not an eternal attribute.
C) It must be acquired by the prophet.

Key Takeaway
A miracle must be an act of God, not an eternal attribute, because it must be exclusive to the prophet's challenge.

The Condition that a Miracle Be Non-Acquired: Two Opinions and an Objection
Sanusi discusses two opinions on the condition that a miracle be non-acquired, with examples and an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two scholarly opinions on whether a miracle must be non-acquired, and the objection raised against one of them.

Core Idea
A miracle is a sign from God, not a human achievement.

In this passage, Sanusi discusses a subtle point: is it necessary that a miracle not be an acquired act of the prophet? He presents two opinions, gives examples of extraordinary acts like walking on water, and recounts an objection by Imam al-Haramayn. Understanding this dispute clarifies the relationship between divine action and human power in miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two opinions
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Look for the two opinions mentioned regarding the condition that the miracle not be acquired (muktasabah).
The two opinions are not explicitly named but implied through the discussion by Ibn Dahaq.
2
Understand the examples: recitation, walking on water, hovering
Notice how these extraordinary acts are described as being both acts of God and within the power of servants, meaning humans can perform them but only by God's creation.
The phrase 'within the power of servants' is explained in the commentary.
3
Follow Imam al-Haramayn's position
He considered these acts to be miracles from the perspective of God's creation, and inclined to the view that the power (qudrah) over them is itself a miracle.
This is a significant refinement of the definition.
4
Grasp the objection and its conclusion
The objection states that the power cannot be a miracle unless it is preceded by a specific claim (da'wa). Understand why this matters: miracles must be signs following a claim.
The objection ties the miracle to the prophetic challenge (tahaddi).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكرت قولين في اشتراط ألا تكونَ المعجزة مكتسبة ، وقد ذكرهما ابنُ دهاقٍ في شرح الإرشادِ . ومثله بتلاوة النبي صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ القرآنَ ، ونظيرُ ذلك أيضاً المشي على الماء ، والتحليق في جوّ السماء إذا وقع التحدّي بهما ؛ فإنَّ تلك الحركاتِ فعل الله تعالى ، وهي أيضاً مقدورة للعبادِ ؛ بمعنى : أنَّ القدرة الحادثة تتعلق بها لا على سبيل التأثير ، وجعلها إمام الحرمين معجزةً مِنْ حيث فعلها البارى ، لا مِنْ حيثُ كونها مكتسبةٌ ، ومال إلى أنَّ القدرة على ذلك معجزة . وأُورِدَ عليهِ : بأَنَّهُ إِذا وقع التحدّي بنفس الحركة الخارقة للعادة فلا يمكن أن تكون القدرة معجزةً ، وإنْ كانَتْ فعلاً للهِ تعالى خارقة للعادة غير مكتسبة ؛ لأنَّ شرط ثبوتِ كونِ الخارقِ معجزةً : أنْ يكونَ مسبوقاً بدعواه آية ، فينبغي ألا تكونَ القدرة معجزة إلا أن يتحدى بها النبي ، وباقي لفظ العقيدة واضح
Then he mentioned two opinions on the condition that the miracle not be acquired, and Ibn Dahaq mentioned them in his commentary on al-Irshad. And similar to it is the recitation of the Prophet (peace be upon him) of the Quran, and likewise walking on water and hovering in the air of the sky when the challenge is issued with them; for those movements are an act of God most high, and they are also within the power of the servants, meaning that the originated power is related to them not by way of effect. And Imam al-Haramayn made it a miracle from the perspective of God's creating it, not from the perspective of its being acquired, and he inclined to the view that the power over that is a miracle. And it was objected to him: that if the challenge is issued with the extraordinary movement itself, then the power cannot be a miracle, even if it is an extraordinary act of God most high that is not acquired, because the condition for establishing the extraordinary as a miracle is that it be preceded by a claim of a miracle, so it is necessary that the power not be a miracle unless the prophet challenges with it. And the rest of the wording of the Creed is clear.
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a disputed condition regarding miracles: whether the miracle must be non-acquired (muktasabah) or can be acquired by the prophet. The author notes two opinions on this condition, referenced by Ibn Dahaq. He then gives examples: the Prophet's recitation of the Quran, walking on water, and hovering in the air, when these are used as challenges. These movements are acts of God, but they are also within the power of servants (i.e., humans can perform them in principle, but not with the same extraordinary nature). The phrase 'within the power of the servants' means that the originated power (al-qudrah al-hadithah) relates to these actions, but not by way of effect (i.e., the servant's power does not create the action; it only makes the action possible by God's creation). Imam al-Haramayn (Juwayni) considered these actions to be miracles from the perspective that God creates them, not from the perspective of being acquired. He inclined to the view that the power (qudrah) over such actions is itself a miracle. An objection is then raised: if the challenge is issued with the extraordinary movement itself (e.g., walking on water), then the power to do it cannot be a miracle because the condition for something extraordinary to be a miracle is that it be preceded by a claim (da'wa) of prophethood. In other words, the miracle must be a sign that follows the prophet's claim. If the power is not directly linked to the claim, it is not a miracle. Therefore, the power itself is only a miracle if the prophet explicitly challenges with it. The author concludes that the rest of the Creed's wording is clear on this point.

Key Terms
مكتسبة
قدرة حادثة
تحدي
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any extraordinary act done by a prophet is automatically a miracle.
Correction: The objection shows that the power to do an extraordinary act is only a miracle if it is preceded by a specific claim (da'wa) of prophethood. The act itself can be a miracle, but the power (qudrah) is not necessarily one.

Checkpoint
According to the objection mentioned, when is the power (qudrah) over an extraordinary act considered a miracle?
A) Whenever it is an act of God.
B) When it is acquired by the prophet.
C) Only when the prophet challenges with it.
D) When it is an eternal attribute.

Key Takeaway
A miracle is not defined by the act's extraordinariness alone, but by being a sign that follows the prophet's claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see something extraordinary in your life—whether a blessing or a trial—do you immediately recognize it as a sign from God, or do you attribute it to your own abilities? How does this lesson's condition of a 'claim' challenge you to re-evaluate the source and purpose of every remarkable event?
?
This passage shows that a miracle is defined by being a sign linked to a divine claim. In your daily actions, what are you 'claiming' through your behavior? Are your actions pointing others to God, or merely to yourself?
?
The objection insists that the power itself is not a miracle unless explicitly challenged. Is there an area in your life where you assume your abilities are from you alone, forgetting that even the power to act is created by God? How can you consciously attribute your capabilities to Him starting today?

The Definition of Miracle and the Objection of Non-Action
Sanusi defines miracle, discusses condition of action, and resolves objection of non-action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of a miracle, the objection that it must be an act, and how the challenge by non-action is resolved.

Core Idea
A miracle can be a divinely caused non-action, with the real miracle being Allah's knowledge and the prophet's informing.

This passage delves into a nuanced debate within Ash'ari theology about the precise definition of a miracle. While the standard definition states a miracle is an act of Allah, the question arises: can a prophet challenge by non-action, such as claiming immunity from harm? Sanusi presents the objection and offers the answer that the miracle in such cases is the knowledge and foretelling of that non-action, which is itself an act of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: definition, objection, answer.
2
Identify the core definition of miracle
Find the initial definition: a miracle is an act of Allah, and it is within the capability of servants (muktasaba). Note the nuance added by Imam al-Haramayn.
3
Understand the objection about ability (qudra)
The passage presents an objection that if the challenge is by the action itself, the ability cannot be a miracle. Grasp the reasoning behind this.
This objection is against al-Juwayni's view.
4
Follow the objection of non-action and its answer
Read how the prophet can challenge by non-action (e.g., immunity from harm). Then see the answer: the miracle is the knowledge and informing of that non-action, which is an act of Allah.
Also note al-Ash'ari's addition 'or what takes its place'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعل الله تعالى، وهي أيضاً مقدورة للعبادِ؛ بمعنى أنَّ القدرة الحادثة تتعلق بها لا على سبيل التأثير، وجعلها إمام الحرمين معجزةً مِنْ حيث فعلها البارى، لا مِنْ حيثُ كونها مكتسبةٌ، ومال إلى أنَّ القدرة على ذلك معجزة. وأُورِدَ عليهِ: بأَنَّهُ إِذا وقع التحدّي بنفس الحركة الخارقة للعادة فلا يمكن أن تكون القدرة معجزةً، وإنْ كانَتْ فعلاً للهِ تعالى خارقة للعادة غير مكتسبة؛ لأنَّ شرط ثبوتِ كونِ الخارقِ معجزةً: أنْ يكونَ مسبوقاً بدعواه آية، فينبغي ألا تكونَ القدرة معجزة إلا أن يتحدى بها النبي، وباقي لفظ العقيدة واضح.
فإن قلت: قد يتحدى النبيُّ بعدم الفعل، كما قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «قَدْ عَصَمَنِي رَبِّي»، وكما قال نوح عليه السلام: ﴿ثُمَّ أَقْضُوا إِلَى وَلَا تُنظرُون﴾ [يونس: 71]، فقد وقع التحدي بعدم وقوع الفعل كالضرب والقتل.
فالجواب: أَنَّ علمَهُ وإخبارَهُ بذلكَ على وَفْقِ ما ظهر.. هو المعجزةُ، وهو فعل الله خلقَهُ لهُ، ومنهم مَنْ قَبِلَ هذا الاعتراض، فزادَ لإدخال ما ورد بعد قوله في شروط المعجزة: ( وهو فعل الله تعالى ) فقالَ: ( أو ما يقومُ مَقامَهُ ).
هذا سؤال يتوجه على اشتراطِ كونِ المعجزة فعلاً؛ وذلكَ أنَّ المعجزة قد تكون عدم فعل، لا فعلاً؛ كالتحدي بالعصمةِ مِنْ إذاية الخلْقِ في الحالين المذكورين؛ فإنَّ المتحدى به عدم الفعل منهم كالضرب والقتل، ومثله إذا قال المتحدّي المدَّعي للنبوَّة: ( آيتي: ألا يقوم أحدٌ في هذا الإقليم مُدَّةٌ ضربَها، ولأجل هذا السؤالِ قالَ الشيخُ أبو الحسن الأشعري رحمه الله: المعجزة فعل أو ما يقومُ مَقامَ الفعل.
أجابَ ابنُ دهاق بالجواب الذي ذكرته في العقيدة؛ وهو المعجزة إلى العلم بذلك، والإخبارِ بهِ على وَفْقِ الواقع.
[The miracle is] an act of Allah, Most High, and it is also within the capability of servants, meaning that the acquired ability relates to it not as an effect. Al-Imam al-Haramayn considered it a miracle in terms of its being an act of the Creator, not in terms of its being acquired, and he inclined to the view that the ability [to perform] that is a miracle. It was objected to him: that if the challenge takes place by the action itself that breaks the norm, then the ability cannot be a miracle, even if it is an act of Allah, Most High, breaking the norm and not acquired, because the condition for establishing that the extraordinary thing is a miracle is that it be preceded by a claim of [proving] a sign, so the ability should not be a miracle unless the prophet challenges by it, and the rest of the text of the creed is clear. If you say: The prophet may challenge by non-action, as he (peace and blessings be upon him) said: 'My Lord has protected me,' and as Noah (peace be upon him) said: 'Then proceed to me and do not give me respite' [Yunus 71], so the challenge occurs by the non-occurrence of an action like striking and killing. The answer: That his knowledge and his informing of that in accordance with what appeared is the miracle, and it is an act of Allah which He created for him. And among them is one who accepted this objection and added, to include what was mentioned after his statement in the conditions of the miracle (And it is an act of Allah, Most High), and he said: 'Or what takes its place.' This is a question directed at the condition that the miracle be an act; because the miracle may be a non-action, not an act, like the challenge of protection from harm to him by creation in the two mentioned cases; for what is challenged by is the non-action from them like striking and killing, and similarly if the challenger claiming prophethood says: 'My sign is that no one will stand in this region for a specified period.' And for this reason, Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The miracle is an act or what takes the place of an act. Ibn Dihaq answered with the answer that I mentioned in the creed, namely the miracle [is attributed] to the knowledge of that and the informing of it in accordance with reality.
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the definition of a miracle (mu'jiza). It begins by restating the standard Ash'ari definition: a miracle is an act of Allah, and it is also within the capability of servants (muktasaba) in the sense of acquisition (kasb), not as causing the effect. The author then discusses a nuance: Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) considered the miracle to be the act of the Creator, not the acquired act, and he tended to say that the ability (qudra) to perform the miracle is itself a miracle. An objection is raised against this: if the challenge (tahaddi) is made by the extraordinary action itself, then the ability cannot be a miracle, because the condition for an extraordinary act to be a miracle is that it be preceded by a claim of prophethood. So the ability would only be a miracle if the prophet challenges specifically by it. The author then presents a further objection: a prophet may challenge by non-action, as the Prophet Muhammad said 'My Lord has protected me' (showing his immunity from harm) and as Noah said 'Then proceed to me and do not give me respite' (challenging his enemies to harm him but they cannot). In these cases, the challenge is by the absence of an action (like striking or killing). The answer is that the miracle is actually the knowledge ('ilm) and the informing (ikhbar) of that non-action in accordance with what appears, and that knowledge is an act of Allah created in the prophet. Some scholars accepted the objection and added to the definition of miracle the phrase 'or what takes its place' (aw ma yaqumu maqamahu), which is the position of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The author then notes that this question is directed at the condition that the miracle be an act (fi'l). He explains that a miracle can be a non-action, like the example of protection from harm. For this reason, al-Ash'ari said the miracle is an act or what takes the place of an act. Finally, Ibn Dihaq answered by referring back to the earlier answer: the miracle is attributed to the knowledge and informing of the event, which is an act of Allah.

Key Terms
معجزة
فعل
عدم الفعل
التحدي
العصمة
علم
إخبار
قدرة
كسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A miracle must always be a positive, visible action.
Correction: A miracle can be a non-action, such as a prophet's immunity from harm, and the miracle is the knowledge and foretelling of that non-action, which is an act of Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the answer to the objection that a prophet can challenge by non-action (e.g., immunity from harm)?
A) The miracle is the non-action itself, which is created by Allah.
B) The miracle is the knowledge and informing of the non-action in accordance with reality.
C) Such a challenge is not valid because a miracle must be an act.
D) The prophet's ability to refrain from action is the miracle.

Key Takeaway
A miracle, in Ash'ari theology, can be a non-action when the prophet challenges by divine protection, and the miracle is the knowledge and informing of that protection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever experienced a situation where you were protected from harm despite active threats? Did you recognize it as a miracle from Allah, or did you attribute it to coincidence? How can viewing divine protection as a miracle change your gratitude and reliance on Him?
?
The passage says the real miracle is Allah's knowledge and the prophet's informing of it. When you reflect on your own life, are you actively seeking to be aware of Allah's knowledge and protection over you, even when no dramatic event occurs? Or do you only look for spectacular signs?
?
If a miracle can be a non-action, then divine intervention often happens subtly. Are you living as if every breath and every shielding from harm is a sign from Allah? What would change in your daily awareness if you truly believed that God's miracles are happening in the ordinary moments of safety and peace?

Critique of Two Answers on the Nature of the Miraculous Act
Critique of answers by Imam al-Haramayn and Ibn Dihaq on the miraculous act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main answers to identifying the miraculous act where the challenge is to prevent an action, and the critiques against them, particularly regarding the nature of the non-existent act.

Core Idea
The miraculous act is not always a positive action; sometimes it is the prevention of an act, which challenges standard assumptions about power and causation.

In debates about miracles, a key question arises: when a prophet challenges people by preventing an action (e.g., 'no one shall stand'), what exactly is the miraculous act? Two prominent scholars, Imam al-Haramayn and Ibn Dihaq, offered different answers. This passage critically examines their positions and reveals the subtle theological issues at stake.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two answers
Read the passage to find the two proposed answers: Imam al-Haramayn's focus on continuous sitting and Ibn Dihaq's focus on the act of causing that sitting.
Both answers try to locate the miraculous event in a positive action despite the challenge being about prevention.
2
Understand the first objection
Note that the first objection applies to both answers: the challenge was actually about non-existence of a second act (e.g., annihilation of a mountain), not about the acts they mentioned.
3
Analyze the second objection and the final response
The second objection is specific to Imam al-Haramayn based on his own view that power cannot affect non-existence. Then see the evaluation of Ibn Dihaq's answer and the attempted counter-response.
Consider why the final response is considered weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب إمام الحرمين بأن القعود المستمر على خلاف الاعتياد في مثل : ( آيتي : ألا يقوم أحد ) .. هو المعجز ، وكذا يقول : إن الترك على خلاف المعتاد في المثالين الآخرين هو فعل وهو المعجز . كلا الجوابين غير مستقيم لوجهين : أحدهما : أن التحدي لم يقع بما ذكر في الجوابين ، وإنما وقع على الفرض بعدم الفعل الثاني – وهو خاص بالإمام – : أنه لو تحدى نبي ؛ بأن يعدم الله هذا الجبل العظيم .. لكان المتحدى به هنا عدما ، فإن أجاب بأن العدم الإضافي فعل تؤثر فيه القدرة كما يقول القاضي ومن تبعه ، وأن العدم ليس بقطع الأعراض .. لم يستقم له ذلك ؛ لأن رأيه أن العدم الطارئ لا يصلح أن تؤثر فيه القدرة ، فبطلت حيلته ، ولزمه اتباع تقييد الشيخ أما جواب ابن دهاق فهو مطرد في جميع الصور ، فهو حسن لو سلم مما أشرنا إليه في الرد الأول ، وقد يجاب عنه بأن التحدي في المعجزة إما مطابقة وهو واضح ، أو لزوما كالعلم والخبر في المثل المذكورة ، وفيه نظر
قوله : ( كالضرب والقتل ) مثال للفعل الذي وقع التحدي بعدمه
Imam al-Haramayn answered that the continuous sitting contrary to custom in the example: 'My sign: that no one should stand' is the miraculous act, and likewise he says that the leaving off contrary to custom in the other two examples is an act and it is the miraculous. Both answers are unsound for two reasons: One of them: that the challenge did not occur by what was mentioned in the two answers; rather it occurred on the assumption of non-existence of the second act – and this is specific to the Imam – that if a prophet challenged that God should annihilate this great mountain, then what was challenged with here is a non-existence. And if he answers that the accidental non-existence is an act in which power is effective, as al-Qadi and his followers say, and that non-existence is not by cutting off accidents, that is not tenable for him; because his opinion is that accidental non-existence is not suitable for power to be effective in it, so his stratagem is nullified, and he must follow the restriction of the shaykh. As for the answer of Ibn Dihaq, it is consistent in all cases; it is good if it were safe from what we pointed out in the first refutation. And it may be answered that the challenge in the miracle is either contingent (mutabaqa) and that is clear, or by necessity (luzuman) like knowledge and report in the mentioned example, but there is consideration in it. His saying: (like striking and killing) is an example of the act whose non-existence was challenged.
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage presents two answers to the problem of identifying the miraculous act in a challenge where a prophet says 'let no one stand' – is the miracle the continuous sitting or the act of causing that sitting? Imam al-Haramayn says it is the continuous sitting contrary to habit. Ibn Dihaq says it is the act of causing that sitting. Then the author critiques both answers with two objections. First objection: the challenge was not about those acts but about the non-occurrence of the second act (e.g., annihilation of a mountain). Second objection specific to Imam al-Haramayn: if the challenge is about non-existence, his view that power cannot affect non-existence undermines his answer. Ibn Dihaq's answer is more consistent but still subject to the first objection. Then a possible response is mentioned: the challenge in miracles can be either direct (through matching) or indirect (through necessity), but this response is doubtful.

Misconception: One might think that the miraculous act must always be a positive, observable action.
Correction: The passage shows that miracles can involve the prevention of an action (non-existence), and identifying the miraculous requires careful analysis of whether the act is positive or a non-existence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

?

Take a moment to identify one inconsistency—maybe between what you claim to believe and how you actually live—and commit to reconciling it with the truth of the Qur'an and Sunnah before the day ends.
?

Why a Miracle Must Be Extraordinary
Definition of miracle as extraordinary, excluding ordinary events, magic, and natural properties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a miracle must be extraordinary (khariq li al-'adah) and what ordinary events are excluded from being miraculous.

Core Idea
A miracle must be beyond ordinary — even rare magic and natural properties are ordinary.

Sanusi now clarifies the essential condition that a miracle be extraordinary. He distinguishes it from ordinary events, including magic and natural properties, to show why only the truly unprecedented can serve as divine attestation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: definition, exclusions, reasoning, and a mention of an objection.
The passage includes a short sub-section on a doubt from the Brahmins.
2
Identify the three categories of ordinary events
The passage excludes ordinary acts (mu'tad), magic (sihr), and natural properties (khawass) from being considered miraculous. Find each in the text.
Magic is ordinary because it has an ordinary cause, even if rare.
3
Understand the reasoning for the condition
The author gives two reasons: (1) without extraordinay, there is no proof of the miracle; (2) the miracle is like a verbal confirmation, so ordinary occurrence cannot indicate truth because it is not unique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحترز بقوله : ( خارق للعادة ) من المعتاد ؛ فإنه يستوي فيه الصادق والكاذب ، ومن المعتاد : السحر ونحوه ، وإن كان سببه العادي نادرا ، خلافا لمن جعل السحر خارقا لكن بسبب خاص به ، ومن المعتاد أيضا : ما يوجد في بعض الأجسام من الخواص ؛ كجذب الحديد بحجر المغناطيس إنما اشترط كون الفعل خارقا ؛ لعدم ثبوت الإعجاز بدونه ، وأيضا : فإن المعجزة تتنزل منزلة التصديق بالقول ، ومعتاد الوقوع لا يدل على ذلك ؛ لعدم اختصاصه ، ولا يشترط كون الخارق معينا من جهته اتفاقا
[ شبهة دليل البراهمة على استحالة الرسالة ]
قوله : ( ومن المعتاد : ما يوجد في بعض الأجسام من الخواص ) يشير إلى أن المعجزة لا بد وأن يعرى وقوعها عن جميع الحيل المعتادة
And he guarded against by his saying: 'extraordinary (khariq li al-'adah)' against the ordinary (mu'tad); for in it the truthful and the liar are equal. And from the ordinary is magic and its like, even if its ordinary cause is rare, contrary to the one who made magic extraordinary but due to a specific cause. And from the ordinary also is what is found in some bodies of properties, like the attraction of iron by a magnet. The act is only required to be extraordinary because the proof of the miracle is not established without it. And also: the miracle takes the station of attestation by speech, and ordinary occurrence does not indicate that, due to its non-specificity. And it is not stipulated that the extraordinary be specified from its side, by agreement. [Doubt of the argument of the Brahmins on the impossibility of prophethood] His saying: 'And from the ordinary is what is found in some bodies of properties' indicates that the miracle must that its occurrence be free from all ordinary tricks.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage explains the condition that the miracle must be extraordinary (khariq li al-'adah). The author distinguishes it from ordinary events (mu'tad), which include: (1) ordinary acts that anyone can do, (2) magic even if rare, because magic has an ordinary cause, (3) natural properties like magnetism. The author then gives the reason for this condition: without being extraordinary, there is no proof of the miracle; the miracle functions like a verbal confirmation, so ordinary occurrence does not testify to truthfulness because it is not unique. Additionally, it is agreed that the extraordinary act does not need to be specified in advance (e.g., the prophet can just say 'a sign will happen' without detailing it). Finally, a doubt from the Brahmins is referenced: the miracle must be free from all ordinary tricks; this is implied by the exclusion of natural properties.

Key Terms

معتاد
سحر
خواص
معجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Magic is miraculous because it is rare and unusual.
Correction: Magic has an ordinary cause, even if that cause is rare. Therefore, it falls under ordinary events and cannot be a miracle.

Checkpoint
Why must a miracle be extraordinary (khariq li al-'adah) rather than ordinary?
A) Because miracles are always done by prophets.
B) Because ordinary events do not distinguish the truthful from the liar.
C) Because magic is extraordinary.
D) Because magnets are miraculous.

Key Takeaway
A miracle must be extraordinary to serve as a unique sign of truthfulness, excluding all ordinary events including magic and natural properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson says ordinary events do not distinguish truth from falsehood. Are there areas in your life where you have been convinced of something's truth simply because it felt 'rare' or 'special'? How will you apply this criterion to protect yourself from deception?
?
The passage forbids attributing divine attestation to anything ordinary. Think of a common 'spiritual experience' people claim today—does it meet the condition of being extraordinary beyond all known causes? If not, what will you change about how you evaluate such claims going forward?
