Tahaddi and Ikhtisas: The Challenge and Exclusivity of Miracles
Defines tahaddi and explains the necessity of ikhtisas for miracles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of tahaddi, its essential elements, and why the condition of exclusivity (ikhtisas) is necessary for a miracle to serve as proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
A miracle's proof lies in its exclusivity to the prophet, not in a dramatic challenge.

Sanusi clarifies two intertwined concepts: tahaddi (the prophet's challenge) and ikhtisas (the miracle's exclusivity). He shows that the challenge need not be explicit, and the impossibility of imitation is not for the challenge itself but to prove the miracle belongs uniquely to the prophet. Let's unpack his precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi first defines tahaddi linguistically and then applies it to the prophetic context.
The definition starts with 'فيه الحاديانِ'.
2
Identify the definition of tahaddi
Sanusi explains that tahaddi is the prophet's statement designating a sign, and it does not require a specific phrase like 'no one can bring its like'. Focus on what suffices for a valid challenge.
3
Understand the role of ikhtisas
Notice that the impossibility of an opponent producing the miracle is necessary not for the challenge itself but to establish exclusivity. This is a key point. Trace why exclusivity is essential for a miracle to be evidence.
4
Distinguish tahaddi from contemporaneity
Sanusi concludes by explaining why tahaddi is an additional condition beyond the miracle being contemporaneous with the claim. A miracle could coincide with the claim but not be presented as a sign.
This shows the precision of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيه الحاديانِ، ويُقالُ: تحدَّيْتُ فلاناً؛ إذا ماريته ونازعتُهُ الغلبة، وهو هنا عبارة عن قولِ النبي: ( آيةً صدقي كذا )، وليسَ مِنْ شرط التحدّي أنْ يقول: لا يأتي أحدٌ بمثلها، بل يكفي أن يقول: ( آيتي: أنْ يفعلَ الله كذا ) فيفعلُهُ لهُ، فإجابة دعواه دليلٌ على صدقه في مقالتِهِ. نعم؛ تعذر صدورِها مِنْ مثلِهِ إِذا كانَ يبغي معارضته . . لا بدَّ منه، لا لأجل التحدّي، بل لأجل ثبوتِ الاختصاص؛ فإنَّ المعجزة لا بدَّ أن تكونَ مُختصَّةً بالنبيَّ، ولهذا شُرِطَ أنْ تكونَ خارقة للعادةِ واقعةً على وَفْقِ دعواه؛ فإنَّ المعتاد وما لا تسبقه الدعوى مِنَ الخوارق .. لا اختصاص لهُ بهِ، وإذا كانَ لا بدَّ مِنَ الاختصاص فالخارقُ الواقع قبل الدعوى تتساوى فيه الأقوال، وتتكافأ فيه الدعاوي، وكذا الواقع بعد دعوى الرسالة، ولكن لم يتحدَّ بهِ أصلاً. وإنما لم أستغن بما قدَّمْتُ مِن اشتراطِ كون المعجزة مقارنة لدعوى الرسالة عن هذا الشرط، وهو التحدي بها . . لأنها قد تقترن بدعوى الرسالةِ ولا يَتحدَّى بها؛ أي: لا يدَّعيها آيةَ صدقِهِ.
In it is tahaddi, and it is said: I challenged so-and-so if I disputed with him and vied with him for supremacy. It is here an expression of the Prophet's statement: 'The sign of my truthfulness is such-and-such.' It is not a condition of tahaddi that he say: 'No one can produce its like,' rather it suffices that he says: 'My sign is that Allah does such-and-such,' so He does it for him. So the answering of his claim is evidence of his truthfulness in his statement. Yes, the impossibility of its occurrence from someone like him when he intends to oppose him is necessary not because of the challenge but for the sake of establishing exclusivity. For the miracle must be specific to the prophet, and for this it is conditioned that it be a breach of custom occurring in accordance with his claim. For the customary and what is not preceded by the claim from among the supernatural events have no exclusivity to him. And since exclusivity is necessary, then the supernatural event that occurs before the claim makes statements equal and claims equivalent, as does that which occurs after the claim of prophethood but without being challenged at all. And I did not dispense with what I have presented of the condition that the miracle be contemporaneous with the claim of prophethood concerning this condition, namely the challenge, because it (the miracle) may be contemporaneous with the claim of prophethood without being challenged; that is, he does not claim it as a sign of his truthfulness.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of tahaddi (challenge) and explains the necessity of ikhtisas (exclusivity) in miracles. The author begins by linguistically defining tahaddi as disputing and striving for supremacy, then applies it to the prophet's statement designating a sign. He clarifies that tahaddi does not require the prophet to explicitly say 'no one can bring its like'; it is sufficient for him to state his miracle and have it occur. The response of Allah to his claim is evidence of truthfulness. Next, he emphasizes that the impossibility of the miracle being produced by an opponent is necessary not for the challenge itself, but to establish that the miracle is specific to the prophet. For a miracle to be valid, it must be a breach of custom (kharq al-'ada) that aligns with the prophet's claim. Ordinary events or supernatural events not linked to a claim cannot be exclusive. He explains that if a supernatural event occurs before the claim, it cannot serve as evidence because it lacks exclusivity, and similarly if it occurs after the claim but is not challenged. Finally, he notes that the condition of tahaddi is additional to the condition of contemporaneity, because a miracle might coincide with the claim but not be presented as a sign. Thus tahaddi ensures that the miracle is explicitly used as proof.

Key Terms
تحدّي
اختصاص
معجزة
خارق للعادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That tahaddi requires the prophet to explicitly state 'No one can produce its like.'
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is sufficient for the prophet to simply designate a sign, such as 'My sign is that Allah does such-and-such.' The challenge need not be phrased as an explicit prohibition of imitation.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the impossibility of an opponent producing the miracle necessary?
A) To make the challenge more difficult
B) To establish the miracle's exclusivity to the prophet
C) To ensure the miracle is a breach of custom
D) To prove the prophet's truthfulness

Key Takeaway
A valid miracle requires both tahaddi (the prophet's explicit challenge) and ikhtisas (exclusive connection), the latter ensured by the impossibility of imitation by an opponent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly expected a dramatic divine sign before fully committing to a truth, while ignoring the quiet yet undeniable proofs already before you? Will you today accept the sufficiency of Allah's words without demanding a spectacle?
?
Sanusi teaches that a miracle must be exclusive to the prophet. In your own life, are there claims about your faith or spirituality that you attribute to divine favor, but that could equally be coincidence or self-deception? How will you test your sincerity so that your 'signs' are exclusive to a humble heart?
?
The prophet did not need to announce that no one could match his miracle; he simply said, 'My sign is that Allah does this.' Are you willing to let your life be a simple and honest testimony to truth, without needing to prove yourself to others? What would you say if asked for your sign today?

The Condition of Mumathala in Miracles
Two cases of miracle claims and the condition of similarity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the requirement of similarity (mumathala) applies differently to specific vs. non-specific miracle claims in kalam.

Core Idea
Similarity is required for specific miracles but not for non-specific ones.

This short but precise passage from Sanusi's work addresses a key rule in the debate over miracles (mu'jizat). When a prophet claims a specific miracle, the opponent must produce an exactly similar one. But when the miracle is not specified, a different rule applies. Let's examine the two cases and the scholarly disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the two cases: specific and non-specific miracle claims.
2
Distinguish the two cases
Identify the condition for each: for a specific miracle, similarity is required; for non-specific, the scholars differ.
The passage uses 'mu'ayyana' (specific) and 'ghayr mu'ayyana' (non-specific).
3
Identify the two opinions
Al-Amidi reports most scholars require similarity, but al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) does not. The author endorses al-Qadi's view.
The author says 'huwa al-haqq' (it is the correct view).
4
Understand the reasoning
Reflect on why similarity is not needed for non-specific miracles: any extraordinary event can demonstrate the opponent's ability.
This reasoning is implied in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المعجزة: إنِ ادعِيَتْ مُعيَّنَةً فشرط المعارض مماثلته لها، وإن كانَتْ غير مُعيَّنة فقال سيف الدين الآمدي: ( أكثر أصحابنا اشترط المماثلة، والذي اختاره القاضي: أنَّ المماثلة غير مشترطة، وهو الحق ).
Then the miracle: if it is claimed as specific, then the condition of an opponent is its similarity to it, and if it is non-specific, then Sayf al-Din al-Amidi said: 'Most of our associates stipulated similarity, and what the Qadi chose is that similarity is not stipulated, and it is the correct view.'
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage addresses the condition of mumathala (similarity) when a miracle is challenged. The author distinguishes between two cases: if the claimed miracle is specific (mu'ayyana), such as 'splitting the moon', then the opponent must produce an exactly similar miracle. If the miracle is non-specific (ghayr mu'ayyana), e.g., 'Allah will do something extraordinary tomorrow' without specifying what, then the question arises whether the opponent can counter with any miracle or must produce the same type. Al-Amidi reports that most Ash'ari scholars require similarity in both cases. However, al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani chose the view that similarity is not required for non-specific miracles, and the author endorses this as correct (huwa al-haqq). The reasoning is that when the miracle is unspecified, any extraordinary event can demonstrate the opponent's ability, so the condition of exact similarity is unnecessary.

Key Terms
مماثلة
معارض
معجزة معينة
معجزة غير معينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Similarity (mumathala) is always required for any miracle challenge.
Correction: The passage shows that for non-specific miracles, similarity is not required according to the correct view.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the ruling on requiring similarity for a non-specific miracle claim?
A) It is required
B) It is not required
C) It is required only for the opponent
D) There is disagreement but the author does not choose

Key Takeaway
The condition of similarity depends on whether the miracle is specified; for non-specific miracles, similarity is not required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you present evidence for your faith, do you insist that others produce the exact same proof, or are you open to different forms of evidence? This lesson shows that for non-specific claims, similarity is not required. Are you willing to accept that Allah may prove truth through signs you did not expect?
?
If someone demands a specific miracle from you to believe, would you be able to offer them what is similar? And if not, what does that reveal about your reliance on Allah's wisdom rather than your own ability to produce signs?
?
The scholars debated even the conditions of miracles. Does your creed compel you to examine your own assumptions about what constitutes proof? Are you ready to follow the truth even when it challenges your initial understanding, as al-Qadi did when he chose the correct view?

Delaying the Miracle After Death
Examines permissibility of delaying miracle after death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing opinions on delaying a miracle after a prophet's death and the reasoning behind them.

Core Idea
Delaying a miracle after death is permissible for a messenger, but not for a prophet without followers.

In this lesson, we examine a specific issue in the theology of miracles: can a prophet's miracle be delayed until after his death? The author presents the views of major scholars and explains the rationale behind different positions, particularly the Mu'tazila's objection based on objective good and evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the issue and the key positions mentioned.
The passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the arguments presented.
2
Identify the two opinions
The passage mentions that al-Ash'ari had two opinions, and al-Baqillani adopted the second (permissibility). Identify the reasoning given for this view.
The author calls the second opinion 'al-zahir' (the apparent).
3
Distinguish between rasul and nabi
Notice that the issue is only for a messenger (rasul) who delivers a law, not for a prophet (nabi) who does not command obedience.
This distinction affects the permissibility.

The Mu'tazila forbade delaying the miracle based on their doctrine of objective good and evil (tahsin wa taqbih). Al-Qadi agreed but for a different reason.
The passage cuts off before explaining al-Qadi's reasoning fully.

Since the passage ends abruptly, note that the full reasoning is not given. This is a common issue in classical texts where the argument continues on the next page.

مسألة: في تأخير المعجزة عن الموت. وهل يجوز تأخيرُ المعجزة عن موته؟ قولان للأشعري، وقالَ بالثاني القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني، وهو الظاهرُ، فإِنَّ حفظَ ما نص عليهِ مِنْ أحكام شرعه في حياتِهِ لا باعث على تلقيه منه. هذه المسألةُ إِنَّما تُفرَضُ في حقِّ الرسول، ولو كان نبياً ولم يأمرِ الخلق بمتابعته . . لجاز ذلك. وأمَّا الرسولُ فإذا وصف شرعَهُ وبلَّغَهُ، وقال: ( آيتي: أنْ يظهر بعد موتي مِنَ الخوارق كذا وكذا ) فهل يجوز ذلك؟ صرحت المعتزلة بمنع ذلك، ووافقهم القاضي، إلا أنَّ مأخذَهُ غيرُ مأخذ المعتزلة؛ فالمعتزلة بنوا ذلك على القول بالتحسين والتقبيح، فقالوا: لو تأخَّرَتْ حُجَّتُهُ إلى بعدِ وفاتِهِ لَكانَ في حالِ حياتِهِ
Issue: On delaying the miracle after death. And is it permissible to delay the miracle after his death? There are two opinions from al-Ash'ari, and al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said the second, and it is the apparent, because there is no motive to receive from him in his lifetime the preservation of what he prescribed of rulings of his law. This issue is only posited with respect to the messenger, and if he were a prophet and did not command the creation to follow him, then that would be permissible. As for the messenger, if he described his law and communicated it, and said: 'My sign is that such-and-such supernatural events appear after my death,' is that permissible? Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage opens a new issue (mas'ala) concerning the permissibility of delaying a miracle until after the prophet's death. The author notes that al-Ash'ari had two opinions, and al-Baqillani adopted the second view (permissibility), which the author considers the apparent (al-zahir) opinion. His reasoning is that the purpose of the miracle is to preserve the rulings of the law; during the prophet's lifetime, people are not motivated to receive from him if the miracle is delayed. The issue specifically applies to a messenger (rasul) who has delivered a law, not to a prophet (nabi) who does not command obedience. If a messenger proclaims his law and says that a miracle will appear after his death, the question arises. The Mu'tazila forbade this based on their doctrine of objective good and evil (tahsin wa taqbih), arguing that it would be unjust to delay the proof. Al-Qadi (Baqillani) agreed with their conclusion but for a different reason (left unspecified as the text cuts off). The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing in the next page.

Delaying the Miracle after Death
Sanusi defines the issue, states two opinions, and clarifies the scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological issue of delaying a miracle after a prophet's death and the two opinions of al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani.

Core Idea
Can a prophet's miracle appear after his death? Two scholars gave different answers.

Sanusi now turns to a nuanced problem: can a miracle be delayed until after the prophet's death? This question has important implications for how we understand the role of miracles in confirming prophethood. The passage presents two opinions and clarifies the exact scenario in which the question arises.

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Delaying Miracles
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila argument assuming their own principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila's argument against delaying miracles, using two distinct strategies: rejecting their foundational principle and showing that even within their framework, delay can be beneficial.

Core Idea
Even if you grant their premise, envy can justify delaying miracles.

The Mu'tazila argued that delaying a prophet's miracle is impossible because it would violate God's wisdom. Sanusi refutes them in two ways: first by rejecting the entire moral framework of al-tahsin wa al-taqbih, and second by showing that within their own framework, delay can be beneficial due to human envy. This lesson examines both arguments in detail.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila position
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi describes the Mu'tazila argument: they say delaying the miracle would make honoring the prophet non-obligatory, which is unwise.
Notice they rely on the principle of al-tahsin wa al-taqbih.
2
Examine the first refutation
Sanusi's first rebuttal is to invalidate the very foundation of the Mu'tazila's reasoning. This has been established elsewhere.
He does not repeat the proof here, just references it.
3
Analyze the second refutation
Sanusi grants their corrupt principle for argument's sake and shows that even then, God might know that some people are so envious that they would reject the miracle during the prophet's lifetime but accept it after his death.
This demonstrates that delay can be wise within their own framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صرحت المعتزلة بمنع ذلك، ووافقهم القاضي، إلا أن مأخذه غير مأخذ المعتزلة؛ فالمعتزلة بنوا ذلك على القول بالتحسين والتقبيح، فقالوا: لو تأخرت حجته إلى بعد وفاته لكان في حال حياته لا يجب توقيره وتعظيمه، والوفاء بحرمته ورعاية حق النبوة والرسالة له، وذلك منع للخلق من الرتب السنية والمقامات العلية لا يحسن ممن وجب عليه أن يكون حكيماً لطيفاً راعياً لصلاح البرية. وهذا إبطال قولهم بوجهين: أحدهما: من جهة إبطال أصل التحسين والتقبيح، ومراعاة الصلاح والأصلح، وقد سبق تحقيق ذلك. الثاني: على تقدير تسليم هذا الأصل الفاسد لهم قد يقال: يمتنع أن يكون صلاح بعض الخلق في ذلك؛ إذ قد يعلم الله من طائفة حسد الأحياء ومنافستهم، واستحكام هذا الخلق في قلوبهم، ويزول عنهم هذا الخلق بموت محسودهم، ويتلقون حينئذ ما يكون منه بالقبول، وأكثر الكفرة والفجرة إنما أتوا من حسد وحب رئاسة وأنفة من التبعية، فلا يمتنع في المعلوم - على تسليم أصل التحسين والتقبيح - أن يكون صلاح قوم في تأخير المعجزة.
The Mu'tazila explicitly forbade that, and al-Qadi agreed with them, except that his reasoning is different from theirs. The Mu'tazila based that on the doctrine of al-tahsin wa al-taqbih (moral valuation), saying: If his proof were delayed until after his death, then during his lifetime it would not be obligatory to honor and venerate him, nor to fulfill his sanctity and observe the right of prophethood and messengership for him, and that would be a prevention of the creatures from high ranks and lofty stations, which is not becoming of one who must be wise, kind, and caring for the welfare of humanity. This is a refutation of their statement in two ways: First, by refuting the very foundation of al-tahsin wa al-taqbih and the consideration of benefit and what is most beneficial, and this has been previously established. Second, assuming we grant this corrupt principle to them, it can be said: It is not impossible that the benefit of some creatures lies in that; for God may know of a group's envy and rivalry of the living, and the strengthening of this trait in their hearts, and that this trait leaves them upon the death of the one they envy, and then they receive his message with acceptance. Most disbelievers and sinners were only afflicted due to envy, love of leadership, and disdain for subordination. Therefore, it is not impossible in the divine knowledge - assuming the principle of al-tahsin wa al-taqbih - that the benefit of some people lies in delaying the miracle.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila's position against delaying miracles. Sanusi first describes their argument: based on rational ethical judgment (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih), delaying the miracle implies that during the prophet's lifetime he is not owed honor, which would be unwise for a wise God. He then refutes in two ways. First, he invalidates the very foundation of the Mu'tazila's principle, noting that this has already been established elsewhere. Second, even accepting their corrupt principle, he argues that God may know that some people are so envious that they would only accept the miracle after the prophet's death, making the delay actually beneficial. This second argument uses their own framework to show that delay can be wise.

المعتزلة
التحسين والتقبيح
الحكمة
الصلاح
الحسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second rebuttal concedes the Mu'tazila's principle, so the refutation is weak.
Correction: Sanusi only grants it hypothetically to show that even on their own terms, the conclusion does not follow. He does not accept the principle as valid.

Checkpoint
What is the second way Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila's argument even after granting their principle?
A) The prophet does not need honor
B) God may have knowledge that some people benefit from the delay due to envy
C) The Mu'tazila are inconsistent
D) Miracles cannot be delayed

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila by rejecting their moral framework and, hypothetically, by pointing out that divine knowledge of human envy can make delaying a miracle wise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a truth—Islamic or otherwise—that you have rejected because you envied the person delivering it? How might your envy be robbing you of guidance that could benefit you after that person is gone?
?
Sanusi points out that delaying proof can be more beneficial for some. Do you demand immediate signs from God before obeying, or do you trust that His timing is perfect even when it tests your patience?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed that everyone would benefit from immediate miracles. Are you quick to assume that what you want is what is best for everyone, or do you humble yourself before God's omniscience, acknowledging that He knows what hearts need better than you?

Al-Baqillani's Objection and Its Rebuttal
Sanusi presents al-Baqillani's objection and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Baqillani's objection that a delayed miracle cannot support messengership after death, and the rebuttal that it confirms the past claim.

Core Idea
A miracle after death proves a past message, not a current one.

Sanusi presents a classic objection from the Qadi al-Baqillani: how can a miracle that appears after the messenger's death serve as proof for his messengership? If messengership requires ongoing divine address, and address after death is impossible, then the miracle seems futile. Sanusi then answers by distinguishing the role of the miracle as a confirmation of a past claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand al-Baqillani's reasoning: messengership depends on divine address, which cannot occur after death. So how can a miracle after death serve as evidence?
2
Analyze the rebuttal
See how Sanusi responds: the miracle shows that the messenger was indeed addressed in the past to convey that message; it does not require ongoing address.
The key distinction is between confirming a past claim and requiring current address.
3
Reflect on the application
Consider why this objection and rebuttal matter for the theology of miracles. How does this shape our understanding of prophetic signs?
This ties into broader discussions of how miracles authenticate prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القاضي رضي الله عنه: فقد يحتج بأن الرسالة مرجعها إلى تعلق الخطاب بالرسول، وذلك ممتنع بعد الموت، فكيف تكون الآية لا تتحقق إلا في وقت امتناع ما هي آية عليه؟! ورد: بأنه تبين بعد موته أنه كان مخاطباً بتبليغ ما بلغه، ولا يضر امتناع تعلق الخطاب عند وجود الآية؛ فإنها تدل على ما سبق من دعواه، وقد جوزنا تأخير الآية إلى زمن مضروب في حال الحياة.
As for al-Qadi (may God be pleased with him), he might argue that messengership is based on the attachment of divine address to the messenger, and that is impossible after death, so how can the sign (miracle) only occur at a time when that which it is a sign for is impossible? It is replied: It becomes clear after his death that he was addressed to convey what he conveyed, and the impossibility of the attachment of address at the time of the miracle does not harm; for it indicates his past claim, and we have permitted the delay of the miracle to a specified time during life.
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This passage presents al-Baqillani's objection and its rebuttal. Al-Baqillani argues that messengership essentially involves God's direct address to the messenger, which cannot occur after death. Therefore, a miracle appearing after death cannot serve as evidence for a current message. The rebuttal distinguishes: the miracle confirms that the messenger was indeed addressed in the past to convey that message; it does not require ongoing address. The delay is analogous to permitting a designated time during life. The key point is that the miracle verifies a past claim, not a present one.

Key Terms
القاضي
الخطاب
الآية
الدعوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A posthumous miracle cannot serve as evidence for a previous prophetic claim because the messenger is no longer alive to receive divine address.
Correction: The miracle functions as a confirmation of the past claim, not as a conditional sign requiring ongoing address. It shows that the messenger was indeed commissioned by God.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi rebut al-Baqillani's objection that a delayed miracle is impossible?
A) By saying the messenger is still alive after death
B) By saying the miracle confirms the past claim, not a current address
C) By denying that messengership requires address
D) By saying the miracle is not necessary

Key Takeaway
A delayed miracle can still validate a prophetic claim by confirming that the messenger was divinely commissioned in the past.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How can you actively remind yourself of this argument to strengthen your certainty?
?
Al-Baqillani's objection hinges on the impossibility of God speaking to a dead prophet. What 'impossibilities' in your current situation prevent you from accepting past divine proofs? Are you demanding a sign right now, when the sign has already been delivered and validated by the consensus of scholars and the preservation of the Quran? What would it take for you to submit without a fresh miracle?
?
Sanusi permits delaying a miracle to a set time. Have you ever delayed responding to a clear truth, telling yourself that you will 'see the proof later'? Consider an aspect of your faith you have postponed acting upon. How does this lesson challenge your excuse of waiting for further confirmation? Write down one step you will take today to act on a truth you already know.

The Objection on Delayed Miracles and the Nature of Karamat
Sanusi presents an objection about delayed miracles and refutes it by clarifying the nature of karamat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a delayed prophet's miracle would undermine karamat, and why this objection does not invalidate the possibility of delayed miracles.

Core Idea
Karamat are not certain proofs of sainthood; early scholars never treated them as such.

Sanusi presents an objection raised by al-Qadi: if a prophet's miracle can be delayed after death, then every karamah could be mistaken for such a delayed miracle. This would undermine the reliability of karamat. But Sanusi shows that this is not a problem because karamat were never considered certain proofs in the first place, and early scholars were always cautious about them.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify al-Qadi's argument: that allowing miracles to be delayed after a prophet's death would make every karamah potentially a delayed miracle, thus stripping karamat of their evidentiary value.
Notice the logical structure: if every karamah could be a delayed miracle, then karamat cannot prove sainthood.
2
Examine the refutation
See how the answer concedes that this possibility removes certainty (qat'iyya) from karamat, but argues that this is not a problem because karamat were never certain proofs anyway.
The refutation uses the phrase 'ghayatuhu butlan' indicating the utmost consequence is the loss of certainty.
3
Reflect on the early scholars' attitude
Understand that early scholars did not rely on karamat as foolproof signs; they feared istidraj (divine enticement) and remained cautious.
This reinforces that the objection does not damage the original position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيتّجه أن يتأخَّرَ إلى أجل مضروب بعد الوفاة ، ويتبيَّنُ بذلكَ صدقُهُ في الدعوى السابقة . وربما احتج القاضي : بأنَّ القولَ بذلك يؤدّي إلى إبطال الكرامةِ ؛ إذْ ما مِنْ كرامة إلا ويجوز على هذا أن تكون معجزةً لنبي تأخَرَتْ بعد وفاتِهِ . وأُجيبَ : بأنَّ غايتَه بطلان كون الكرامة دليلاً قطعياً على ولايةِ مَنْ ظهرَتْ على يديهِ ؛ لتطرُّقِ هذا الاحتمالِ فيها ، ولا ريبَ أَنَّا نقولُ بمُوجَبِهِ ؛ فإنَّ دلالةَ الكرامة على الولاية ليسَتْ قطعيَّةً ولو أَمِنَّا فِيهَا مِنْ هذا الاحتمالِ الذي ذُكِرَ ؛ لاحتمال كونها استدراجاً ، ويكونَ مَنْ ظهرَتْ على يديهِ مِنْ أهل عداوةِ اللهِ تعالى ، وممَّنْ سبقَ القضاء بأَنَّهُ لا يُختم له بالسعادة ، ولهذا كانَ الأوَّلونَ لا يثقون بها ، بل لا يزدادونَ معَها إلا خوفاً .
It therefore suggests that [the miracle] be delayed to a fixed term after death, and thereby his truthfulness in the previous claim becomes evident. Perhaps al-Qadi argued: that saying so leads to invalidating the karamah (miracle of a saint); since there is no karamah except that on this [view] it is possible that it be a miracle of a prophet that was delayed after his death. And it was answered: that its [the view's] utmost consequence is the nullification of the karamah being a certain proof of the sainthood of the one on whose hands it appeared, due to the entailing of this possibility in it, and there is no doubt that we affirm its consequence; for the indication of the karamah for sainthood is not certain even if we were secure in it from this possibility that was mentioned, because of the possibility that it be an istidraj (gradual enticement), and the one on whose hands it appears may be from the people of enmity to Allah Almighty, and from those whom the decree has preceded that his end will not be with happiness, and for this reason the early [scholars] did not rely on them, rather they only increased in fear with them.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the possibility of a prophet's miracle being delayed until after his death. The objection is raised by al-Qadi: if a miracle can be delayed beyond the prophet's lifetime, then every karamah (saintly miracle) could be mistaken for a delayed prophet's miracle, thus undermining the certainty of karamat. The answer acknowledges that this possibility does remove the certainty of karamat as proofs of sainthood, but it is not a problem because the early scholars never considered karamat to be certain proofs anyway—they always feared that such wonders could be istidraj (divine enticement) for an enemy of God. Thus, the objection does not damage the original position.

Key Terms
كرامة
معجزة
ولاية
استدراج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that karamat (miracles of saints) are certain proofs of sainthood, leaving no room for doubt.
Correction: The passage shows that karamat are not certain proofs; they may be istidraj (enticement) for an enemy of God. Even without the possibility of a delayed prophet's miracle, karamat are inherently uncertain.

Checkpoint

B) Because the early scholars never considered karamat to be certain proofs of sainthood.
C) Because karamat are always miracles of prophets.
D) Because karamat are not possible after the prophet's death.

Key Takeaway
Karamat are not certain proofs of sainthood; early scholars recognized their uncertainty and feared the possibility of istidraj.

Have you ever relied on a visible sign—a dream, a vision, or a 'coincidence'—as proof that you are on the right path? What if that sign was an istidraj, leading you away from humility and into self-satisfaction? Examine your heart now and ask Allah for protection.
?
The early scholars increased in fear when they saw wonders. Where is your fear? Is your relationship with Allah based on seeking spiritual highs or on daily, hidden obedience? Pray that He makes you from those who fear Him even when good things happen.

The Mukadhdhib: Two Opinions on Counter-Signs
Sanusi explains mukadhdhib and presents two views on counter-signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what constitutes a contradictory sign (mukadhdhib) and the two scholarly opinions on whether a revived person's denial invalidates a miracle.

Core Idea
Only the specific challenged act matters; later contradictions do not affect the miracle.

Sanusi now addresses a subtle question: if a prophet presents a miracle, and that miracle itself is later contradicted (e.g., a hand that was to testify speaks against him, or a revived person denies him), does that invalidate the proof? Scholars differ, and Sanusi lays out two key views. This lesson explores the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Note that the passage is dense with technical terms like mukadhdhib and qadih.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage discusses two scenarios: a hand that speaks to contradict, and a dead person revived who then denies the prophet. Notice how each case is treated differently.
The hand speaking is itself the claimed sign; the revival is the sign, not the speech.
3
Compare the two opinions
Al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn disagree on whether the dead person's denial is a defect. Compare their conditions and reasoning.
Al-Qadi requires the dead to die soon; Imam al-Haramayn says it's never a defect.
4
Understand the key distinction
The core point is that the sign must be the thing challenged. The revival itself is the sign, not the subsequent speech. Therefore, contradiction after the sign does not harm it.
This distinction is crucial for evaluating miracles.
5
Reflect on the takeaway
Consider how this principle protects the integrity of miracles. A prophet's sign stands on its own; later events do not retroactively cancel it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبقولِهِ : ( غيرُ مُكذِّب ( ممَّا إذا قالَ : آيةً صدقي : أنْ يُنطِقَ الله تعالى يدي ، فنطقَتْ بتكذيبه ، وفي تكذيب الميت المتحدَّى بإحيائِهِ قولان للقاضي وإمام الحرمين ، واختار أيضاً بعضُ المتأخّرينَ عدم القدح في تكذيب اليدِ وشبهها؛ لعدم التحدّي بتصديقها . مذهب القاضي في تكذيبِ الميتِ الذي تُحدِّيَ بإحيائِهِ : أَنَّهُ قادح ، لكن بشرط ألا تطولَ مُدَّتُهُ في عودِهِ إلى الحياة ، بل يموتُ عَقيبَ تكذيبه . ومذهب الإمام : : أنَّ ذلكَ غير قادح مطلقاً ، وحُجَّتُهُ : أَنَّ التحدّي وقع بالإحياء ، وقد حصل ، وهذا حي كفر ، والفرقُ عندَهُ بينَ تكذيب الميت وتكذيب اليدِ والجمادِ ونحوهما : أنَّ نفس النطقِ في اليدِ والجمادِ مُكذِّبٌ ، وهو نفسُ الآية ، والنطقُ في إحياء الميت هو المكذِّبُ ، وليس هو المدعى آيةً ، فافترقا مِنْ جهةِ أَنَّ المكذِّبَ المدعى آية الصدق في إحدى الصورتين ، وليس المكذِّبُ في الأخرى هو المدعى آية . ورأى بعضُ المتأخرينَ - وهو ابنُ دهاق في شرح الإرشادِ (۳) .
And by his saying: 'not a mukadhdhib (contradictory sign)' - from the case when he says: 'a sign of my truthfulness: that Allah Almighty makes my hand speak,' and it spoke contradicting him. And regarding the contradiction of the dead man who was challenged to be revived, there are two opinions from al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn. Some later scholars also chose that there is no defect in the contradiction of the hand and its like, due to the lack of a challenge to its verification. The madhhab of al-Qadi regarding the contradiction of the dead who was challenged to be revived: that it is a defect, but on condition that his duration in returning to life is not long, rather he dies right after his contradiction. And the madhhab of Imam [al-Haramayn]: that it is not a defect at all, and his proof: that the challenge was about reviving, and it occurred, and this one is alive having disbelieved. And the difference with him between the contradiction of the dead and the contradiction of the hand and inanimate objects and their like: that the utterance itself in the hand and inanimate object is the contradictory, and it is itself the sign, while the utterance in reviving the dead is the contradictory, but it is not the claimed sign. So they differ in that the contradictory claimed as a sign of truthfulness in one of the two cases, while the contradictory in the other is not the claimed sign. And some later scholars saw - and he is Ibn Dihaq in Sharh al-Irshad (3) - ...
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage discusses what constitutes a 'mukadhdhib'—something that contradicts the claim of prophethood. It presents a case: a prophet claims that his hand speaking will be a sign of his truth, but the hand speaks to deny him. Is that a valid counter-sign? Also, if a prophet challenges by reviving a dead person, and the revived person then denies the prophet, does that invalidate the miracle? Two opinions are given: Al-Qadi says it is a defect (qadih) if the revived person dies soon after; Imam al-Haramayn says it is never a defect. The reasoning is that in the case of the hand, the speaking itself is the claimed sign and it contradicts, so it is a valid counter. But in reviving the dead, the miracle is the reviving itself, not the subsequent speech of the revived; the speech is separate. Thus, the contradiction does not affect the miracle. The passage ends with mention of a later scholar, Ibn Dihaq, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
مكذِّب
تحدي
آية
قادح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any later event that contradicts the prophet's claim invalidates the miracle.
Correction: Only the specific act that was challenged counts as the sign. Events after the miracle do not retroactively cancel it, as shown in the revival case where the speech is separate from the miracle.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn, why does the dead person's denial after revival not invalidate the miracle?
A) Because the dead person is not reliable.
B) Because the challenge was about revival, not about the dead person's speech.
C) Because the revival happened before the denial.
D) Because the denial itself is also a miracle.

Key Takeaway
A miracle is only contradicted if the very act that was challenged itself turns out false; subsequent events do not invalidate it.

?
In your own life, do you focus on the true sign of your deeds (the act itself) or are you easily shaken by people's reactions? How can you distinguish between the two when your sincerity is challenged?

The Condition of Non-Belying in Miracles
Passage discusses non-belying condition with cases and scholarly opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition that a miracle must not be belied and the scholarly differences regarding cases where the miracle itself or its aftermath appears to contradict the claimant.

Core Idea
A miracle must not be belied, but scholars differ on what counts as belying.

This lesson delves into a nuanced condition for miracles: they must not be 'mukadhdhib' (belied). The author provides examples such as a hand that speaks to confirm truthfulness but instead speaks lies. Then he explores two different scenarios—the revived dead person and the speaking hand—and presents the opinions of al-Qadi, Imam al-Haramayn, and later scholars. This will clarify how the condition applies differently based on whether the miracle itself is the source of the contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two main cases
The passage discusses two scenarios: a speaking hand that belies the claimant, and a revived dead person who belies. Note how the author links them to the condition of non-belying.
3
Analyze al-Qadi's position
Al-Qadi holds that the belying of a revived dead person is a defect only if the person dies soon after. Understand why he imposes this temporal condition.
4
Understand Imam al-Haramayn's distinction
Imam al-Haramayn argues that the miracle itself (the revival) does not contain the belying; the speech of the revived is separate. This is a key distinction.
Contrast this with the hand case where the speech is the miracle.
5
Consider Ibn Dahaq's extension
Ibn Dahaq extends the reasoning to the hand case, arguing that if the challenge was only for the occurrence of speech, not its content, then even direct belying may not defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبقولِهِ: غيرُ مُكذِّبٍ ممَّا إذا قالَ: آيةً صدقي: أنْ يُنطِقَ الله تعالى يدي، فنطقَتْ بتكذيبه، وفي تكذيب الميت المتحدَّى بإحيائِهِ قولان للقاضي وإمام الحرمين، واختار أيضاً بعضُ المتأخّرينَ عدم القدح في تكذيب اليدِ وشبهها؛ لعدم التحدّي بتصديقها. مذهب القاضي في تكذيبِ الميتِ الذي تُحدِّيَ بإحيائِهِ: أَنَّهُ قادح، لكن بشرط ألا تطولَ مُدَّتُهُ في عودِهِ إلى الحياة، بل يموتُ عَقيبَ تكذيبه. ومذهب الإمام: أنَّ ذلكَ غير قادح مطلقاً، وحُجَّتُهُ: أَنَّ التحدّي وقع بالإحياء، وقد حصل، وهذا حي كفر، والفرقُ عندَهُ بينَ تكذيب الميت وتكذيب اليدِ والجمادِ ونحوهما: أنَّ نفس النطقِ في اليدِ والجمادِ مُكذِّبٌ، وهو نفسُ الآية، والنطقُ في إحياء الميت هو المكذِّبُ، وليس هو المدعى آيةً، فافترقا مِنْ جهةِ أَنَّ المكذِّبَ المدعى آية الصدق في إحدى الصورتين، وليس المكذِّبُ في الأخرى هو المدعى آية. ورأى بعضُ المتأخرينَ - وهو ابنُ دهاق في شرح الإرشادِ - أن تكذيب اليد ونحوها لا يقدح أيضاً؛ لما أشرنا إليه في الأصل مِنْ أَنَّ التحدّي إنَّما وقع فيهِ أيضاً بمجرَّدِ النطقِ، وقد وقع، والتصديق لم يقع التحدي به حتى يضر تخلفه.
And by his saying: 'not a belier' is from when he says: 'a sign of my truthfulness' that God the Exalted makes my hand speak, and it speaks belying him. And concerning the belied of the dead person challenged by reviving him, there are two opinions from al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn, and some later scholars also chose that there is no defect in the belied of the hand and the like, because the challenge was not for its verification. The doctrine of al-Qadi concerning the belied of the dead person who was challenged by reviving him: that it is a defect, but on condition that his period in returning to life does not become long, but rather he dies immediately after his belied. And the doctrine of the Imam: that that is absolutely not a defect, and his proof is: that the challenge occurred for revival, and it has occurred, and this is a living disbeliever. And the distinction according to him between the belied of the dead person and the belied of the hand, inanimate object, and the like: that the speech itself in the hand and inanimate object is the belier, and it is the sign itself, while the speech in reviving the dead person is the belier, and it is not the claimed sign. So they differ in that the belier claimed as a sign of truthfulness in one of the two forms, and in the other the belier is not the claimed sign. And some later scholars—and he is Ibn Dahaq in his commentary on al-Irshad—held that the belied of the hand and the like does not defect either, because of what we indicated previously that the challenge occurred therein also merely for the speech, and it has occurred, and the verification was not challenged so that its failure would be harmful.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition that a miracle (mu'jiza) must not be 'mukadhdhib' (belied), i.e., it must not contradict the claimant's truthfulness. The author gives an example: if a claimant says 'my hand is a sign of my truthfulness' and God makes the hand speak, but it says he is lying, then the miracle would be belied and thus invalid. The passage then examines two cases: (1) the miracle of reviving a dead person, where the revived person then belies the claimant, and (2) the miracle of speech from a hand, where the hand speaks but the words are belying. There are two main opinions from al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). Al-Qadi holds that the belied of a revived dead person is a defect, provided the person dies soon after; if he lives long, it is not a defect. Imam al-Haramayn holds it is never a defect, because the challenge was for revival, which occurred, and the revived person being a disbeliever is irrelevant. The distinction is that in the hand's speech, the speech itself is the miracle and it is directly belying, so the miracle contradicts truthfulness. In the revival, the miracle is the act of reviving, and the speech of the revived person is separate; the miracle itself does not contain the belied. Then a later scholar, Ibn Dahaq, states that the belied of the hand is also not a defect, because the challenge was only for the occurrence of speech, not for its content to attest positively. So the condition of non-belying is not violated if the miracle does not include an explicit attestation of truth. The passage thus clarifies the nuance in applying the condition.

Key Terms
تكذيب
آية
قادح
التحدّي
معجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any miracle that results in someone calling the claimant a liar is automatically invalid.
Correction: Scholars differentiate: if the belying is from the miracle itself (e.g., hand speaking lies), it is invalid. But if the belying is from an aftermath separate from the miracle (e.g., revived person speaking), some scholars like Imam al-Haramayn and Ibn Dahaq argue it does not invalidate the miracle.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn, why is the belied of a revived dead person not a defect?
A) Because the revived person is a disbeliever.
B) Because the miracle (revival) occurred, and the belying is not the miracle itself.
C) Because the revived person dies immediately.
D) Because the challenge was not for revival.

Key Takeaway
The condition of non-belying in miracles requires careful analysis of whether the belying is inherent in the miracle itself or arises from a separate event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have asked Allah for a sign in your life, have you ever been disappointed because the sign came in a way you did not expect or that seemed to contradict your desires? Are you willing to accept that Allah's signs may test your faith rather than confirm your assumptions?
?
In what areas of your life do you demand clear confirmations from Allah, and how do you respond when the evidence around you does not align with your personal narrative? Can you still hold onto faith even when the 'signs' seem to belie your hopes?
?
This lesson teaches that a miracle can be genuine even if the one through whom it occurs is disbelieved. Are there people or circumstances in your life where you have rejected a clear truth because of the imperfections of the vessels through which it came? Will you commit to seeking the truth regardless of the vessel?

How Miracles Indicate Truthfulness
The muqtarah explains that the verification depends on the mode of indication, which is necessary not rational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the verification of whether a belied miracle is a defect depends on the mode of indication of the miracle, which produces a direct necessary conviction of truthfulness, not a rational proof.

Core Idea
Miracles produce a direct, necessary conviction, not a logical demonstration.

The proposer (al-muqtarah) now delves into the heart of the matter: how exactly does a miracle indicate the truthfulness of a claimant? He argues that the miracle's indication is not like rational proofs; it is a direct, necessary connection. This lesson unpacks that idea and its implication for the issue of belied miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and its translation. Notice how the muqtarah frames the verification as dependent on the mode of indication of the miracle.
Pay attention to the words 'ضرورةً' (necessarily) and 'علم ضروري' (necessary knowledge).
2
Understand the distinction
The miracle's indication is 'ضرورةً' (necessarily), not through rational deduction. This means it produces an immediate, unavoidable conviction of truthfulness.
Contrast this with rational proofs (أدلة العقول) which work through logical steps.
3
Apply the introspection test
The muqtarah asks you to introspect: when you witness a true miracle, do you necessarily feel that God is affirming the claimant? If the miracle is belied (mukadhdhiba), that necessary feeling is absent, so the miracle fails to produce knowledge.
This test shows why a belied miracle cannot fulfill its purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالَ المُقترحُ: ( والتحقيقُ في هذه المسألة مبني على البحث في وجه دلالة المعجزة، وأنَّها لا تدلُّ دلالة أدلَّةِ العقول، وإنَّما هي مرتبطة عند اجتماع شرائطها بالصدق ضرورةً. فإذا تمهَّدَ ذلكَ قلنا في المسألةِ: ليراجع العاقل نفسَهُ أَنَّ ما يجدُهُ مِنْ نزول هذا الفعلِ مِنَ اللهِ منزلة قولِهِ لمدَّعي النبوَّةِ: « صدقْتَ » هل يجده ضرورةً عندَ كونِ الآية الخارقة مُكذِّبةً أم لا؟ فإذا لم يجده علم أنَّ المعجزة المستعقِبةَ العلم الضروري لم تحصل، وهذا مأخذُ الكلام ).
The proposer said: "And the verification in this issue is based on investigating the mode of indication of the miracle, and that it does not indicate according to the indication of rational proofs, but rather it is necessarily connected to truthfulness when its conditions are met. So if that is established, we say in the issue: Let the rational being refer to himself: that what he finds from this act from God in the position of His saying to the claimant of prophethood: 'You have spoken truthfully' — does he find it necessarily when the extraordinary sign is belied or not? If he does not find it, he knows that the miracle that produces necessary knowledge has not occurred, and this is the basis of the discussion."
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage presents the 'tahqiq' (verification) proposed by the muqtarah (the proposer, likely a later scholar summarizing the issue). It states that the resolution of whether a belied miracle is a defect depends on the mode of indication (wajh al-dalala) of the miracle. Miracles do not indicate truthfulness in the way rational proofs do—they are not demonstrative arguments. Instead, when all conditions for a miracle are met, they necessarily (daruratan) connect to the truthfulness of the claimant. The author then instructs the reader to introspect: When you witness an extraordinary act that you know comes from God, does it naturally (necessarily) occur to you that God is saying 'You have spoken truthfully'? If the miracle is belied (mukadhdhiba)—that is, if it seems to contradict the claimant—then you do not have that necessary impression. Hence, you know that the miracle that would produce necessary knowledge (al-'ilm al-daruri) has not actually occurred. So the condition of non-belying is essential for the miracle to perform its function. This explanation grounds the earlier opinions in a psychological or epistemic fact: the miracle generates a direct, necessary conviction of truth, and if belied, that conviction fails.

Key Terms
التحقيق
وجه دلالة المعجزة
علم ضروري
الآية الخارقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are like logical proofs that demonstrate truthfulness through rational deduction.
Correction: Miracles produce a direct, necessary conviction (daruri) that the claimant is truthful, not through logical demonstration.

Checkpoint
According to the muqtarah, what does a person find when the miracle is not belied?
A) A rational proof that the claimant is truthful.
B) A necessary impression that God is saying 'You have spoken truthfully'.
C) A conventional sign that the claimant is truthful.
D) Nothing, because miracles are not connected to truthfulness.

Key Takeaway
The verification of whether a belied miracle is a defect depends on whether the miracle produces necessary knowledge of truthfulness, which it cannot if it is belied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you read the muqtarah's invitation to 'let the rational being refer to himself,' do you honestly find that your belief in prophethood is grounded in that direct necessary conviction from the Quran's miracles, or have you been relying on rational arguments you've memorized? What would it take for you to feel that inner certainty as if God Himself said 'You have spoken truthfully'?
?
Have you ever encountered a claim of a miracle that seemed to contradict Islamic teachings? In that moment, did you perform the self-check the muqtarah describes — looking for that necessary knowledge of truthfulness — or did you dismiss it for other reasons? What does that reveal about the state of your heart?
?
If you were to face a situation where a miracle appears to be 'belied' — for instance, a contradiction in a prophetic claim — would you be willing to admit that the miracle that produces necessary knowledge has not occurred? The Mode of Indication of the Miracle: Rational, Conventional, or Customary
Sanusi enumerates the three possible modes of indication and argues against the first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a miracle's indication of truth cannot be rational or conventional, and why it must be customary.

Core Idea
Miracles indicate truth by God's custom, not by logic or convention.

Sanusi asks a key question: how does a miracle prove a prophet's truthfulness? Is it rationally necessary, based on a divine agreement, or simply God's normal way? He quickly eliminates the first two possibilities, showing that if a miracle were a rational proof, denying it would contradict reason, and if it were based on a divine convention, it would imply God lies. This leaves only the customary indication, which is flexible and fits the nature of miracles as extraordinary events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the concise style and the logical structure.
2
Identify the three proposed modes
The author lists three possible ways a miracle indicates truth: rational, conventional, and customary. Find these terms in the Arabic.
They are: عقليَّة, وضعية, عادية.
3
Understand the impossibilities
The author argues that the first two are impossible. For rational, it would contradict rational proof. For conventional, it would imply God lies. Reflect on why these are impossible for God.
This leaves customary as the only viable option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل دَلالة المعجزة على صدْقِ الرسولِ دَلالة عقليَّةٌ أو وضعية، أو عادية بحسب القرائن؟ أقوال. أمَّا على الأوَّلين فيستحيلُ صدورها على يد الكاذب؛ لما يلزمُ على الأَوَّلِ مِنْ نقضِ الدليل العقلي، وعلى الثاني مِنَ الخُلْفِ في خبرِهِ جلَّ وعلا؛ إذْ تصديق الكاذب كذب.
And is the indication of the miracle of the truthfulness of the messenger a rational indication, or conventional, or customary according to circumstances? [There are] opinions. As for the first two, it is impossible for it to occur from a liar; because on the first, there would follow a contradiction of rational proof, and on the second, a breach in His, exalted, word; since confirming a liar is a lie.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage poses a question about the nature of the miracle's indication (dalala) of the prophet's truthfulness. It offers three possibilities: rational (aqliyya), conventional (wad'iyya), or customary (adiyya) depending on circumstances. The author says there are opinions. Then he focuses on the first two: if the indication were rational, then denying a miracle would be like denying a logical proof, which is impossible. If it were conventional (i.e., based on a divine convention or stipulation), then a miracle coming from a liar would imply that God has violated His own word, since God would be attesting to a liar's truth, which is a lie (kadhib) and impossible for God. Therefore, it is impossible for a miracle to occur from a liar in either case. This leaves the third possibility: the indication is customary (adiyya), i.e., based on God's usual way of acting, which can be broken. The passage thus establishes that miracles are not rational proofs nor conventional signs but depend on God's custom, which is why they can be interrupted by disbelievers etc. The full argument implies that the correct view is that miracles are 'cadiyya' (customary), as the author likely holds, though not explicitly stated here.

Key Terms
دلالة عقلية
دلالة وضعية
دلالة عادية
خلف في خبره
تصديق الكاذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles could be rational proofs for truthfulness.
Correction: If miracles were rational proofs, a miracle from a liar would contradict reason, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why is a miracle impossible from a liar if its indication is conventional (wad'iyya)?
A) Because a liar cannot perform a conventional act.
B) Because it would imply God is lying by confirming a liar.
C) Because conventional indications are weaker than rational ones.
D) Because customary miracles only occur from truthful people.

Key Takeaway
The indication of a miracle is customary (ʿādiyya), not rational or conventional, because otherwise a miracle from a liar would be impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the miracle's indication is merely customary and not logically necessary, does that weaken your certainty in the truth of the prophets? What would it take for you to accept a miracle as a sign from God even if it could theoretically be otherwise?
?
The passage rejects the idea that God would confirm a liar. Consider a time when you were tempted to present something false as true. How does knowing that God's truthfulness is absolute—that He cannot authenticate a lie—affect your own standard of truthfulness? In what area of your life are you being dishonest, rationalizing that it's 'okay' because it seems necessary?
?
The author argues that miracles are not conventional signs (like a divine promise). When you read about miracles in the Qur'an or Sunnah, do you treat them as if God is bound by a promise? What does it mean for you to trust in God's habits (sunnat Allah) rather than a legalistic guarantee? How can you cultivate a relationship with God based on His customary ways rather than demanding logical certainty?

The Rational Indication of Miracles: First Opinion
Sanusi presents the first opinion that miracles indicate rationally and its justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first opinion holds that miracles indicate Allah's will to verify a prophet rationally, based on the principle of specification of contingent attributes.

Core Idea
A miracle's verification is a contingent attribute that requires a specifying will.

This lesson explores the first of three opinions on how miracles indicate prophethood. Imam Sanusi presents the view that the indication is rational, not conventional or initial. He grounds this in a key principle of kalam: any contingent attribute's occurrence over its opposite points to a will. The miracle, as a specific event, shows Allah's will to confirm the prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow from the miracle's specific occurrence to the will of Allah.
2
Identify the core argument
Look for the analogy between a miracle's occurrence in a specific time/place and the will behind it. Then the principle that a contingent attribute cannot be specified without a will.
3
Understand the principle of specification
The passage states that nature and cause cannot specify one possibility over another. This is the key rational foundation.
4
Consider the implication
The verification is an attribute of the miracle itself. If a condition is missing, the miracle would not be a verification. So the verification is a contingent attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أنَّ دلالتها عقلية : وإليه ميل الأستاذ قالوا : لأنَّ خَلْقَ اللهِ تعالى لهذا الخارقِ على وَفْقِ دعواه وتحدّيهِ معَ العجز عن معارضتِهِ ، وتخصيصه بذلكَ . . يدلُّ على إرادةِ اللهِ تعالى لتصديقِهِ ، كما يدلُّ اختصاص الفعل بالوقتِ المعيَّن والمحلّ المعيَّن على إرادتِهِ تعالى لذلك بالضرورة . وبالجملة : فقد جعلوا التصديق في هذا القول صفة للخارقِ الواقع على الوجه المخصوص ، مع جواز أنْ يعرى ذلك الخارقُ عن صفة التصديق بانعدام شرطٍ مِنْ شروط المعجزة ، فصارَتْ صفةً التصديق للخارقِ الحادثِ كسائر صفاتِ الأفعال الحادثة ، وقد علمت أنَّ اتّصاف الحادث بصفةٍ بدلاً عن نقيضها الجائز يدلُّ عقلاً على إرادة الفاعل ؛ وهو البارى تعالى لذلكَ ؛ لما تقرَّرَ أنَّ الطبيعة والعلَّةَ لا تُخصصانِ جائزاً بالوقوع بدلاً عن جائز يساويهِ
First: that their indication is rational; and towards this inclines the Master. They said: Because Allah's creation of this miraculous matter in accordance with his claim and challenge, with inability to oppose it, and its specification with that... indicates Allah's will to verify him, just as the specification of an action to a particular time and place indicates His will for that necessarily. In summary: they made verification in this opinion an attribute of the miraculous event occurring in the specific manner, while allowing that this miraculous event may be stripped of the attribute of verification by the absence of a condition from the conditions of a miracle, so verification became an attribute of the contingent miraculous event like other attributes of contingent actions. And you have known that the contingent's being qualified by an attribute instead of its equally possible opposite indicates rationally the will of the agent; and He is the Creator, the Most High, to that; because it is established that nature and cause do not specify a possibility to occur instead of an equal possibility.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage presents the first opinion on the indication of miracles: that it is rational (عقلية). The author notes that the Master (al-Ustadh) inclines to this view. The proof is as follows: Allah creates a miraculous event in response to a claimant's challenge, and the claimant is unable to produce its like. This specification – that the miracle occurs exactly when and where needed – necessarily indicates Allah's will to verify the claimant's truthfulness, just as the temporal and spatial specification of an action indicates the agent's will. The opinion treats verification (تصديق) as an attribute of the miracle itself, a contingent quality that can be absent if a condition of miracle is missing. The rational principle is that a contingent being's possession of one attribute instead of its equally possible opposite requires a specifying will. Neither nature nor cause can differentiate between equally possible options. Consequently, the miracle being a verification rather than something else points to Allah's will to confirm the prophet.

Key Terms
دلالة
عقلية
معجزة
تصديق
إرادة
خارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that rational indication means the miracle itself is a logical proof like a syllogism, but it is based on the specifying will, not logical entailment.
Correction: The rational indication is not a direct logical entailment but follows from the principle that a contingent attribute's occurrence over its opposite requires a will. The miracle's verification attribute points to a willing agent.

Checkpoint
What is the key principle that supports the rational indication of miracles according to the first opinion?
A) The miracle is a necessary logical consequence of prophethood.
B) The inability to oppose the miracle proves the claimant's truth directly.
C) The specification of a contingent attribute by will instead of its opposite.
D) The miracle is a conventional sign like words.

Key Takeaway
Miracles indicate prophethood rationally because their specific occurrence as verification over its opposite requires Allah's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you building your faith on the solid ground of rational inference from Allah's will, or are you vulnerable to doubt when signs seem ambiguous? Objection and Answers to the Rational Indication of Miracles
Sanusi presents an objection and two answers to the rational indication of miracles, plus a rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objection to the rational indication of miracles, the two answers offered, and the subsequent rebuttal concerning the nature of miraculous proof.

Core Idea
Miracles indicate prophethood not by mere existence but by meeting a challenge.

Sanusi now addresses a sophisticated objection to the rational argument from miracles. The objection challenges whether divine will can attach to verification (tasdiq) if it is eternal. Two nuanced answers follow, reinterpreting 'verification' in a contingent sense. Finally, a rebuttal from Imam al-Haramayn and a counter-rebuttal sharpen the distinction between miracles and rational proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify what the objection claims about verification (tasdiq) and why it is problematic for the rational indication. Note the key terms: khabar (report), eternal, contingent, will (irada).
The objection hinges on the nature of God's speech.
2
Examine the first answer
Understand how the first answer reinterprets 'verification' to refer to the contingent act of creating the miracle, not God's eternal report.
This answer shifts the object of will from God's speech to the created miracle.
3
Examine the second answer
See how the second answer proposes an omitted genitive: the miracle indicates God's will that the verification be true, i.e., the truth of the messengers.
This answer preserves the eternal nature of God's report while addressing the will.
4
Analyze the rebuttal by Imam al-Haramayn
Understand his argument that a miracle can exist without indicating prophethood, unlike a rational proof which necessarily indicates its conclusion.
This distinguishes miracles from rational proofs.
5
Consider the proposer's counter-rebuttal
Notice how he calls Imam al-Haramayn's argument a sophism: the indication comes from the miracle's response to a challenge, not its mere existence.
This clarifies the true basis of miraculous indication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتُرِضَ على هذا القولِ : بأنَّ التصديق عندنا خبر عن الصدق ، وخبر الله تعالى أزلي لا يصحُ أنْ يكونَ حادثاً ولا صفةً ، فلا يصح أن تتعلَّقَ بهِ الإرادةُ ؛ لأنَّها كالقدرة لا تتعلَّقُ إلا بالحادث بالممكن . وقد يُجابُ : بأنَّ التصديق الذي تعلَّقَتْ بهِ الإرادة هو التصديق بهذا الخارقِ ؛ أي : خلقُهُ لهُ دالاً على خبره تعالى بصدق رسله ، فيكون خبره تعالى الدال على صدق رسله مدلولاً لهذا التصديق الحادث الذي هو مُتعلَّق لإرادتِهِ جَلَّ وعزَّ . أو يُجابُ : بأنَّ الكلام فيهِ حذف مضاف؛ أي: الخارقُ بالشروط المذكورة يدلُّ على إرادةِ اللهِ تعالى صدق التصديق ؛ أي : صدق الرسل الناشئ عن تصديقِهِ تعالى لهم بذلك الخارقِ ، والله تعالى أعلم . وقد قرَّر إمام الحرمين : أنَّ المعجزة لا تدل دلالة الأدلَّةِ العقليَّةِ مِنْ : حيث يتصور وجود الخارقِ بدونِ دلالة النبوة ، والدليل العقلي لا يصح أنْ يُوجَدَ عريّاً عن دلالتِهِ . قالَ المُقترَحُ : ( وهذهِ مغالطةٌ ؛ فإنَّ الدليل ليسَ مُجرَّدَ وجودِ الخارقِ ، وإنَّما الدلالة مِنْ حيثُ إجابة دعوى المتحدّي بالخارقِ ، فمُجرَّدُ الخارقِ لا يدلُّ إذاً ، فلم يكن هذا نقضاً على مَنْ أجراها مجرى الأدلة العقلية
And an objection was raised against this opinion: that verification with us is a report about truthfulness, and the report of Allah the Exalted is eternal, not permissible to be contingent nor an attribute, so it is not permissible that will attaches to it; because will, like power, only attaches to the contingent possible. And it may be answered: that the verification to which will attaches is the verification of this miraculous event; i.e., His creating it as indicating His report about the truth of His messengers, so that His report indicating the truth of His messengers is indicated by this contingent verification which is the object of His will, may He be glorified and exalted. Or it may be answered: that the statement involves an omitted genitive; i.e., the miraculous event with the mentioned conditions indicates Allah's will of the truth of the verification; i.e., the truth of the messengers arising from His verification of them by that miracle. And Allah knows best. And Imam al-Haramayn established: that the miracle does not indicate with the indication of rational proofs, because it is conceivable that the miracle exist without indicating prophethood, and a rational proof is not valid to exist devoid of its indication. The proposer said: (This is a sophistry; for the proof is not merely the existence of the miracle; rather the indication is from the aspect of responding to the challenger's claim by the miracle; so the mere miracle does not indicate, so this is not a refutation against those who run it in the course of rational proofs.)
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit concerning the rational indication of miracles. The objection argues that verification (تصديق) is God's eternal speech (خبر), not a contingent attribute, so it cannot be the object of divine will, which only attaches to possible contingent things. The first answer reinterprets 'verification' as the contingent act of creating the miracle that indicates God's report about the prophet's truthfulness. The second answer proposes an elision: the miracle indicates God's will that the verification be true, meaning the truth of the messengers resulting from God's verification. Then Imam al-Haramayn's argument is cited: a miracle could exist without indicating prophethood (e.g., if it happened without a challenge), whereas a rational proof necessarily indicates its conclusion. The proposer (al-Muqtarah) rebuts, calling this a sophism because the miracle's indication is not from its mere existence but from its being a response to a challenge, so the comparison to rational proofs fails.

خبر
إرادة

معجزة
خارق
دلالة عقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection completely refutes the rational indication of miracles.
Correction: The answers show that 'verification' can be interpreted contingently, preserving the indication.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection to the rational indication of miracles?
A) Miracles can be imitated by magicians.
B) Verification is an eternal attribute of God, not contingent.
C) Rational proofs cannot be applied to miracles.
D) The miracle might be a mere coincidence.

Key Takeaway
Miracles indicate prophethood not by mere existence but by being a response to a challenge, which distinguishes them from rational proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever expected a clear, undeniable sign from Allah to confirm something in your faith, and when it didn't come, you doubted? How does this lesson's distinction between a miracle as a response to a challenge versus a random event change your understanding of what you're asking for? Will you continue to wait for a sign, or will you trust the messengers who have already come?
?
When you face a situation where your faith is tested, do you find yourself demanding a 'miracle' to prove that Allah is with you? This lesson teaches that even miracles only indicate truth when they answer a specific claim. What does that reveal about your heart's expectation? Are you willing to believe without a personal supernatural display, relying on the signs that have already been given?
?
Al-Muqtarah warns against sophistry: assuming that the mere existence of a miracle proves prophethood. In your daily life, have you ever fallen into the trap of thinking that any extraordinary event must be from Allah, without considering its context? How can you apply the lesson's emphasis on the challenge to discern true signs from false? Will you commit to studying the conditions of miracles before drawing conclusions?

Miracles and Rational Proof
Sanusi discusses the rational proof of miracles and an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how miracles serve as rational proofs for prophethood and why they require the context of a challenge.

Core Idea
A miracle is a rational proof only when it responds to a challenge.

In this passage, Sanusi explains the rational basis for miracles as evidence of prophethood. He first presents a standard account: God creates a miracle as evidence for the truth of His messengers. He then addresses the objection by Imam al-Haramayn that miracles do not function like rational proofs because they could exist without indicating prophethood. The proposer counters that the proof lies not in the mere miracle but in its connection to a challenge. This lesson unpacks this subtle but crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the footnote markers (١), (٢), (٣) in the Arabic.
2
Identify the standard view
Find where Sanusi describes how a miracle (khāriq) functions as evidence for the truthfulness of messengers. Understand that the miracle is created by God and indicates His report.
The passage begins with 'بهذا الخارقِ' and explains this.
3
Understand Imam al-Haramayn's objection
Imam al-Haramayn argues that miracles do not indicate like rational proofs because a miracle could exist without indicating prophethood. Contrast this with a rational proof, which cannot exist without its indication.
Look for the phrase 'قد قرَّر إمام الحرمين'.
4
Grasp the proposer's response
The proposer says the objection is a fallacy: the proof is not the mere existence of the miracle but the fact that it comes as a response to a challenger's claim. Thus a miracle without a challenge does not indicate, so it is still a rational proof in context.
The response begins with 'قالَ المُقترَحُ'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بهذا الخارقِ؛ أي: خلقُهُ لهُ دالاً على خبره تعالى بصدق رسله، فيكون خبره تعالى الدال على صدق رسله مدلولاً لهذا التصديق الحادث الذي هو مُتعلَّق لإرادتِهِ جَلَّ وعزَّ. أو يُجابُ: بأنَّ الكلام فيهِ حذف مضاف؛ أي: الخارقُ بالشروط المذكورة يدلُّ على إرادةِ اللهِ تعالى صدق التصديق؛ أي: صدق الرسل الناشئ عن تصديقِهِ تعالى لهم بذلك الخارقِ، والله تعالى أعلم. وقد قرَّر إمام الحرمين: أنَّ المعجزة لا تدل دلالة الأدلَّةِ العقليَّةِ مِنْ: حيث يتصور وجود الخارقِ بدونِ دلالة النبوة، والدليل العقلي لا يصح أنْ يُوجَدَ عريّاً عن دلالتِهِ (١) قالَ المُقترَحُ: (وهذهِ مغالطةٌ؛ فإنَّ الدليل ليسَ مُجرَّدَ وجودِ الخارقِ (٢)، وإنَّما الدلالة مِنْ حيثُ إجابة دعوى المتحدّي بالخارقِ، فمُجرَّدُ الخارقِ لا يدلُّ إذاً، فلم يكن هذا نقضاً على مَنْ أجراها مجرى الأدلة العقلية ((٣)
By this miraculous event – i.e., His creating it is evidence of His (Exalted) report about the truthfulness of His messengers. Thus, His (Exalted) report that indicates the truthfulness of His messengers is the thing indicated by this occurring attestation which is the object of His (Mighty and Exalted) will. Or it is answered: that the statement contains an omitted complement; i.e., the miraculous event under the mentioned conditions indicates God's (Exalted) will for the truthfulness of the attestation; i.e., the truthfulness of the messengers arising from His (Exalted) attesting them with that miraculous event. And God (Exalted) knows best. And Imam al-Haramayn established: that the miracle does not indicate in the manner of rational proofs, because it is conceivable for a miraculous event to exist without indicating prophethood, while a rational proof cannot exist devoid of its indication (1). The proposer said: 'This is a fallacy; for the proof is not merely the existence of the miraculous event (2), but rather the indication is from the aspect of responding to the challenger's claim by the miraculous event. So the mere miraculous event does not indicate then. So this is not a refutation against those who treat it in the manner of rational proofs (3).'
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage discusses the rational proof of miracles and addresses an objection. It begins by explaining that a miracle (khāriq) is created by God as evidence for the truthfulness of the messengers. The existing miracle is the thing indicated by God's report that confirms the messengers. Then an alternative answer is given: the phrase is elliptical, meaning the miracle indicates God's will to attest to the truthfulness of the messengers. Next, Imam al-Haramayn is quoted: miracles do not indicate in the manner of rational proofs because a miracle could conceivably exist without indicating prophethood, while a rational proof cannot exist without its indication. The proposer objects that this is a fallacy: the proof is not the mere existence of the miracle, but the indication arises from the challenge (daʿwā al-mutaḥaddī) that the miracle responds to. Thus, a miracle without a challenge does not indicate, so the objection does not refute those who treat miracles as rational proofs. The passage maintains the rational basis but clarifies its context.

إمام الحرمين

دعوى المتحدّي
