Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any supernatural event is automatically a rational proof of prophethood.
Correction: The passage clarifies that a miracle only indicates prophethood when it is performed in response to a challenge (daʿwā al-mutaḥaddī). Without that context, the mere existence of a miraculous event does not serve as proof.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does Imam al-Haramayn argue that miracles do not indicate in the manner of rational proofs?
A) Because miracles are not necessary for proof.
B) Because a miracle can exist without indicating prophethood.
C) Because miracles are conventional.
D) Because miracles are based on custom.

Key Takeaway
Miracles are rational proofs for prophethood only when they occur as a response to a challenge; otherwise, they are not automatically indicative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows that even rational proofs have conditions. When you seek signs from God in your own life, do you demand that they come in response to a direct challenge? Or do you expect God to prove Himself without you taking a step of faith? How can you cultivate patience and discernment instead of demanding signs on your terms?
?

Let this lesson humble you: God’s signs are not for entertainment but for guidance. Are you willing to reevaluate your past experiences in light of this truth?

Conventional and Customary Proofs of Miracles
Sanusi enumerates the conventional and customary proofs of miracles and notes their relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how miracles can indicate truthfulness through conventional agreement (waḍʿ) and through customary association (ʿāda), and how these relate to rational proof.

Core Idea
Miracles prove prophethood through signs we interpret by convention or custom.

Sanusi continues his discussion of the proofs of miracles by presenting two additional types: conventional indication, based on implicit agreement, and customary indication, based on habitual association. He explains how these differ from purely rational proof and notes that ultimately they reduce to a rational foundation. This passage is crucial for understanding how miracles function as evidence within kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two proofs
First read the Arabic and translation to see the structure: the second proof (conventional) and third proof (customary) are clearly marked. Notice how Sanusi gives examples for each.
2
Understand the conventional proof example
The king-and-messenger example shows how an action (standing) becomes a conventional sign of truthfulness when it's understood as a response to a challenge. Focus on the condition that the actor is aware of the challenge.
3
See how the first two views reduce to one
The passage notes that the rational and conventional views essentially agree that the indication is rational; they differ only in explanation. This is an important point about the unity of the rational approach.
4
Examine the customary proof
Customary indication is like the natural signs of emotions: embarrassment, fear, etc. Miracles can also be linked to truthfulness through customary association, though the passage acknowledges that the conventional proof ultimately ties back to the attribute of speech.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Think about why miracles are linked to God's speech (kalām) and the impossibility of lying. This connects miracles to divine communication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنَّ دلالتها وضعية: كدلالة الألفاظ بالوضع على معانيها • قالوا: إلا أنَّ المواضعة قد تُعرَفُ بصريح يدلُّ على التواضع كما لو قال شخص لشخص: (إن فعلتُ كذا فاعلم بذلك قصدي في طلبك)، ففعل ما واضعَهُ عليهِ، فإِنَّ مَنْ وقعَتْ مَعَهُ المواضعةُ يفهم طلبه على حسَبِ ما واضعَهُ عليهِ. وقد تُعرَف المواضعةُ بجمع بصريح مِنْ أحد المتواضعينِ، وفعلٍ مِنَ الثاني مِنْ غيرِ أنْ يسمعَ كلامَهُ، فإذا قالَ شخص في مَحْفِل بمجلس ملكِ وقد تأزَّرَ مجلسُهُ (1): أنا رسولُ الملكِ إليكم، وآيتي يخرق عادته، وهو بمرأى مِنَ الملكِ ومسمع، ثم قال: أيُّها الملكُ؛ إن كنتُ صادقاً فاخرق عادتكَ وقُمْ واقعدْ، فأجابَهُ إلى القيام... كانَ ذلك كالتصريح بالمواضعة على أنَّ خَرْقَ عادتِهِ بقيامِهِ يَدلُّ على إرساله. وظاهر كلام المقترح وكثيرٍ: أنَّ هذين الرأيين يرجعان إلى قول واحد؛ وهو أنَّ الدلالة عقليَّةٌ، وإنَّما اختلفا في تقرير كونها عقليَّةً والأمر في هذا قريب (۲) الثالثُ: أنَّ دلالتها عاديَّةٌ: كدَلالة قرائنِ الأحوالِ على خَجَلِ الخَجِلِ ووَجَلِ الوَجِلِ وخوفِ الخائف رجوع دلالة المعجزة بالمواضعة لصفة الكلام، فلذا يذكرون معها استحالة الكذب على الله تعالى، فحكم الفعل في المواضعة حكم الكلام الصريح
The second: that its indication is conventional: like the indication of words by convention upon their meanings. They said: except that the convention may be known by explicit [statement] indicating humility. As if one person said to another: 'If I do such-and-such, then know by that my intent in your request', then he did what he had agreed with him upon, then the one with whom the convention was made understands his request according to what he had agreed with him upon. And the convention may be known by a combination of an explicit statement from one of the two parties making the convention, and an action from the second without hearing his speech. So if a person said in an assembly in a king's court, while his court was crowded (1): 'I am the king's messenger to you, and my sign is that he breaks his custom, while he is in the king's sight and hearing', then he said: 'O king, if I am truthful, then break your custom and stand and sit', and he answered him by standing... that was like an explicit declaration of the convention that the breaking of his custom by his standing indicates his sending. And it is apparent from the speech of the proposer and many that these two opinions reduce to one statement: that the indication is rational, but they differed in establishing how it is rational, and the matter in this is close (2). The third: that its indication is customary: like the indication of circumstantial evidence upon the embarrassment of the embarrassed and the fear of the fearful and the fright of the frightened. The return of the miracle's indication through convention to the attribute of speech, so they mention with it the impossibility of lying about God (Exalted). So the ruling of an action in the convention is like the ruling of explicit speech.
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage enumerates two more types of evidence for miracles: the conventional (waḍʿiyya) and the customary (ʿādiyya). It begins by defining conventional indication as like the indication of words by agreed convention. The convention may be known by explicit statement or by a combination of explicit statement and action. An example is given: a person claims to be the king's messenger and challenges the king to break his custom by standing up. The king's action becomes a conventional sign of the messenger's truthfulness. The passage then notes that the first two views (rational and conventional) essentially reduce to one: both consider the indication rational, differing only in formulation. Then the third view is presented: customary indication, like the link between emotional states and their outward signs (e.g., embarrassment and blushing). Finally, a remark explains that the conventional indication of miracles ultimately refers to the attribute of speech (ṣifat al-kalām), so they mention the impossibility of lying about God, and the ruling of an action in convention is like explicit speech.

Key Terms
دلالة وضعية
المواضعة
دلالة عادية
قرائن الأحوال
صفة الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think conventional and customary proof are the same or interchangeable.
Correction: The passage distinguishes them: conventional relies on an implicit agreement (e.g., the king's response to a challenge), while customary relies on habitual association (e.g., blushing with embarrassment). They are different types of indication.

Checkpoint

B) It must occur in response to a challenge.
C) It must be witnessed by the king.
D) It must be explicitly agreed upon beforehand.

Key Takeaway
Miracles prove prophethood through conventional signs that ultimately rely on rational demonstration and are tied to God's attribute of speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When the king's messenger pointed to a visible sign—the king breaking his custom—you see how a conventional sign demands a response. Consider the signs God has placed before you: the Quran itself, the miracles of the prophets, the order of creation. Do you treat them as mere interesting facts, or as binding conventions that call you to submit? What would it take for you to respond as if the King Himself has spoken?
?
The passage ties the miracle's proof to God's attribute of speech and the impossibility of lying. You claim to believe that God never lies and that the Quran is His speech. But when you read verses that command you to believe, pray, give charity, do you obey as if you heard them directly from His mouth? Or do you hesitate, rationalise, delay? What is holding you back from acting on His explicit words?
?
The customary proof works through habitual associations—like blushing from shame. Think of your own habits: your automatic reactions, your emotional responses, your daily routines. Do these habits point toward faith or away from it? What do your ingrained reactions reveal about what you truly believe in your heart? Will you examine them and realign them with the truth?

Impossibility of Miracles from Liars
Two arguments demonstrate the rational impossibility of miracles from liars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments for why miracles cannot be performed by liars, based on the first two views of the nature of miracles.

Core Idea
A miracle at a liar's hand would contradict its own purpose.

In this passage, the author presents two decisive arguments that establish the rational impossibility of miracles occurring through liars. These arguments rest on the two earlier views regarding the nature of miraculous events. The first argument shows that a miracle from a liar would invert the rational proof. The second shows it would breach the implicit promise of God. Understanding these arguments is essential to grasp the connection between miracles and truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then its translation to get the gist of the argument.
Notice the phrase 'على الرأيين الأولين' which links to earlier discussions.
2
Identify the two views
The passage builds on two earlier views: the first view (related to rational proof) and the second view (al-muwada'a, convention). Understand that these are foundational.
3
Analyze the first argument
Focus on 'أما على الأول' and why the first view leads to impossibility: it would contradict the rational proof, turning it into a specious argument and knowledge into compound ignorance.
The phrase 'قلب للحقائق' emphasizes the inversion.
4
Analyze the second argument
Focus on 'أما على الثاني' and why the convention view leads to impossibility: a miracle from a liar would be a breach of God's implicit promise (خلف في الخبر).
The analogy with explicit speech clarifies the point.
5
Reflect on the meaning
Consider how both arguments reinforce the theological necessity that miracles confirm truthfulness. Why is this important for proving prophethood?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : فإنَّ خَلْقَ اللهِ تعالى لهذا الخارقِ على هذا الوجه المفروض يدلُّ على صدقه بالضرورة عادةً فعلى الرأيين الأولين : يستحيلُ عقلاً صدور المعجزاتِ على أيدي الكاذبين : أما على الأَوَّلِ : فلما يلزمُ مِنْ نَقْضِ الدليل العقليّ ؛ بأنْ يُوجَدَ ولا يُوجَدَ مدلوله ، فيصيرُ ذلكَ الدليلُ شبهةً ، ويصيرُ العلم الذي استلزمَهُ جهلاً مُركَّباً ؛ وذلكَ قلب للحقائق ، ولا خفاءَ باستحالتِهِ وأما على الثاني - وهو المواضعة - : فلما يلزمُ مِنَ الخُلْفِ في خبرِهِ تعالى ؛ لأنَّ حكم المواضعة في الفعل حكم الكلام الصريح
They said: Indeed, the creation of Allah, exalted, of this miraculous event (al-khariq) in the supposed manner indicates his truthfulness necessarily by custom. According to the two earlier views, it is rationally impossible for miracles to occur at the hands of liars. As for the first: because of the contradiction of the rational proof; that it exists while its implication does not exist, so that proof becomes a specious argument, and the knowledge that it entailed becomes compound ignorance; and that is an inversion of realities, and its impossibility is not hidden. As for the second—which is the convention (al-muwada'a)—: because of the breach of promise in His report; for the ruling of convention in action is like the ruling of explicit speech.
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments showing the impossibility of miracles being performed by liars. The first argument is based on the first of two earlier views: if a miracle occurred at the hands of a liar, the rational proof that miracles confirm truthfulness would be contradicted—the proof would exist without its intended implication, turning it into a specious argument and knowledge into compound ignorance. That is an inversion of realities and rationally impossible. The second argument is based on the second view of convention (al-muwada'a): God's creation of a miracle for a liar would be a breach of His implied promise that miracles indicate truthfulness, just as explicit speech would be broken. Thus, both views show that liars cannot perform miracles.

Key Terms
خارق
معجزة
دليل عقلي
مواضعة
جهل مركب
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think a miracle can be performed by a liar if God allows it, but this passage shows it is impossible based on rational and conventional grounds.
Correction: A miracle from a liar would either contradict the rational proof that miracles confirm truthfulness or breach God's implicit promise, both of which are rationally impossible.

Checkpoint

C) Because the miracle would not be miraculous
D) Because the liar would become truthful

Key Takeaway
Miracles cannot be performed by liars because doing so would either contradict rational proof or break God's implicit promise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this lesson change your evaluation of such events? Will you now examine your intentions more closely before interpreting any 'sign'?

Miracles as Conventional Proof of Prophethood
Explains the conventional indication of miracles and why liars cannot perform them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how miracles conventionally indicate prophethood and why the possibility of a liar performing a miracle does not undermine certainty.

Core Idea
Miracles reliably indicate truth through God's consistent custom, not logical necessity.

This lesson explores the conventional (ʿādī) nature of miracles as proof of prophethood. Sanusi explains that while it is possible in principle for a liar to perform a miracle, God's actual practice ensures that miracles are only given to truthful prophets. The passage clarifies that possibility does not negate actual certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic once for flow, then read the translation to understand the argument.
Notice the structure: a conditional statement followed by an exception.
2
Identify key concepts
Note the terms: conventional indication, necessary knowledge, custom-breaking (kharq al-ʿādah), and possibility (jawāz). Understand how they relate.
3
Trace the argument
Follow the logic: necessary knowledge → impossibility of liar; but if custom broken, no knowledge; God's grace maintains custom. Consider why this addresses a common objection.
The key insight is that possibility does not entail occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا إِنْ قلنا : إنَّ دلالة المعجزة عاديَّةٌ بحسَبِ القرائن .. فحيثُ حصل العلمُ الضروري عنها بصدقِ الآتي بها فإنَّهُ يستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ كاذباً ، وإلا انقلب العلم الضروري جهلاً ، ولم يُجْرِ سبحانه وتعالى عادتَهُ مِنْ أَوَّلِ الدنيا إلى الآنَ إلا بعدم تمكين الكاذب مِنَ المعجزاتِ وإذا خيَّلَ بسحرٍ ونحوِهِ أظهرَ اللهُ فضيحتَهُ عن قُرْبٍ ، فللَّهِ الحمد على معاملته في ذلك ونحوه بمخض الفضل والكرم . ويجوز أن تظهر المعجزة على يد الكاذب لو انخرقَتِ العادةُ ، ولا يحصلُ حينَئِذٍ بها علمُ صدقِهِ ، وإلا كانَ الجهلُ علماً ، وتجويز خرْقِ العادةِ عند حصولِ العلمِ بالصدق في حقِّ المحقِّ لا يقدح في العلم ؛ إِذْ لا يلزمُ مِنْ جواز الشيء وقوعه ، ألا ترى أَنَّا نُجوِّزُ استمرار عدم العالم معَ علمنا ضرورةً بوجودِهِ ؛ إذْ معنى الجواز : أَنَّهُ لو قُدِّرَ واقعاً لم يلزم منه محال لذاته ؛ لأنَّه محتمل الوقوع . هذا الكلام في غاية الوضوح ، وحاصله : أنَّه يجوز على القول بأنَّ دلالة المعجزة عادية أن تظهر المعجزة على أيدي الكاذبينَ ولا يكون العلمُ حينَئِذٍ حاصلاً بنبوتهم ، وإلا انقلبَ العلم جهلاً ، إلا أنَّهُ سبحانَهُ تفضّل بعدم خرْقِ العادةِ في هذا الأمر ، فلم يُظهِرِ المعجزة
And if we say: that the indication of the miracle is conventional according to the circumstances... then when necessary knowledge is obtained from it of the truthfulness of the one who brings it, it is impossible that he be a liar; otherwise necessary knowledge would turn into ignorance. And He, glorified and exalted, has not continued His custom from the beginning of the world until now except by not empowering the liar with miracles. And if he deludes with sorcery or the like, God exposes his disgrace quickly. So to God be praise for His dealing in that and the like with pure grace and generosity. And it is possible that a miracle appear at the hand of a liar if the custom were broken, but then no knowledge of his truthfulness would be obtained from it; otherwise ignorance would become knowledge. And allowing the breaking of custom when knowledge of truthfulness is obtained in the case of the truthful one does not undermine knowledge; because the mere possibility of something does not entail its occurrence. Do you not see that we allow the possibility of the world's non-existence even though we know necessarily of its existence? For the meaning of possibility is: that if it were supposed to occur, no intrinsic impossibility would follow from it, because it is possible to occur. This speech is extremely clear, and its summary is: that it is possible, on the view that the indication of the miracle is conventional, that a miracle appear at the hands of liars, and then no knowledge of their prophethood would be obtained; otherwise knowledge would turn into ignorance. However, He, glorified, has graciously not broken the custom in this matter, so He has not caused the miracle to appear.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage discusses the conventional (ʿādī) nature of miracles as proof of prophethood. It argues that when a miracle produces necessary knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness, it is impossible for him to be a liar; otherwise, knowledge would become ignorance. God's custom has always been not to empower liars with miracles, and if a liar attempts sorcery, God exposes him. However, if the custom were broken, a miracle could appear at the hand of a liar, but then no certain knowledge would result. The possibility of a custom-break does not undermine the certainty of knowledge in the actual case, because possibility does not entail occurrence. The passage concludes that God, by His grace, does not break the custom, so miracles reliably indicate truth.

Key Terms
المعجزة
الدلالة العادية
العلم الضروري
خرق العادة
الجواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A miracle could only be a proof if it is logically impossible for a liar to perform it.
Correction: The passage teaches that miracles are reliable not by logical necessity but by God's consistent custom. Even if a custom-break is possible, it does not occur in practice, so certainty is preserved.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say would happen if a miracle appeared at the hand of a liar while the custom was broken?
A) His truthfulness would be known with certainty
B) No knowledge of his truthfulness would be obtained
C) The miracle would be a true sign
D) The liar would be exposed immediately

Key Takeaway
Miracles are reliable signs of prophethood because God's consistent custom prevents liars from performing them, and the mere possibility of a custom-break does not undermine actual certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you see someone who seems to succeed through falsehood, do you remember that God exposes liars and does not empower them with true miracles? How does this lesson strengthen your patience and reliance on Him?
?
The passage says God deals with us 'with pure grace and generosity' in protecting miracles. Have you ever felt God's grace in a situation where truth was made plain despite confusion? Can you commit to thanking Him specifically for that grace this week?

Second View: Miracles as Conventional Evidence
Sanusi presents the second view on the impossibility of Allah misleading through conventional miracle evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that because a miracle functions like an explicit divine confirmation, Allah cannot mislead through it, even if its evidentiary status is conventional.

Core Idea
A miracle is like God's explicit speech; since God never breaks His promise, He cannot mislead through miracles.

This lesson examines a key argument in the debate over whether Allah can mislead through miracles. The second view holds that miracles, while conventional in nature, serve the same function as explicit divine speech confirming a prophet's truthfulness. The argument then draws a logical conclusion: if Allah cannot lie in direct speech, He cannot deceive through the miracle either.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that there are two views on the evidentiary basis of miracles: one based on direct divine speech and one based on convention (al-muwāḍa‘a). This lesson focuses on the second view.
2
Grasp the second view's starting point
Read the first part of the passage: the proponents of the second view assert that it is possible for Allah to mislead, but not through creating false belief in a statement. Misleading is possible in other ways, but not through the miracle itself.
The key is that the miracle is not a direct lie.
3
Recognize the analogy with explicit speech
Notice the central move: the miracle is compared to an explicit declaration (taṣrīḥ) that confirms the prophet's truthfulness. Just as Allah cannot break His promise in direct speech, He cannot break the implied promise of the miracle.
The Arabic terms 'al-taṣrīḥ' (explicit speech) and 'al-taṣdīq' (confirmation) are crucial.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
The passage concludes that misleading through what indicates confirmation is impossible, even if the evidence is conventional. This ties the impossibility of misleading to the inviolability of divine promises.
The argument rests on the principle that Allah cannot contradict what He has manifested.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما على الثاني - وهو أنَّ الدليلَ مِنْ جهة المواضعة - فقالوا : يجوز أنْ يُضِلَّ لا بالخَلْقِ في القولِ ، وإذا كانَتِ المعجزة تتنزَّلُ منزلةَ التصريح بكلام ناص على التصديقِ ، ولا يصح الإضلال به ؛ لاستحالة الخلْفِ في خبره تعالى .. فكذا لا يصح الإضلال بما يدلُّ على التصديق وإنْ كانَ بحكم المواضعةِ .
As for the second (view) - which is that the evidence is by convention - they said: It is possible that He misleads, not by creation in speech. And when the miracle is ranked as explicit speech indicating confirmation, and misleading by it is not valid due to the impossibility of breaking His promise, may He be exalted... so likewise, misleading by what indicates confirmation is not valid even if it is by convention.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage presents the second view regarding the possibility of Allah misleading through miracles. According to this view, the evidence of the miracle is conventional (bi al-muwāḍa‘a). The proponents of this view argue that while it is possible for Allah to mislead, that is not by creating false belief in the statement itself. The key point is that a miracle is treated as if it were an explicit statement (taṣrīḥ) that clearly indicates the truthfulness of the prophet. Since it is impossible for Allah to break His promise—He cannot lie or contradict what He has manifested—it is impossible for Him to mislead through the miracle. By extension, anything that indicates confirmation (al-taṣdīq) cannot be used for misleading, even if its evidence is conventional. The argument relies on the principle that divine promises are inviolable, and the miracle is akin to God's explicit speech confirming the prophet.

Key Terms
الدليل المواضعة
المعجزة
الإضلال
التصريح
التصديق

Checkpoint

C) Because miracles are not conventional evidence
D) Because prophets are infallible

Key Takeaway
The second view argues that miracles, even though conventional, are treated as explicit divine speech, and because Allah never breaks His promise, He cannot mislead through them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Have you ever justified a decision based on a conventional 'sign' (e.g., a dream, a coincidence) only to later question its reliability? How does this lesson assure you that a true sign from Allah is never deceptive, even if it comes through conventional means?
?
If you truly believed that every authentic miracle is as trustworthy as Allah's own speech, would it transform your certainty in the prophethood of Muhammad (ﷺ)? What would change in your daily life if you held that conviction without doubt?

Certainty Despite Rational Possibility: The Third View on Miracles
Sanusi explains the third view on miracles and defends certainty against the objection of rational possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third view on miracles argues that the rational possibility of falsehood does not undermine the necessary knowledge of a prophet's truthfulness, using the analogy of self-existence.

Core Idea
Knowledge excludes its opposite; rational possibility is not actual doubt.

Sanusi presents the third view on miracles, which holds that the evidence of a miracle is customary (i.e., by Allah's custom, it produces knowledge). The key challenge is: if we can rationally conceive that a prophet might be false, how can we be certain? Sanusi answers by distinguishing between rational possibility and actual probability, using the powerful analogy of our knowledge of our own existence despite the logical possibility of non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from stating the view to addressing the objection.
The passage includes a marginal note (قوله) that clarifies a phrase.
2
Identify the core claim about knowledge
Find the statement that knowledge does not admit its opposite. Understand why this is crucial: if it admitted its opposite, it would be ignorance, not knowledge.
3
Understand the objection and the answer
The objection is that we rationally allow falsehood for a truthful person. The answer is that 'rational allowance' means no logical impossibility from the opposite occurring, not that the opposite is probable.
Sanusi uses the term 'jawāz 'aqlī' (rational permissibility).
4
Analyze the self-existence analogy
Read the analogy: we know our own existence necessarily, yet we can rationally allow that we might not have existed. This shows that rational possibility does not negate certain knowledge.
Apply this analogy to the prophet: the miracle gives necessary knowledge of truth, and the rational possibility of falsehood does not affect it.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this argument defends certainty about prophethood. The marginal note ties it back to 'breaking the custom' (kharq al-ʿāda).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما على الرأي الثالث - وهو أنَّ دلالة المعجزة عادية - : فأمرُ الجواب أيضاً سهل ؛ وهو أنَّ آيةَ صدقِ النبي حصولُ العلم تلك المعجزة ، فإذا حصل انتفى معَهُ احتمال عدم الصدقِ ؛ لأنَّ العلم لا يحتمل النقيض بوجهِ مِنَ الوجوه ، وإلا انقلب جهلاً وتجويزنا عقلاً كذبَ المحقِّ الذي تيقنا صدقه لا يقدح في العلم بصدقِهِ ؛ لأنَّ معنى جواز الكذب في حقِّهِ : أَنَّهُ لو وقع بدلاً عن الصدْقِ الواقع في حقِّهِ لم يلزم منه محال ، لا أنَّ معناه احتمال وقوع الكذب في حقه تَقَّهِ ، وكثيراً ما نعلمُ وقوع أشياءَ علماً ضرورياً مع تجويزنا عقلاً نقيض ذلك الواقع ؛ وذلك كعلمنا بوجودنا ؛ فإنَّهُ لا يستريبُ فيهِ عاقل ، وإنْ كنَّا نُجوِّرُ عدَمنا ؛ بمعنى : لو استمر عدمُنا ولم نُوجَدْ أصلاً .. لم يلزم منه محال ، لا بمعنى : أنَّ عدمنا محتمل الحصول لنا حالَ علمنا بوجودنا . قوله : ( في حقٌّ المحقِّ ( الأولى : تعلقه بـ ( خرْقِ العادةِ ) أي : تجويزنا عقلاً خرْقَ العادةِ في حق المحقِّ ؛ بمعنى : لو كان الواقع في حقه الكذبَ بدلاً عن الصدق الذي علمنا وقوعَهُ في حقِّهِ لَمَا لزم منه محال . . لا يقدح في علمنا بصدقه (۱)
As for the third opinion - which is that the indication of the miracle is customary - the matter of the answer is also easy; it is that the sign of the truthfulness of the prophet is the occurrence of knowledge from that miracle. So when it occurs, the possibility of untruthfulness is removed along with it, because knowledge does not admit its opposite in any way; otherwise it would turn into ignorance. And our rational allowance of the falsehood of the truthful one whose truthfulness we are certain of does not detract from the knowledge of his truthfulness, because the meaning of the permissibility of falsehood in his case is: if it had occurred instead of the actual truthfulness in his case, no impossibility would follow from it, not that its meaning is the possibility of the occurrence of falsehood in his case [تَقَّهِ?]. And often we know the occurrence of things with necessary knowledge while we rationally allow the opposite of that actual occurrence; such as our knowledge of our existence; for no rational person doubts it, even though we allow our non-existence; meaning: if our non-existence had continued and we had not been created at all, no impossibility would follow from it, not that our non-existence is possibly occurring to us at the time of our knowledge of our existence. His saying: 'concerning the truthful one' – the first: its attachment is to 'breaking the custom', i.e., our rational allowance of breaking the custom concerning the truthful one; meaning: if the actuality in his case had been falsehood instead of the truth which we know occurred in his case, no impossibility would have followed from it... it does not detract from our knowledge of his truthfulness.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage presents the third view on the impossibility of Allah misleading through miracles. According to this view, the miracle's evidence is customary ('ādiyya), meaning that by custom the miracle produces knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness. The argument is straightforward: the sign of the prophet's truth is the knowledge that arises from the miracle. Once that knowledge is obtained, the possibility of untruth is eliminated because knowledge by definition excludes its opposite; if it admitted its opposite, it would be ignorance, not knowledge. The author addresses a potential objection: we rationally allow the possibility that a truthful person could lie, but this rational allowance does not undermine our certain knowledge of their truthfulness. The reason is that 'rational permissibility' (jawāz 'aqlī) means that if the opposite had occurred, no logical contradiction would have followed; it does not mean that the opposite is actually probable. To clarify, the author gives the example of our knowledge of our own existence. We know it necessarily and cannot doubt it, yet we can rationally conceive that we might never have existed—that possibility does not make our existence doubtful. The same applies to the prophet: we know his truthfulness through the miracle, and the logical possibility that he could have been false does not affect that knowledge. The marginal note explains that the phrase 'concerning the truthful one' refers to 'breaking the custom', i.e., the rational allowance of a miracle-breaking custom in the case of a truthful person, which likewise does not detract from knowledge.

Key Terms
دلالة عادية
العلم الضروري
تجويز عقلي
المحق
خرق العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is rationally possible, it creates doubt about what is actually known.
Correction: The passage clarifies that rational possibility (jawāz 'aqlī) means only that the opposite involves no logical contradiction, not that it is a real probability. Certain knowledge, especially necessary knowledge, is not undermined by mere logical conceivability.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Sanusi use to show that rational possibility does not negate necessary knowledge?
A) The impossibility of a square circle
B) Our knowledge of our existence while rationally allowing non-existence
C) The necessity of causality
D) The infallibility of prophets

Key Takeaway
The rational possibility of a prophet's falsehood does not negate the necessary knowledge obtained from a miracle, just as our knowledge of our existence is not shaken by the logical possibility of non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear arguments against the prophethood of Muhammad ﷺ, do you feel any inner doubt? This lesson says rational possibility is not doubt. But what about actual hesitation? Have you ever allowed a logical 'what if' to weaken your certainty? Commit today to study the proofs until your knowledge is like your knowledge of your own existence—undeniable.
?
You know you exist without any doubt, yet you can conceive of your non-existence. Does your certainty about the Prophet ﷺ feel that immediate? If not, what are you doing to make it so? This lesson shows that rational objections should not shake you—but are you shaking? Resolve to examine the miracle of the Qur'an until your certainty is necessary knowledge.
?
The passage says knowledge excludes its opposite. Do you act as if your faith excludes doubt, or do you entertain opposing thoughts as if they are valid? This week, when a doubt arises, repeat to yourself: rational possibility does not equal real doubt. And ask: Am I treating Allah's signs as certain or as probable?

Logical Proof from Miracles for Muhammad and Jesus
Sanusi presents a logical dilemma on miracles and prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses a logical argument based on the nature of miracles to establish the truth of Muhammad and Jesus, using the Jews' own acceptance of Moses.

Core Idea
Miracles are either proof for all prophets or none—the logic is inescapable.

This lesson presents Sanusi's concise logical argument directed at Jewish interlocutors. He forces a dilemma: either miracles prove prophethood or they don't. If they don't, then even Moses' prophethood lacks evidence. If they do, then Muhammad and Jesus must be accepted. This demonstrates the power of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the argument settle in your mind.
Notice the conditional structure: 'either...or...'.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage presents a conditional (if-then) with two branches. Find the condition and the two possible outcomes.
The condition is: miracle occurs with challenge and inability to oppose.
3
Consider the audience
The argument is addressed to Jews who accept Moses. Identify how their own belief is used to force the conclusion.
The dilemma leverages their acceptance of Moses' prophethood.
4
Trace the implications
Follow each branch: if miracles do not indicate truth, then Moses' proof collapses. If they do, then Muhammad and Jesus must be believed.
This is a classic 'dilemma' argument.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this argument is compelling. It uses the opponent's own premises against them.
It does not rely on external evidence, only logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لليهودِ : وقوع الخارقِ على وَفْقِ دعوى المتحدّي معَ العجز عن معارضتهِ مَنْ تُحدِّيَ عليهِ لا يخلو : إِمَّا أَنْ يدل على صدْقِ مُدَّعي الرسالة أو لا , فإن لم يدلُّ لزم ألا تقوم دلالة على صدق موسى عليه السلامُ على زعمِكم , وإِنْ دلَّ وجب تصديق محمد وعيسى عليهما السلام.
Then we say to the Jews: The occurrence of a miracle (al-khāriq) in accordance with the claim of the challenger, along with the inability of the one challenged to oppose it, is not free: either it indicates the truthfulness of the claimant of prophethood or not. If it does not indicate, then necessarily there would be no evidence for the truth of Moses, peace be upon him, according to your claim. And if it does indicate, then it is obligatory to believe in Muhammad and Jesus, peace be upon them.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof aimed at the Jews. Sanusi presents a conditional: a miracle (kharq al-‘āda) that occurs in accordance with the claim of a person who challenges others to produce its like, while those challenged are unable to match it, must either be evidence for the truth of the claimant or not. If it is not evidence, then the miracles of Moses would not prove his prophethood, which the Jews accept. But if it is evidence, then the same logic applies to the miracles of Muhammad and Jesus, so the Jews must accept them. The argument forces the Jews to either reject their own prophet or accept the others.

Key Terms
خارق (khāriq)
تحدي (challenge)
معارضة (opposition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are not definitive proofs; they could be magic or coincidence.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the specific conditions (challenge and inability to oppose) make miracles reliable indicators of truth, as accepted for Moses.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, if a miracle does not indicate the truth of the claimant, what follows?
A) The claimant is still believed.
B) Moses' prophethood would have no evidence.
C) The miracle is rejected.
D) The Jews must accept Muhammad.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's dilemma shows that the logic of miracles forces either rejection of all prophets or acceptance of Muhammad and Jesus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi forces consistency: either accept the logic for all prophets or reject it for Moses. Is there a prophet you have accepted without truly examining the evidence, or one you have rejected out of cultural habit? What would consistency demand of you?

Do you truly believe that miracles are sufficient proof for prophethood, or do you demand additional signs? If you require more than what Sanusi describes, what might that say about your faith in the prophets you claim to follow?

The Miracle of the Qur'an's Inimitability
Sanusi enumerates the Qur'an's challenge to the Arabs and their inability to meet it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'an's inimitability (i'jāz) challenged the eloquent Arabs and why their failure to respond proves its divine origin.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's unmatched eloquence is a living miracle that the Arabs could not imitate.

Sanusi presents the Qur'an as the greatest miracle of Prophet Muhammad. He describes how the Qur'an directly challenged the most eloquent Arabs of the time, who prided themselves on their mastery of language. Despite their fierce pride, they were unable to produce even a single chapter like it, and the Qur'an progressively intensified the challenge, culminating in a declaration that all of humanity and jinn combined could not produce its like. This passage highlights the linguistic miracle as a central proof of the Qur'an's divine origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: Sanusi sets the scene with the Arabs' eloquence, then presents the Qur'anic challenge in three stages.
2
Identify the three-stage challenge
Find the three Quranic verses cited: Hud 11:13 (ten surahs), Al-Baqarah 2:23 (one surah), and Al-Isra 17:88 (total inability). Notice the progression from easier to impossible.
This shows God's gradual tightening of the challenge.
3
Reflect on the Arabs' reaction
Sanusi marvels that the Arabs did not respond, given their natural disposition to defend their honor. Ask yourself: why didn't they even try? What does their silence indicate?
Their failure is proof of the Qur'an's divine origin.
4
Connect to the concept of i'jāz
Understand that i'jāz (inimitability) is a key miracle. It is not just about content but also the linguistic form that exceeds human capacity.
This is a common misconception corrected by the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ معجزة القرآن الكريم ] وأفضلها القرآنُ العظيم الذي لم تزل تقرعُ أسماع البلغاء بتضليل كل دين غير الإسلام آياته ، وتُحرّك بطلب المعارضة على سبيل التعجيز حميَّةَ اللُّسْنِ المتوقدي الفطنة ، الأقوياء العارضة نظماً ونثراً ، الخائضين في كلِّ فن مِنْ فنونِ البلاغةِ طولاً وعرضاً ؛ بحيثُ لا تُفْلِتُ مِنْ معارضتهم أمنعُ كلمةٍ وإِنْ لم يُعرض فيها بعجزهم ، فكيف وهم يسمعون في تعجيزهم صريح قوله تعالى : ﴿ فَأْتُواْ بِعَشْرِ سُوَرٍ مِثْلِهِ مُفْتَرَيَاتٍ ﴾ [هود : ١٣] ، ثم تنزَّلَ معَهم فقالَ : ﴿ فَأْتُواْ بِسُورَةٍ مِّن مِثْلِهِ ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٣] ، ثم صرَّحَ بعجز الجميع ؛ جنهم وإنسهم ، مفترقين أو مجتمعين ، فقالَ : ﴿ قُل لَّئِنِ اجْتَمَعَتِ الإِنسُ وَالْجِنُّ عَلَى أَن يَأْتُوا بِمِثْلِ هَذَا الْقُرْآنِ لا يَأْتُونَ بِمِثْلِهِ وَلَوْ كَانَ بَعْضُهُمْ لِبَعْضٍ ظَهِيرًا ﴾ [الإسراء : ۸۸] . ومع ذلك لم تتحرَّك أنفُهم وهم المجبولونَ عليها ؟! ومِنْ عادتهم أنَّهم لا يتمالكونَ معَها ضبطَ أنفسهم عند ورودِ أدنى
[The Miracle of the Noble Qur'an] And the greatest of them is the Mighty Qur'an, whose verses continue to strike the ears of the eloquent, disparaging every religion other than Islam, and it moves, by demanding imitation as a challenge, the zeal of the fiery intelligence, the strong in eloquence, in poetry and prose, who delve into every art of rhetoric extensively; such that no most fortified word escapes their imitation, even if their inability is not shown in it. How (then) when they hear in their incapacitation the explicit saying of the Most High: 'Then bring ten forged surahs like it' [Hud 11:13], then He descended with them and said: 'Then bring a surah like it' [Al-Baqarah 2:23], then He declared the inability of all, their jinn and humans, separate or together, saying: 'Say: If mankind and the jinn gathered to bring the like of this Qur'an, they could not bring its like, even if they were helpers of one another' [Al-Isra 17:88]. Yet despite that, their noses did not move, and they are naturally disposed to that?! And it is their habit that they cannot control themselves with it at the occurrence of the least...
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage extols the Qur'an as the greatest miracle. It describes the challenge of the Qur'an to the eloquent Arabs, who prided themselves on their mastery of language in poetry and prose. The author says the Qur'an's verses constantly 'strike the ears of the eloquent,' refuting every religion other than Islam, and it stirs their zeal to attempt imitation (mu'āraḍa) as a test of their skill. Yet they are unable to produce even a single word that escapes the Qur'an's challenge. Then the author cites three verses where God progressively challenges them: first to produce ten surahs like it, then one surah, and finally declares that even if all humans and jinn cooperated, they could not produce its like. Despite their pride and natural inclination to respond to any affront, the Arabs did not rise to the challenge. The author marvels that they did not react, given their customary fierce pride. The passage is cut off at the word 'adna' (the least), suggesting further elaboration on their inability.

Key Terms
إعجاز (inimitability)
معارضة (imitation challenge)
بلاغة (eloquence)
تعجيز (incapacitation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Qur'an's miracle is merely about its content, not its linguistic form.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that the challenge is specifically about rhetorical excellence (balāgha), and the Arabs were masters of that art, yet they could not imitate it.

Checkpoint

A) Ten surahs, then one surah, then total inability

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's inimitability is proven by the Arabs' inability to meet its challenge, despite their expertise in Arabic eloquence.

If you have never felt that overwhelming awe, what does that say about your engagement with it? Will you today read even a single verse as if it were a direct challenge to your soul?
?
The passage says the Qur'an 'disparages every religion other than Islam.' Have you ever felt the sting of that when you saw others following falsehood? Or are you comfortable? Do you actually believe that the Qur'an is the final word, or do you live as if other paths are acceptable? Confront that right now.
?
The Arabs were challenged to produce ten surahs, then one. They couldn't. You are not challenged to produce a surah, but you are challenged to live by it. Are you meeting that challenge? Are you making any attempt at all, or are you like the Arabs who remained silent? What will you do today to respond to the Qur'an's call?

The Quran's Miraculous Challenge
Sanusi enumerates the Quranic challenge and the Arabs' silence as proof of i'jaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's inimitability (i'jaz) is proved through the escalating challenge to the Arabs and their inexplicable silence.

Core Idea
The Arabs' silence before the Quranic challenge is the loudest proof of its divine origin.

Sanusi presents the Quran as the greatest miracle, arguing that its challenge to the masters of eloquence—the Arabs—and their failure to respond despite their pride is a decisive proof of its divine origin. This passage walks through the three stages of the challenge and highlights the paradoxical silence of those who were most capable of rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of Sanusi's argument.
2
Identify the three stages of the challenge
Find the three Quranic verses quoted: ten surahs (Hud 13), one surah (al-Baqara 23), and the collective challenge (al-Isra' 88). Notice the escalating ease.
The challenge moves from ten surahs to one surah, then to a declaration of total inability.
3
Consider the psychological argument
Sanusi points out that the Arabs were extremely proud and would react aggressively to any slight. Their silence in the face of a direct challenge in their own field of eloquence is thus extraordinary.
Think about why their silence is considered a miracle.
4
Reflect on the proof of prophethood
Ask yourself: How does the Arabs' inability to meet the challenge prove that the Quran is from Allah and that Muhammad is His messenger?
The challenge is a direct test of divine origin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معجزة القرآن الكريم وأفضلها القرآنُ العظيم الذي لم تزل تقرعُ أسماع البلغاء بتضليل كل دين غير الإسلام آياته ، وتُحرّك بطلب المعارضة على سبيل التعجيز حميَّةَ اللُّسْنِ المتوقدي الفطنة ، الأقوياء العارضة نظماً ونثراً ، الخائضين في كلِّ فن مِنْ فنونِ البلاغةِ طولاً وعرضاً ؛ بحيثُ لا تُفْلِتُ مِنْ معارضتهم أمنعُ كلمةٍ وإِنْ لم يُعرض فيها بعجزهم ، فكيف وهم يسمعون في تعجيزهم صريح قوله تعالى : فَأْتُواْ بِعَشْرِ سُوَرٍ مِثْلِهِ مُفْتَرَيَتٍ ﴾ [هود : ١٣] ، ثم تنزَّلَ معَهم فقال : ﴿ فَأْتُواْ بِسُورَةٍ مِّن مِثْلِهِ ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٣] ، ثم صرَّحَ بعجز الجميع ؛ إنسهم وجنهم ، مفترقين أو مجتمعين ، فقالَ : قُل لَّينِ اجْتَمَعَتِ الإِنسُ وَالْجِنُّ عَلَى أَن يَأْتُوا بِمِثْلِ هَذَا الْقُرْآنِ لا يَأْتُونَ بِمِثْلِهِ وَلَوْ كَانَ بَعْضُهُمْ لِبَعْضِ ظَهِيرًا ﴾ [الإسراء : ۸۸] . ومع ذلك لم تتحرَّك أنفَتُهم وهم المجبولونَ عليها ؟! ومِنْ عادتهم أنَّهم لا يتمالكونَ معَها ضبطَ أنفسهم عند ورودِ أدنى عارض يقدح في مناصبهم وإنْ كانَ في ذلك حتفُ أنفسهم فكيف بما هو مِنْ نوع البلاغةِ التي هي كلامهم وتَدِبُّ فيهم دبيباً ، حتى إنَّهم بها في كلِّ وادٍ يهيمونَ ؟!
The miracle of the Noble Qur'an, and the greatest of them is the Mighty Qur'an, whose verses continue to strike the ears of the eloquent by refuting every religion other than Islam, and it moves, by demanding opposition in a way of incapacitation, the zeal of the sharp-witted masters of speech, the strong in rhetoric, prose and poetry, those who delve into every art of eloquence extensively and broadly; such that not even the most guarded word escapes their opposition, even if they are not presented with their incapacity. How (then) when they hear in their incapacitation the explicit words of Allah Almighty: 'Then bring ten surahs like it, invented' [Hud 13]; then He condescended with them and said: 'Then bring a surah like it' [al-Baqara 23]; then He declared the incapacity of all: their humans and jinn, individually or collectively, saying: 'Say: If mankind and jinn gathered to produce the like of this Qur'an, they could not produce the like of it, even if they were helpers of one another' [al-Isra' 88]. Yet despite that, their noses did not twitch, though they were naturally inclined to that? And it is their habit that they cannot control themselves when faced with the slightest challenge that impugns their positions, even if it means their destruction. So how (do they react) with what is of the type of eloquence which is their speech and permeates them deeply, so that they wander in every valley (of it)?!
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the miraculous nature of the Qur'an by highlighting the escalating challenge and the inexplicable silence of the Arabs, who were masters of eloquence. The author begins by stating that the greatest miracle is the Qur'an, which constantly refutes non-Islamic religions and challenges eloquent Arabs to produce something similar. The challenge is presented in three stages: first to produce ten invented surahs, then one surah, and finally a declaration that all humans and jinn together cannot produce its like. Despite their pride and habit of reacting aggressively to any slight against their honor, the Arabs did not respond to this challenge to their own field of excellence. Their silence is itself a proof of the Qur'an's divine origin, as it defies their natural inclinations.

Key Terms
معجزة
القرآن
التحدي
المعارضة
الإعجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic challenge was only for the Arabs of the Prophet's time.
Correction: The verse from al-Isra' includes all humans and jinn, making the challenge perpetual.

Checkpoint
What are the three stages of the Qur'anic challenge to produce something like it?
A) Ten surahs, then one surah, then a declaration of collective inability
B) One surah, then ten surahs, then a hadith
C) A single verse, then a surah, then a book
D) No challenge; it was only a test for believers

Key Takeaway
The escalating challenge and the Arabs' silence prove that the Quran is the speech of Allah and beyond human capability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Arabs' silence in the face of the Quran's challenge is proof of its divine origin. What is your silence about the Quran? If you have not responded to its call, what does that say about your recognition of its miraculous nature? Will you let this lesson's truth move you from silence to submission today?
?
Sanusi highlights that the Arabs would defend their honor at all costs, yet they did not respond to the Quran's challenge. Think of something you are proud of—your intellect, your skill, your status. Would you be silent if your proudest ability was challenged in public? What does your comfort with the Quran's claim say about your spiritual state compared to the Arabs?
?
The Quran reduced its challenge to a single surah, yet the Arabs could not produce even one. You claim to believe the Quran is Allah's word. How does that belief shape your daily life? If you truly believed that no human or jinn can match its eloquence, would you not be more eager to recite it, study it, and live by it? What is stopping you?

Why the Arabs Failed: Inability or Sarfa
Sanusi enumerates two opinions on the Arabs' failure and illustrates with Musaylima.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two explanations for why the Arabs could not oppose the Quran and why the inability explanation is considered more correct.

Core Idea
The Arabs were silenced by the Quran's divine nature — not just by a lack of desire.

Sanusi addresses a key question in the proof of prophethood: Why did the eloquent Arabs fail to produce anything like the Quran? He presents two views — inability and sarfa — and endorses one. The story of Musaylima illustrates the futility of challenging divine revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very concise style here.
2
Identify the two opinions
The passage presents two reasons why the Arabs were silenced: inability (al-'ajz) and sarfa. Find each one and note how the author describes them.
3
Notice the author's preference
The author explicitly states which opinion is 'more correct' (الأصح). Understand why inability is preferred over sarfa based on the context.
4
Consider the example of Musaylima
Read about Musaylima's attempt and its outcome. Reflect on how this example reinforces the author's point about the impossibility of opposing divine revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنَّ القومَ أخرسَهم أنَّهم أحسُّوا أنَّ الأمر إلهي لا تمكن مقاومتُهُ ؛ إمَّا لأنَّهُ ليس في طوقهم وهو الأصح ، أو للصَّرْفة ، وهما قولان ومَنْ لم يستحْيِ منهم ، وانْتَدَبَ لمقاومة هذا الأمر الإلهي ؛ كمُسَيْلِمَةَ افْتَضَحَ ، وأتى بمَخرَقةٍ يُتضاحَكُ منها إلى قيام الساعة
But the people were silenced because they sensed that the matter is divine, impossible to resist; either because it was beyond their capacity—and that is the correct opinion—or due to sarfa (the turning away [of their attempts]), and these are two views. And whoever among them had no shame and set out to resist this divine matter, like Musaylima, was disgraced and produced something ridiculous that will be laughed at until the Day of Resurrection.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage presents the explanation for the Arabs' failure to oppose the Qur'an despite their eloquence. The author states that they were silenced because they perceived the Qur'anic challenge as a divine matter that could not be resisted. Two opinions are given: first, that it was simply beyond their power to produce anything like it—which is deemed the more correct view (الأصح); second, that they were prevented by 'sarfa', meaning that Allah turned away their attempts or desire to compete. The author then illustrates with the example of Musaylima the Liar, who, lacking shame, attempted to challenge the Qur'an but only produced laughable nonsense, serving as a warning.

Key Terms
الصرفة
العجز
المسايلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think sarfa is the mainstream explanation for the Arabs' failure.
Correction: The author explicitly states that inability (al-'ajz) is the more correct opinion, not sarfa.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the more correct explanation for why the Arabs did not produce something like the Qur'an?
A) It was beyond their capacity
B) Allah turned away their efforts (sarfa)
C) They did not want to
D) They were not given enough time

Key Takeaway
The Arabs' failure to oppose the Quran was due to their genuine inability, not merely divine prevention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'an, do you feel the same awe that silenced the Arabs—a sense that this is a divine matter not to be resisted? Or have you become so familiar with its words that you treat it like any other book? What would change in your heart if you truly believed it is beyond any human capacity to produce?
?
Musaylima had no shame and tried to compete with the Qur'an, only to produce something laughable. In what areas of your life are you 'competing' with Allah's guidance—preferring your own opinions, desires, or worldly standards over the clear word of Allah? Will you humble yourself today and accept that the divine matter cannot be resisted?
?
The correct view is that the Arabs were genuinely unable to imitate the Qur'an, not that Allah simply turned away their attempts. Does your belief in the Qur'an's inimitability lead you to submit to it unquestioningly, or do you still hesitate to obey certain commands? What is one command you have been resisting, and will you now surrender to its divine authority?

The Tawatur and Enduring Miracle of the Quran
Sanusi argues for the Quran's widespread transmission and additional proofs of its divine origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's mass transmission and its internal evidences combine to prove its divine origin, and why these arguments are decisive for the rational person.

Core Idea
The Quran's transmission is so widespread that no rational person can deny it, and its content proves it is from Allah.

Sanusi now turns to the Quran itself as a proof of prophethood. He argues that its transmission is unlike any other book: it has been spread across the entire earth by countless people for nearly a millennium, leaving no excuse for doubt. Moreover, its content—accurate prophecies and comprehensive teachings—confirms that it could only come from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the emphatic 'No!' (كلا) that introduces the contrast with isolated reports.
The passage builds to a rhetorical question that summarizes the argument.
2
Identify the two main proofs
Look for the external proof (tawatur: mass transmission across all regions and types of people) and the internal proofs (prophecies and the comprehensive Sharia sciences). How do they work together?
The external proof eliminates doubt, while the internal proofs add positive evidence of divine origin.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical force
Consider why Sanusi mentions 'nine hundred years' and includes jinn and humans. This shows the transmission is complete and undeniable. Then ask: Why does he add the internal proofs after already declaring no doubt?
He is building a cumulative case: first overwhelming external evidence, then additional internal miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أنَّهم نُقِلَ إليهمُ القرآنُ نقل غيرِهِ مِنَ الكلام نقل آحاد .. لأمكن الاعتذارُ عنهم بعدم الوصول ، كلا بل امتلاتْ بحَمَلَتِهِ وصُحُفِهِ وإشادة أمرِهِ الأَرضُ كلُّها ؛ سهلها وجبلها بدوها وحضرها ، برُّها وبحرها، مؤمنُها وكافرها ، جِنُّها وإنسُها ، وتطاولَتْ أزمنته على تلك الصفة قريباً مِنْ تسع مئة سنةٍ ، أفيستريب عاقل بعد هذا في كونه مِنْ  ِعندِ اللهِ جَلَّ وعلا صدَّقَ بهِ نبيَّه صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ؟! هذا معَ ما فيهِ مِنَ الإخبار قبل الوقوع بالغيوب المطابقة ، ومحاسنِ علوم الشريعة المشتملة على ما لا يَقدِرُ البشر على ضبطِهِ ؛ مِنَ المصالح الدنيوية والأخروية
And if the Qur'an had been transmitted to them by the transmission of isolated reports like other speech, then an excuse on their part due to non-receipt would have been possible. No! Rather, the earth, with its plains and mountains, its Bedouins and townspeople, its land and sea, its believer and disbeliever, its jinn and humans, has been filled with its bearers, its copies, and the elevation of its affair. And its times have been extended in that manner for nearly nine hundred years. Can any rational person then doubt that it is from Allah, the Mighty and Exalted, by which He confirmed His Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace? This, along with what is in it of news of unseen things before their occurrence, matching reality, and the excellent sciences of the Sharia that contain what humans cannot fully encompass: of worldly and otherworldly benefits.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage provides additional proofs for the divine origin of the Qur'an. The author argues that the Qur'an was not transmitted as isolated reports (āhād) that could be doubted or rejected on grounds of non-receipt. Rather, it was transmitted with mass frequency (tawātur) across the entire earth, reaching all types of people—city and desert, land and sea, believer and unbeliever, human and jinn—for nearly nine hundred years. This overwhelming transmission leaves no room for doubt. The author then adds two further internal evidences: the occurrence of prophecies before their events (like the conquests), and the comprehensive sciences of the Sharia that contain benefits for this world and the next, beyond human capacity to produce. Thus, the Qur'an's miracle is both in its transmission and its content.

Key Terms
تواتر
إخبار بالغيب
علوم الشريعة
مصالح دنيوية وأخروية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Qur'an's miraculousness is only in its eloquence.
Correction: The author emphasizes its tawātur (mass transmission) and accurate prophecies as additional proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would have been a valid excuse for the Arabs if the Quran had been transmitted in isolated reports?
A) The eloquence of the Quran
B) The lack of collective transmission
C) The length of time
D) The number of surahs

Key Takeaway
The Quran's divine origin is proven both by its unparalleled mass transmission (tawatur) and by its internal content of accurate prophecies and comprehensive guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have heard the claim that the Qur'an is from Allah. After reading about its tawatur, can you still find any rational excuse to avoid accepting it fully? What specifics in your life do you need to change to live by its teachings?
?
The passage mentions that the Qur'an contains accurate prophecies and comprehensive guidance. Reflect on one prophecy or teaching in the Qur'an that you have personally seen to be true. How does that strengthen your conviction?
?
If the Qur'an is transmitted to all types of people, including disbelievers and jinn, what excuse do you have for not sharing its message with others? Whom in your life have you neglected to inform?

Signs, Miracles, and the Quran's I'jaz
Sanusi defines aya and mu'jiza, states the Quran as the greatest miracle, and lists differing views on its i'jaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between a sign (āya) and a miracle (muʿjiza) and the differing scholarly views on the miraculous nature of the Quran.

Core Idea
Not all signs are miracles; a miracle requires a challenge.

This passage from Sanusi establishes key terms in the study of prophethood: 'āya' and 'muʿjiza'. It then presents the Quran as the greatest miracle and summarizes the debates about what makes it miraculous. Understanding these distinctions is essential for engaging with later arguments about prophetic proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the definitions
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to how Sanusi defines 'āya' and 'muʿjiza' and the condition he attaches to the latter.
The footnote gives context about why the Quran's miracle is especially suited to its audience.
2
Identify the condition that distinguishes a muʿjiza
Find the phrase that specifies the condition for a miracle. Ask yourself: why is this condition important for proving prophethood?
3
Recognize the two views on the Quran's iʿjāz
The passage lists two opinions about the miraculous aspect of the Quran. Note which groups held these views and what specific features they emphasized.
Sanusi does not endorse a view here; he reports the disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أنَّ لنبينا ومولانا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ آيَاتٍ ومعجزاتِ كثيرةً ، لا حصر لها ، والفرقُ بينَ الآيةِ والمعجزة : أنَّ الآية تدلُّ على صحة ما جاءَ بهِ وإن لم يتحدَّ بها، والمعجزة مشروطة مع ذلك بالتحدّي . ومعجزته العظمى التي تحدى بها على الكافة : القرآن(۱) ، وقدِ اجتمع المسلمون قاطبةً على أنَّهُ مُعجز واختلفوا في تعيين الوجْهِ المُعجِزِ الذي تحدّى بهِ منه وإن اشتمل على وجوهِ مِنَ الإعجاز : فقال بعضُ المعتزلة : إعجازه أسلوبه ونظمه الخاص فقط وقال قوم : إعجازه فصاحته وجزالته فقط . (۱) لأن الغالب على القوم الذين نشأ فيهم عليه الصلاة والسلام . . هو الفصاحةُ والبلاغة ، كذا نبه عليه العلامة المنجور في ( حاشيته ) ( ص ٢٢٤ )
Know that our prophet and master Muḥammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, has many signs (āyāt) and miracles (muʿjizāt), without limit. And the difference between the āya and the muʿjiza is that the āya indicates the correctness of what he brought, even if it is not accompanied by a challenge (taḥaddī), while the muʿjiza is conditioned in addition to that by the challenge. And his greatest miracle with which he challenged all people is the Qur'an. The Muslims have universally agreed that it is a miracle. And they differed in specifying the miraculous aspect (al-wajh al-muʿjiz) with which he challenged from within it, although it includes multiple aspects of iʿjāz. Some Muʿtazila said: Its iʿjāz is only its style (uslūb) and its unique composition (naẓm). And a group said: Its iʿjāz is only its eloquence (faṣāḥa) and conciseness (jazāla). (1) Because the predominant trait among the people among whom he, peace and blessings be upon him, was raised is eloquence (faṣāḥa) and rhetoric (balāgha). The scholar al-Manjūr pointed this out in his Ḥāshiya (p. 224).
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a classification. Sanusi first asserts that the Prophet has countless āyāt (signs) and muʿjizāt (miracles). He then defines the difference: an āya is a sign of the truth of his message even if not accompanied by a challenge (taḥaddī), while a muʿjiza specifically includes a challenge to produce something similar. The Qur'an is his greatest miracle and is universally agreed upon by Muslims as a miracle (muʿjiz). However, scholars differed on the precise miraculous aspect (wajh al-iʿjāz) by which the challenge was issued, even though the Qur'an contains multiple aspects. Sanusi then names two opinions: some Muʿtazila held that its iʿjāz lies only in its unique style (uslūb) and composition (naẓm); another group held it is only its eloquence (faṣāḥa) and conciseness/rhythmic power (jazāla). A footnote adds that the Arabs, among whom the Prophet was raised, were known for eloquence and rhetoric, as noted by al-Manjūr.

Key Terms
آية
معجزة
تحدي
إعجاز
أسلوب
نظم
فصاحة
جزالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All miracles require a challenge (taḥaddī).
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that an āya does not require a challenge; only a muʿjiza does. Thus, not every prophetic sign is a miracle in the technical sense.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between an āya and a muʿjiza according to Sanusi?
A) A muʿjiza is greater than an āya.
B) A muʿjiza is specifically associated with a challenge (taḥaddī), while an āya is not.
C) An āya is found only in the Quran, while a muʿjiza can be other things.
D) There is no difference; they are synonyms.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi distinguishes signs from miracles by the presence of a challenge, and highlights the Quran as the supreme miracle with differing interpretations of its miraculous quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi says the Quran is the greatest miracle with which Allah challenged all people. Have you ever personally felt that challenge? Has the Quran ever confronted you in a way that demanded a response, or have you treated it as just another book of guidance? What will you do today to stand under its challenge and let it stir your heart?
?
The Arabs were masters of eloquence, yet they could not produce anything like the Quran. You may not be an Arab or a poet, but are there areas in your life—your pride, your intellect, your desires—where you still resist the Quran's authority? Where is your own 'challenge' to the Quran hiding? Name one such area and commit to surrendering it this week.
?
The Muslims agree on the miracle of the Quran but differ about how it is miraculous. You have heard the Quran recited many times. Has its miraculous nature ever caused you to stop, reflect, and change your course? If not, what does that say about your engagement with it? Let this lesson be a turning point: before you sleep tonight, read one verse with the conviction that it is impossible for any human to produce, and ask Allah to make it real in your life.

The Unlettered Prophet as a Proof
Sanusi presents the Prophet's unlettered state as miraculous evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Prophet's unlettered nature serves as a proof of his prophethood and the divine origin of his message.

Core Idea
The Prophet's unlettered state makes his profound knowledge a clear miracle.

Sanusi begins by highlighting the extraordinary outcomes of the Prophet's mission: decisive proofs, refutation of opponents, and deep spiritual teachings. He then emphasizes that all this came through an unlettered man who never studied a book or sat with scholars—a fact confirmed by the Quran itself. This passage establishes the miraculous foundation upon which the discussion of miracles rests.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Notice the list of benefits before the key point about the Prophet's unlettered state.
The verse at the end is a direct Quranic proof.
2
Identify the core argument
Sanusi argues that the Prophet's achievements were impossible for an unlettered person. Find the phrase that expresses this impossibility.
Look for 'علم ذلك كله بالضرورة' and the verse.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why is the Prophet's illiteracy important? How does it connect to the idea of miracles? This sets the stage for the next lesson.
This is a foundational point for understanding prophethood.

Source Text

وتحرير الأدلة، والرد على المخالفين بالبراهين القطعية وسرد قصص الماضين، وتزكية النفس بمواعظ يغرق في أدنى بحارها جميع وعظ الواعظين. هذا كله على يد نبي أمي لم يلحظ قط كتاباً، ولا حصلت قط له مخالطة لذي علم ما يمكن بها تحصيل أدنى شيء من ذلك، علم ذلك كله بالضرورة، ﴿ وَمَا كُنتَ تَتْلُوا مِن قَبْلِهِ مِن كِتَابٍ وَلَا تَخُطُّهُ بِيَمِينِكَ إِذًا لَّارْتَابَ الْمُبْطِلُونَ ﴾. اعلم أن لنبينا ومولانا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم آيات ومعجزات كثيرة، لا حصر لها، والفرق بين الآية والمعجزة: أن الآية تدل على صحة ما جاء به وإن لم يتحد بها، والمعجزة مشروطة مع ذلك بالتحدي. ومعجزته العظمى التي تحدى بها على الكافة: القرآن، وقد اجتمع المسلمون قاطبة على أنه معجز. واختلفوا في تعيين الوجه المعجز الذي تحدى به منه وإن اشتمل على وجوه من الإعجاز: فقال بعض المعتزلة: إعجازه أسلوبه ونظمه الخاص فقط. وقال قوم: إعجازه فصاحته وجزالته فقط. وقال إمام الحرمين والقاضي: إعجازه بالمجموع. وقال قوم: إعجازه بالصرف عن معارضته وإن كان في مقدورهم وهو قول لأبي الحسن الأشعري، وهو قول النظام من المعتزلة. قال النظام: كانت العرب تقدر على النطق بمثله قبل مبعثه عليه الصلاة والسلام، فلما بعث سلبوا هذه القدرة. وقال قوم: إعجازه في جملته، عدم مناقضته في آياته، وتصديق بعضها بعضاً. وقال قوم: إعجازه إنباؤه عن المغيبات فيما مضى وفيما هو آت. وقال قوم: إعجازه موافقته لقضايا العقول.
And the editing of proofs, and the refutation of opponents with decisive evidences and the narration of stories of the past, and the purification of the soul with exhortations in the shallowest seas of which all the exhortations of exhorters drown. All of this is at the hand of an unlettered prophet who never once looked at a book, nor did he ever have any association with a person of knowledge by which the slightest thing of that could be acquired; all of that is known necessarily. Allah says: 'And you did not recite before it any book, nor did you inscribe it with your right hand, for then the falsifiers would have doubted.' Know that our Prophet and master Muhammad (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him) has many signs and miracles, beyond count. The difference between a sign (aya) and a miracle (mu'jiza) is that the sign indicates the truth of what he brought even if it is not accompanied by a challenge, while the miracle is conditioned additionally by a challenge. His greatest miracle with which he challenged all people is the Qur'an, and all Muslims have unanimously agreed that it is miraculous. They differed in specifying the miraculous aspect (al-wajh al-mu'jiz) by which he challenged from it, although it comprises aspects of miraculousness: Some Mu'tazila said: Its miraculousness is its style and its particular composition only. Some people said: Its miraculousness is its eloquence and its fluency only. Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi said: Its miraculousness is by the totality. Some people said: Its miraculousness is by the turning away (al-sarf) from opposing it, even though it was within their capability; and this is a saying of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and it is the saying of al-Nazzam from the Mu'tazila. Al-Nazzam said: The Arabs were able to utter something like it before his mission (peace be upon him and blessings), but when he was sent, they were deprived of this ability. Some people said: Its miraculousness is in its entirety: the absence of contradiction in its verses, and the confirmation of some of them by others. Some people said: Its miraculousness is its informing of the unseen, both past and future. Some people said: Its miraculousness is its agreement with the dictates of intellects.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage is a combined introduction and enumeration. It first establishes the extraordinary nature of the Prophet being unlettered, then defines the key terms 'aya' (sign) and 'mu'jiza' (miracle), and finally lists the various scholarly opinions on the precise nature of the Quran's miraculousness (i'jaz).

The passage begins by describing the benefits of the Prophet's mission: establishing proofs, refuting opponents, and spiritual purification. It emphasizes that all this was accomplished through an unlettered prophet who never studied a book nor associated with scholars, a fact known necessarily and confirmed by Quran 29:48.

Next, it states that the Prophet had many signs and miracles, but the greatest is the Qur'an, on which Muslims unanimously agree. However, they differed on the specific aspect that makes it miraculous. The author then enumerates six opinions:

1. Some Mu'tazila: The miraculousness is in its unique style (uslub) and composition (nazm).
2. Some others: Only in its eloquence (fasaha) and fluency (jazala).
3. Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi: In the combination of these qualities.
4. Some, including Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and al-Nazzam from the Mu'tazila: In the 'sarf' or turning away—the Arabs were capable of producing similar speech but were prevented from doing so after the Prophet's mission.
5. Some: In its lack of contradiction and internal consistency.
6. Some: In its reports of the unseen.
7. Some: In its agreement with rational principles.

The passage thus presents a range of views without deciding in favor of one, leaving the matter open.

Key Terms
نبي أمي
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Prophet could read or write or had studied with scholars.
Correction: The passage establishes that he was unlettered and never studied, making his knowledge a miracle.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is it known that the Prophet did not acquire his knowledge from human sources?
A) It is known by necessity (bi al-darura).
B) It is known through historical reports.
C) It is known by logical inference alone.
D) It is known only through the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's unlettered state, combined with his profound teachings, serves as a clear proof of the divine origin of his message.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet, though untaught by any human, brought the most profound knowledge. In your own life, do you prioritize seeking knowledge from the Quran and Sunnah over worldly sources? What one change can you make today to turn more toward divine guidance?
?
If the Prophet's unlettered state is a miracle that testifies to the truth of his message, how does that challenge the way you approach the Quran? Do you read it as the Word of God or as just another book? Commit to reading even one verse reflectively every day this week.
?
The passage says the Prophet's exhortations 'drown' all other exhortations. What does that say about the depth of Islamic teachings? Are you satisfied with shallow religiosity, or will you dive deeper into the ocean of the Prophet's guidance starting right now?

Miracles
beginner
4 min
Defining Aya and Mu'jiza
Sanusi defines aya and mu'jiza, and states the Quran's miraculous status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical difference between a sign (aya) and a miracle (mu'jiza), and why the Quran is considered the greatest miracle with universal Muslim consensus.

Core Idea
A miracle is a sign accompanied by a challenge; the Quran is the supreme miracle.

Having set the stage with the Prophet's unlettered state, Sanusi now defines the key terms used in discussing miracles. He distinguishes between a general 'sign' and a specific 'miracle' that involves a challenge. Then he declares the Quran as the Prophet's greatest miracle and notes the unanimous agreement of Muslims on its miraculous nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and then the translation. Notice the two definitions and the concluding statement.
2
Compare aya and mu'jiza
Identify the one difference: the presence of a challenge (tahaddi). A sign indicates truth even without a challenge; a miracle requires a challenge.
This distinction is crucial in kalam discussions.
3
Understand the Quran's status
Sanusi says the Quran is the greatest miracle and that all Muslims agree on its miraculous nature. Note that the consensus is on its i'jaz, not on the specific aspect.
This consensus forms the basis for the differing opinions in the next lesson.

What additional condition distinguishes a miracle (mu'jiza) from a sign (aya)?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Qur'an is the greatest miracle, do you truly engage with it as a challenge that demands a response from you? What prevents you from taking up that challenge today?
?
