?
All Muslims agree the Qur'an is miraculous—yet does your daily treatment of the Qur'an reflect that you are among those who agree? If not, what concrete step will you take tonight to honor it as the miracle it is?

Miracles

Opinions on the I'jaz of the Quran
Sanusi enumerates seven opinions on the specific aspect of the Quran's miraculousness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various scholarly opinions on the specific aspect that makes the Quran miraculous.

Core Idea
Scholars agree the Quran is miraculous, but disagree on the precise reason.

After establishing the Quran's miraculous status, Sanusi presents the diversity of scholarly opinion on exactly what constitutes its miraculousness. He lists six distinct views, ranging from its style and eloquence to the prevention of imitation (sarf) and its agreement with reason. This lesson surveys those opinions without settling on one, showing the richness of Islamic theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice that Sanusi lists multiple opinions, each introduced by 'وقال'.
This is an enumeration, so pay attention to each view.
2
Identify each opinion
List the six distinct views: (1) style and composition only, (2) eloquence and fluency only, (3) the totality, (4) sarf (prevention), (5) internal consistency, (6) reports of the unseen, (7) agreement with reason.
Some sources combine the last three. The passage includes seven opinions but some overlap.
3
Understand the sarf theory
Note the view of al-Nazzam and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari: that the Arabs were capable of imitating the Quran but were prevented from doing so after the Prophet's mission.
This is a distinct and controversial opinion.
4
Appreciate the diversity
Reflect on why there are so many views. This shows the depth of reflection on the Quran's miraculous nature.
Sanusi does not favor one opinion here; he simply reports them.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which scholar or school held the view that the Quran's miraculousness is due to 'al-sarf' (prevention)?
A) Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi
B) Some Mu'tazila
C) Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and al-Nazzam
D) Those who said 'informing of the unseen'

Scholars have proposed at least seven different opinions on the specific aspect of the Quran's miraculousness, reflecting deep reflection on its inimitability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opinion that the Quran's miracle lies in its consistency (lack of contradiction) forces you to ask: If the Quran is internally consistent, why is your life often inconsistent with its commands? Where today did your actions contradict a Quranic principle you claim to believe?
?
The opinion that the Quran's i'jaz includes its agreement with reason ('aql) challenges those who think faith and reason are separate. In what area of your life are you afraid to let the Quran's rational arguments confront your comfortable assumptions?
?
Al-Nazzam believed God prevented the Arabs from imitating the Quran after the Prophet's mission. If God can prevent people from producing speech like the Quran, what does that say about your own ability to truly comprehend and live by it without His help? Where do you need to beg for His assistance to understand?

Miracles
intermediate
10 min
Opinions on the Inimitability of the Quran and Refutation of Sarfa
Sanusi enumerates opinions on i'jaz, selects best, and refutes sarfa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different scholarly opinions on the nature of the Quran's inimitability (i'jaz), why the combination of eloquence and unique style is the strongest view, and the refutation of the sarfa theory.

Core Idea
The Quran's inimitability lies in its unique blend of eloquence and style, not mere prevention.

Scholars have debated what makes the Quran inimitable. In this passage, Sanusi systematically lists the main opinions, selects the one he considers strongest, and then refutes the theory of sarfa. Understanding these positions is crucial for appreciating the miracle of the Quran and the theological reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the full range of opinions and arguments. Pay attention to the flow from enumeration to evaluation.
The passage is dense with different views; take notes.
2
Identify the eight opinions
List the eight different opinions on i'jaz mentioned by Sanusi. Notice how each is introduced with 'wa qala qawm' or similar.
Some opinions are attributed to specific scholars.
3
Understand the best opinion
Focus on how Sanusi declares the view of al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn as the best. Note the reasoning: the challenge involves a surah with both jasala and uslub khas.
Compare this to the examples of poet and sermon.
4
Analyze the refutation of sarfa
Examine the two arguments Sanusi gives against the sarfa theory: lack of historical transmission and inconsistency with the Quran's high eloquence.
Consider why these are powerful objections.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Connect the passage to the broader doctrine of miracles and the Quran's unique status. Ask yourself why this debate matters for Islamic theology.

وقال إمام الحرمين والقاضي : إعجازه بالمجموع . وقال قوم : إعجازه بالصَّرفِ عن معارضتِهِ وإِنْ كانَ في مقدورهم وهو قول لأبي الحسن الأشعري ، وهو قول النظام مِنَ المعتزلة . قالَ النظّامُ : كانَتِ العربُ تَقدِرُ على النطقِ بمثلِهِ قبل مبعثِهِ عليهِ الصلاةُ والسلام ، فلمَّا بُعِثَ سُلبوا هذه القدرة . وقال قوم : إعجازه في جملتِهِ ، عدم مناقضته في آياتِهِ ، وتصديقِ بعضها بعضاً . وقال قوم : إعجازُهُ إنباؤُهُ عنِ المغيَّباتِ فيما مضى وفيما هو آت . وقال قوم : إعجازه موافقته لقضايا العقول . وقال بعضُ المُحدثين : إعجازه أنَّه قديم . وقال قوم : إعجازُهُ أَنَّهُ عبارةٌ عنِ الكلام القديمِ . وأحسنُ هذه الأقوالِ : القولُ الذي اختاره القاضي وإمامُ الحرمين ؛ فإنَّهُ عليهِ الصلاةُ والسلام تحدَّى بسورة مِنَ القرآن ، وهي مشتملة على الأمرين معاً ؛ الجزالة والأسلوب الخاص ، وإِنَّما يتحقَّقُ الإتيان بمثلها عندَ الإتيان بالمشتمل على الوجهين معاً ، فإنَّ الشاعر المُفْلِقَ إذا سرد قصيدةً بليغةً ، ودعا إلى المعارضة بمثلها ، فعُورِضَ بخُطبة بليغةٍ أو نثر مرسل بالغ أقصى الفصاحة.. لم يكن الآتي بذلك معارضاً لها ، ولو أتى شاعر بمثل وزنِ شعره عارياً عن فصاحتِهِ وجزالته . . لم يكن معارضاً له ، وهو نظير معارضةِ مُسَيْلِمَةَ الكَذَّابِ له بتُرَّهَاتِهِ التي يُتضاحَكُ منها . وأمَّا مَنْ ذهب إلى أنَّ إعجازَهُ بالصَّرْفِةِ : فقد سبق التنبيه على ضَعفِهِ ؛ فإنَّهُ لو كانَ لَنُقِلَ شيء عنهم مِنْ ذلك فيما مضى ، ولو نُقِلَ لوُجِدَ ؛ فإِنَّهُ ممَّا تتوفَّرُ الدواعي على نقلِهِ ، وأيضاً : فلو كانَ إعجازه بالصَّرْفِةِ لكانَ كونه في أدنى مراتب الفصاحة أنسب ؛ لظهور إعجازه ، كيف ولا خلاف أنَّهُ في أعلى مراتبِ البلاغةِ ؟!
And the Imam al-Haramayn and the Qadi said: Its inimitability is by the totality. And a group said: Its inimitability is by turning away from opposing it, even if it were within their ability, and this is a saying of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and it is the saying of al-Nazzam from the Mu'tazila. Al-Nazzam said: The Arabs were able to speak something like it before his mission (peace and blessings be upon him), but when he was sent, they were stripped of this ability. And a group said: Its inimitability is in its entirety, the lack of contradiction in its verses, and the confirmation of some of them by others. And a group said: Its inimitability is its informing of unseen things in the past and the future. And a group said: Its inimitability is its agreement with the dictates of the intellects. And some of the later scholars said: Its inimitability is that it is eternal. And a group said: Its inimitability is that it is an expression of the eternal speech. And the best of these sayings is the saying chosen by the Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn; for he (peace and blessings be upon him) challenged with a surah of the Quran, and it comprises both matters: the eloquence (jasala) and the unique style (al-uslub al-khas). And indeed, producing something like it is only realized when one produces something containing both aspects together. For if a brilliant poet recites a eloquent poem and calls for opposition with something like it, and he is opposed with an eloquent sermon or unrhymed prose reaching the utmost eloquence, the one who brought that would not be an opponent to it. And if a poet brought something like the meter of his poem but devoid of its eloquence (fasaha) and brilliance (jasala), he would not be an opponent to it. And this is analogous to the opposition of Musaylima the liar to it with his absurdities which are laughed at. As for the one who held that its inimitability is by the turning away (sarfa): the warning of its weakness has already preceded. For if it were, something of that would have been transmitted from them in the past, and if transmitted it would have been found; for motives for transmitting it would be abundant. Also, if its inimitability were by sarfa, its being at the lowest ranks of eloquence would be more appropriate for the manifestation of its inimitability; how [could it be] when there is no dispute that it is at the highest ranks of eloquence?!
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage classifies the various opinions (aqwal) on the nature of the Quran's inimitability (i'jaz), then selects the best opinion, and finally refutes the theory of sarfa (prevention). The author (Sanusi) presents eight opinions in sequence. First: the view of the Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar and Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni that i'jaz lies in the 'totality' (bi al-majmu'), meaning the combination of eloquence and unique style. Second: the opinion of some (including a position attributed to al-Ash'ari and al-Nazzam) that i'jaz is by 'turning away' (sarfa)—i.e., Allah prevented the Arabs from producing a similar text even though they were capable. The author notes that al-Nazzam claimed the Arabs could do so before the Prophet's mission but were then prevented. Third: i'jaz lies in the entire Quran's coherence—absence of contradiction and mutual confirmation of verses. Fourth: i'jaz in predicting the unseen. Fifth: i'jaz in agreement with reason. Sixth: the opinion of some later scholars that i'jaz is the Quran's pre-eternality. Seventh: that i'jaz is its being an expression of the eternal divine speech. After listing, the author declares the best opinion to be that of the Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn: i'jaz is the combination of eloquence (jasala/fasaha) and a unique style (uslub khas) that is beyond human capacity. He argues that the Prophet challenged with a single surah, which contains both aspects. To produce a true imitation, one must match both qualities. He gives examples: a poet who produces a eloquent poem cannot be opposed by a prose work even if eloquent, nor by a poem that matches meter but lacks eloquence. True opposition requires both. He cites Musaylima's laughable attempts as proof. Finally, he refutes the sarfa theory with two arguments: (1) if Allah had prevented the Arabs, this prevention would have been widely reported, but no such reports exist; (2) if i'jaz were merely prevention, lower eloquence would be more appropriate for showing the miracle, but the Quran is unanimously acknowledged to be at the highest level of eloquence, which contradicts the sarfa theory.

صرفة
جزالة
أسلوب خاص
الفصاحة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the sarfa theory (prevention) is a valid explanation for the Quran's inimitability.
Correction: Sanusi refutes sarfa by arguing that if prevention occurred, it would have been widely transmitted, and that a lower quality text would have been more suitable for manifesting the miracle.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the opinion of al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn the best?
A) Because it is the only opinion based on reason.
B) Because the Prophet challenged with a surah that contains both eloquence and unique style.
C) Because it is the opinion of the majority of scholars.
D) Because it refutes the sarfa theory.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is best understood as the unique combination of unmatched eloquence and a distinctive style that no human can replicate, as argued by al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn, and the theory of mere prevention (sarfa) is refuted by historical and rational arguments.

When you recite or listen to the Quran, do you actively ponder its linguistic and stylistic beauty, or do you treat it as mere ritual words? What specific change will you make this week to engage with its language in a way that stirs your heart and mind?
?
The refutation of sarfa shows that if Allah merely prevented imitation, the Quran's high eloquence would be unnecessary. Does that challenge you to value the actual words of the Quran more deeply? Are you guilty of treating the Quran as a talisman rather than a living challenge to your intellect and soul? Commit to reading one passage this week with the intention of recognizing its unique style and eloquence.

The author presents several opinions on what makes the Quran inimitable. Among them are claims about its coherence, prophecies, and agreement with reason. Four Alternative Views on the Quran's Miracle
The author examines four opinions on the nature of the Quran's i'jaz and shows they are not the basis of the challenge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quran's miraculousness (i'jaz) is specifically based on its eloquence (balāgha) and not on other proposed qualities like coherence, prophecy, harmony with reason, or eternity.

Core Idea
The Quran's miracle is its unique eloquence, not its other praiseworthy qualities.

Sanusi now examines four alternative explanations for the Quran's miraculous nature that some have proposed. While acknowledging that each describes a genuine quality of the Quran, he argues that none of them is the actual basis of the challenge (tahaddi) that defines its miracle. This passage clarifies why the miracle is specifically tied to eloquence and not to these other features.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and list the four opinions
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Make a list of the four opinions Sanusi discusses.
Each opinion is introduced with 'wa-ammā man qāla'.
2
Note the author's response to each opinion
For each opinion, identify what Sanusi concedes (e.g., 'we do not deny') and what he denies (e.g., 'the challenge did not occur by that').
The first three opinions receive a concession; the fourth is refuted outright.
3
Observe the pattern
Notice that for the first three opinions, Sanusi accepts the quality but says it is not the basis of the challenge. For the fourth (eternity), he gives a detailed refutation.
The refutation distinguishes between the eternal meaning and contingent expressions.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters. How does it protect the doctrine that the Quran's miracle is its inimitability (i'jaz) in eloquence?
The challenge (tahaddi) was specifically to produce a surah like it, which tests eloquence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأَمَّا مَنْ قالَ : إعجازه في جملتِهِ ؛ بعدم التناقض فيه على طولِهِ وتصديق بعضه بعضاً : فلا ننكرُ أنَّ ما ذكرَهُ مِنْ أعظمِ دليل على أَنَّهُ مِنْ لدن حكيم عليم ، ولذا وصفَهُ تعالى بأنَّهُ : لَا يَأْتِيهِ الْبَاطِلُ مِنْ بَيْنِ يَدَيْهِ وَلَا مِنْ خَلْفِهِ ﴾ [فصلت : ٤٢]، إلا أنَّ التحدي لم يقع بذلكَ
وأمَّا مَنْ قالَ : إعجازه إنباؤُهُ عن المُغيَّبَاتِ : فلا يُنكَرُ أَيضاً اشتماله على ذلكَ ، وأَنَّهُ مِنْ أصدقِ الآياتِ ، إلا أنَّه لم يقع التحدّي بهِ ؛ إذْ لا تحقق له في كلِّ سورة ، والتحدي وقع بمطلق سورة وإن لم تشتمل على المغيّبات
وأَمَّا مَنْ قالَ : إعجازه بموافقتِهِ لقضايا العقول : فلا ننكر أيضاً أنَّ ذلكَ وصفه ، للكنَّ التحدي لم يقع بذلك
وأَمَّا مَنْ قالَ : إعجازه لأنَّهُ قديمٌ : فلا يصح ؛ لأنَّه إن أراد بالقديم ما دلّ عليه . . فقد سبق أنَّ مِنْ شرطِ المعجزةِ أنْ تكونَ فعلاً الله تعالى ، وإن أراد العبارات الدالة .. فلا يخفى أنَّها حادثة . وأَمَّا مَنْ قالَ : إعجازُهُ أنَّهُ عبارةٌ عن الكلام القديم : فلا يصح أيضاً
As for the one who said: His miracle in its entirety; due to the absence of contradiction in it despite its length and the confirmation of some parts by others: we do not deny that what he mentioned is among the greatest proofs that it is from the presence of an All-Wise, All-Knowing, and therefore the Exalted described it as: 'Falsehood cannot approach it from before it or from behind it' [Fussilat 41:42], except that the challenge did not occur by that. And as for the one who said: His miracle is its informing about unseen things: we also do not deny that it includes that, and that it is among the truest signs, except that the challenge did not occur by it; since that is not verified in every surah, and the challenge occurred with any surah even if it does not contain unseen things. And as for the one who said: His miracle is its agreement with the judgments of intellects: we also do not deny that that is its description, but the challenge did not occur by that. And as for the one who said: His miracle is because it is eternal: it is not valid; because if he means by 'eternal' what it indicates, it has preceded that among the conditions of a miracle is that it be an act of Allah the Exalted; and if he means the indicative expressions, it is not hidden that they are contingent. And as for the one who said: His miracle is that it is an expression of the eternal speech: it is also not valid.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage examines and evaluates four alternative opinions on the nature of the Quran's miraculousness (i'jaz). The author acknowledges that each opinion describes a real quality of the Quran, but he argues that none of these qualities is the actual basis of the challenge (tahaddi) that constitutes the miracle. The first opinion says the miracle is the Quran's overall coherence and lack of contradiction. The author agrees this is a great proof that it is from God, but notes the challenge was not made on that basis. The second opinion says the miracle is the Quran's prophecies about the unseen. Again, this is true but not the basis of the challenge, since not every surah contains such prophecies, yet the challenge applies to any surah. The third opinion says the miracle is its harmony with rational truth. The author accepts this as a description but insists the challenge did not occur on that basis. The fourth opinion says the Quran is miraculous because it is eternal (qadim). The author refutes this: if by 'eternal' one means the meaning (i.e., the eternal speech), then a miracle must be an act of Allah, and the eternal speech is not an act; if one means the physical words and letters, they are clearly contingent (haditha). The same refutation applies to the claim that the miracle is its being an expression of the eternal speech: the expression itself is not miraculous. Thus, all four views are either insufficient or invalid as the basis of the challenge.

معجزة
بلاغة
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran's miracle is its coherence, prophecies, or harmony with reason.
Correction: These are genuine qualities but not the basis of the challenge; the miracle is specifically its eloquence (balāgha).

Checkpoint

A) Because the prophecies are not always accurate.
B) Because not every surah contains prophecies, yet the challenge applies to any surah.
C) Because the prophecies were only understood later.
D) Because the challenge was only for the disbelievers of Quraysh.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's miraculousness (i'jaz) is not based on coherence, prophecy, rationality, or eternity, but specifically on its inimitable eloquence (balāgha), which is the object of the challenge (tahaddi).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The opinion that the Quran is miraculous because it is eternal is refuted. Do you ever treat the physical mushaf as if it were eternal? What is the danger of conflating the eternal speech with its temporal expression?

The Miracle of Eloquence and the Required Surah Length
Establishing the miracle as eloquence and the dispute over minimal surah length.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the chosen view that the Quran's miracle is its eloquence and the majority position that the shortest surah suffices for the challenge, as well as a nuanced middle view.

Core Idea
The Quran's miracle is eloquence, and even its shortest surah suffices for the challenge.

This passage from Sanusi establishes a foundational point in the study of miracles (i'jāz): what exactly is the miraculous element of the Quran? The author presents the chosen view that it is eloquence (balāghah) and then addresses a practical question: how much of the Quran constitutes the challenge? Three scholarly positions are examined, and the majority view is favoured.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author first states the chosen view about eloquence, then moves to the dispute about the surah length.
The passage uses technical terms like 'āy al-ta'jīz' (verses of incapacitation).
2
Identify the three positions
The passage presents three opinions on what constitutes the challenging surah. Find each one and note the reasoning given for and against them.
The first is rejected, the second is the majority view, the third is a middle position.
3
Consider the implications
Reflect on why the majority view is that the shortest surah suffices. What does this say about the nature of the Quran's miracle?
The indefinite word 'surah' in the challenge points to any surah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا تقرر أن المعجز على المختار المتحدى به البلاغة، وأن التحدي قد استقر بالإتيان بسورة .. فقد قال بعض الأصحاب : هذه السورة هي المشتملة على آي التعجيز، وهذا ضعيف، لأن لفظ سورة فيها منكر مطلق، فلا يقيد بمثلها قدراً.
وقال الجمهور من أصحابنا : يكفي أقصر سورة ; كالعصر والكوثر.
والذي ارتضاه القاضي في كتاب النقض وارتضاه أبو إسحاق : أن الإعجاز إنما يتعلق بقدر ما من الكلام ; بحيث يتبين فيه تفاضل ذوي البلاغة، وهذا لا يتبين إلا فيما طال من السور بعض الطول،
وقال: وهذا لا يضبط بحروف وكلم، وإنما يصار في مثله إلى المتعارف من أهل الخبرة والدراية بالبلاغة والنظم
And when it is established that, according to the chosen view, the miracle by which the challenge is made is eloquence (balāghah), and that the challenge has been settled on producing a surah, some of the companions said: 'This surah is the one that contains the verses of incapacitation.' And this is weak, because the word 'surah' is indefinite and unqualified, so it is not restricted by something like it in quantity. And the majority of our companions said: 'The shortest surah suffices, like al-Asr and al-Kawthar.' And the view which the judge (al-Qadi) approved in the book 'al-Naqd' and which Abu Ishaq approved is that the miraculousness is connected to a certain amount of speech in which the superiority of people of eloquence becomes evident, and this is not evident except in surahs that are somewhat long. And he said: 'This is not fixed by letters or words; rather, in such a case one defers to the common practice of the people of expertise and knowledge of eloquence and composition.'
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage establishes the chosen definition of the Quran's miracle and then discusses a subsidiary issue: how much of the Quran constitutes the challenge. The author begins by stating that, on the preferred view, the miracle is the eloquence (balāghah) itself, and the challenge was definitively to produce a surah. Then three positions are reported. First, some companions held that the challenging surah must be one that contains the 'verses of incapacitation' (āy al-ta'jīz), i.e., a specific set of long suras. The author rejects this as weak because the word 'surah' in the challenge is indefinite (nakirah mufradah), so it refers to any surah, not a specific type or length. Second, the majority of the author's school (al-Ash'aris) say the shortest surah, like Surat al-Asr or al-Kawthar, suffices. Third, the judge (al-Qadi) and Abu Ishaq take a middle position: the miracle requires a minimum amount of speech sufficient to demonstrate the difference in eloquence among skilled speakers. This is not a fixed number of letters or words but is determined by the conventional judgment of experts in eloquence and composition. Thus the passage clarifies that the miracle is in the eloquence, not in any other quality, and that the shortest surah is sufficient for the challenge, though there is a nuance about how short is short.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the miracle is the entire Quran or that a long surah is needed to show eloquence.
Correction: The majority hold that even the shortest surah suffices, because the challenge was to produce any surah, and the word 'surah' is indefinite.

Checkpoint

C) It must be a surah of at least a certain number of verses.
D) Only the Fatiha suffices.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's miracle is its eloquence, and the challenge is to produce any surah, with the shortest sufficient according to the majority.

Disagreement About I'jāz Does Not Undermine the Miracle
The author refutes that disagreement on i'jāz's nature implies doubt about its miraculousness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that disagreement among scholars about the precise nature of the Quran's inimitability does not cast doubt on the fact that the Quran is a miraculous revelation.

Core Idea
Disagreement about how the Quran is miraculous does not mean it isn't.

This passage addresses a common objection: if scholars cannot agree on what makes the Quran inimitable, perhaps the miracle itself is uncertain. The author responds by distinguishing between the undeniable fact of human inability to match the Quran and the secondary debate about the exact mechanism of that inability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector lists various positions on i'jāz (naẓm, ṣarf, etc.) and argues that such disagreement implies the miracle is not evident to all.
Notice how each position denies certain aspects of the miracle.
2
Identify the author's central claim in the answer
He insists that the sheer inability of anyone to produce a sūra like the Quran is known and undisputed.
This is the foundation of the miracle's evident nature.
3
Examine the distinction he makes
The disagreement is only about the aspect of i'jāz, not about whether the Quran is a miracle.
This preserves certainty while allowing scholarly inquiry.
4
Reflect on the implication
This distinction protects the certainty of the miracle while allowing scholarly inquiry into its nature.
The author references his earlier work for a fuller treatment.

وبلاغتُه ، ثم اختلفتُمُ اختلافاً كثيراً على ما نقلتُموهُ مِنْ تفاصيل الأقوالِ في ذلكَ ، فإِنَّ مَنْ يزعمُ أَنَّ ذلكَ هو النظمُ فقط فقد أنكر كونَ الفصاحةِ والجزالة فيهِ مُعجزاً ، وبالعكس ، ومَنْ زعمَ أَنَّهُ الصَّرْفُ فقد أنكرَ الوجهين جميعاً، ومَنْ قالَ بغيرِ الصَّرْفِ فقد أنكر كونَ الصَّرْفِ مُعجزاً، وحق المعجزة أن تكون ظاهرة للكلِّ بحيثُ لا يُسترابُ فيها ألبتة . والجواب : أنَّ عجْزَ الخَلْقِ عن معارضته بسورةٍ مِنْ مثلِهِ معلوم ظاهر لا يُسترابُ فيهِ ألبتة ، ولم يختلف فيه أحدٌ ، وبهذا يُعرَفُ كونُهُ معجزةً ، والاختلافُ بعد ذلك في وجهِ إعجازه لا يقتضي الخلاف في كونه معجزةً ولا في عدم ظهورِ ذلك ، وإنَّما خلاف هو في تحقيق الوجه الذي جاءَ منه الإعجازُ ، وقد بيَّنا في أصل العقيدة عجْزَ البلغاء عن معارضته بياناً شافياً لا يحتاج إلى شرح .
And its eloquence. Then you differed greatly according to what you have transmitted of the detailed opinions about that. For whoever claims that it is only the composition (al-naẓm) has denied that eloquence (al-faṣāḥa) and fluency (al-jazāla) in it are miraculous, and vice versa. And whoever claims that it is the turning away (al-ṣarf) has denied both aspects, and whoever says other than al-ṣarf has denied that al-ṣarf is miraculous. And the right of the miracle is that it be evident to all such that there is absolutely no doubt about it. The answer: that the inability of creation to oppose it with a sūra like it is known, evident, absolutely undoubted, and no one has disagreed about it. By this, its being a miracle is known, and the disagreement thereafter about the aspect of its inimitability (iʿjāz) does not entail disagreement about its being a miracle nor about its not being apparent. Rather, the disagreement is only about verifying the aspect from which the inimitability came. And we have explained in the foundation of the creed (Aṣl al-ʿAqīda) the inability of the eloquent to oppose it with a sufficient explanation that needs no elaboration.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The author first presents an objection: people have differed greatly about the nature of the Quran's miraculousness—some say it is only the composition (naẓm), others say it is the turning away (ṣarf), etc. Each position denies certain aspects. The objector claims that such disagreement shows the miracle is not evident to all. The author answers by distinguishing between the fact of the miracle and its specific modality. He asserts that the inability of mankind to produce even a single sūra like the Quran is known and undisputed; this suffices to prove its miraculousness. The subsequent disagreement about the precise aspect (whether naẓm, ṣarf, or both) does not affect the clear, evident nature of the miracle. The author concludes by referencing his fuller treatment of the topic in Aṣl al-ʿAqīda.

Key Terms
إعجاز
معجزة
نظم
صرف
فصاحة
جزالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If scholars cannot agree on the nature of i'jāz, then the Quran's miraculousness is doubtful.
Correction: The inability to produce a similar sūra is clear and undisputed; disagreement about the specific aspect (e.g., naẓm, ṣarf) does not affect the certainty of the miracle.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct answer to the objection that disagreement about the nature of i'jāz undermines the miracle?
A) The miracle is not evident to all because of the disagreement.
B) The disagreement is only about the specific aspect of i'jāz, not about the fact that the Quran is a miracle.
C) The disagreement proves that the Quran is not a miracle.
D) The miracle is only the composition (naẓm), not the eloquence.

Key Takeaway
The fact of the Quran's inimitability is evident and undisputed; scholarly debate about its precise aspect does not affect its miraculous status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt shaken when you hear scholars disagree about details of faith? How does this passage challenge you to separate what is absolutely certain from what is open to interpretation? What will you do this week to anchor yourself in the undeniable truths of Islam?
?
Is your trust in the Quran's divine origin based on personal reflection on its unique beauty and inability to be imitated, or do you rely solely on the opinions of scholars? What specific step can you take today to witness this miracle for yourself?
?
When you encounter differing opinions about the Quran's inimitability, does it ever cause you to doubt its miraculous nature, even slightly? How does this lesson call you to hold fast to the undisputed fact of its inimitability? What will you memorize from the Quran this week as a personal challenge to produce something like it?

Miracles
beginner
5 min
Fulfilled Prophecies in Quran and Sunnah
Enumeration of fulfilled prophecies from Quran and Sunnah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how fulfilled prophecies from the Quran and Sunnah serve as proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
Fulfilled prophecies are a clear proof that the Prophet spoke the truth.

In this passage, the author presents concrete examples of prophecies made by the Prophet (peace be upon him) that were fulfilled exactly as foretold. These examples are drawn from both the Quran and the hadith, demonstrating that the Prophet's knowledge of the unseen came from Allah. By examining these prophecies, we can see how historical events matched the predictions, confirming the truth of his message.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the author first mentions prophecies from the Quran then from the hadith.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage divides prophecies into those mentioned in the Quran and those mentioned in the hadith. How many examples are given in each?
3
Examine each prophecy
For each prophecy, check the prediction and how it actually happened. The author provides brief explanations. What is common among them?
4
Reflect on the argument
Think about why fulfilled prophecies are considered a proof of prophethood. How do these examples strengthen belief in the Prophet's truthfulness?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خبرَهُ ؛ فمنها ما ورد في القرآنِ ، ومنها ما ورد في الأخبار
أما الذي ورد في القرآن :
فمنه : قوله تعالى : ( وَهُم مِّن بَعْدِ غَلَبِهِمْ سَيَغْلِبُونَ ) [الروم: ٣] وكانَ كما أخبرَ
وقوله تعالى : ( إِنَّ الَّذِى فَرَضَ عَلَيْكَ الْقُرْءَانَ لَرَادُّكَ إِلَى مَعَادٍ ) [ القصص : ٨٥] أي: إلى مكة ، وقد ردَّهُ الله تعالى إليها
وقوله تعالى : (قُل لِلْمُخَلَّفِينَ مِنَ الْأَعْرَابِ سَتُدْعَوْنَ إِلَى قَوْمٍ أُولِي بَأْسٍ شَدِيدٍ ) [الفتح : ١٦] ، وقد وقع ذلك
وقوله تعالى : ( وَعَدَ اللهُ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا مِنكُمْ وَعَمِلُوا الصَّلِحَتِ لَيَسْتَخْلِفَنَّهُمْ فِي الْأَرْضِ ) ، والمراد بهمُ : الصحابة رضي الله تعالى عنهم ؛ بدليل قولِهِ : مِنكُم ، وبدليل قوله : ( وَلَيُبَدِّلَنَّهُم مِّنْ بَعْدِ خَوْفِهِمْ أَمَنًا ) [النور : ٥٥] ، وكانوا هم الخائفين في صدر الإسلام
وأما الذي ورد في الأخبار :
فمنه : قولُهُ عليهِ الصلاةُ والسلامُ : « الْخِلَافَةُ بَعْدِي ثَلَاثُونَ سَنَةٌ » ، وكانَتْ خلافة الخلفاء الراشدين هذا القدر
وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : « أَقْتَدُوا بِاللَّذَيْنِ بَعْدِي ؛ أَبِي بَكْرٍ وَعُمَرَ » ، وهذا إخبارٌ عن بقائهما بعدَهُ ، فكانَ ذلكَ
وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لعمَّارٍ رضيَ اللهُ عنهُ : « تَقْتُلُكَ الْفِئَةُ الْبَاغِيَةُ » ، فقُتِلَ معَ علي رضي الله عنهُ يومَ صفّينَ ، وهذا أيضاً يدلُّ على خلافة عليّ رضي الله تعالى عنه بعدَهُ
His prophecies; some of them are mentioned in the Qur'an, and some of them are mentioned in the reports (al-akhbār).
As for what is mentioned in the Qur'an:
From them is His statement, Most High: (And they, after their defeat, will be victorious) [Q 30:3], and it happened as He informed.
And His statement, Most High: (Indeed, He who imposed upon you the Qur'an will return you to a place of return) [Q 28:85], meaning to Mecca, and Allah, Most High, returned him to it.
And His statement, Most High: (Say to those who remained behind of the bedouins, 'You will be called to a people of great might') [Q 48:16], and that occurred.
And His statement, Most High: (Allah has promised those who have believed among you and done righteous deeds that He will surely grant them succession in the land) — and what is meant by them is the Companions (aṣ-ṣaḥābah), may Allah be pleased with them; by the evidence of His saying: 'among you', and by the evidence of His saying: (and He will surely substitute for them, after their fear, security) [Q 24:55], and they were the fearful ones in early Islam.
As for what is mentioned in the reports:
From them is his statement, peace and prayer be upon him: 'The caliphate after me is thirty years', and the caliphate of the Rightly Guided Caliphs was this amount.
And his statement, peace and prayer be upon him: 'Follow the two after me: Abu Bakr and 'Umar' — and this is an announcement of their remaining after him, and so it was.
And his statement, peace and prayer be upon him, to 'Ammār, may Allah be pleased with him: 'You will be killed by the rebellious group' — so he was killed with 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, on the day of Ṣiffīn, and this also indicates the caliphate of 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, after him.
Source pages p. 593

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of examples of fulfilled prophecies from the Qur'an and Sunnah, serving as proof of the truth of prophethood. The author first introduces that some of these prophecies come from the Qur'an and some from hadith. Then he lists four Qur'anic verses that were fulfilled: (1) the Romans' victory over Persians after their defeat (Q 30:3), (2) the Prophet's return to Mecca (Q 28:85), (3) the call to fight a people of great might (Q 48:16), and (4) the promise of succession and security for the believers (Q 24:55), which was fulfilled by the Companions. Then he lists three hadith: (1) the caliphate lasting thirty years, which matched the period of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, (2) the command to follow Abu Bakr and 'Umar, confirming they would remain after the Prophet, and (3) the prophecy about 'Ammār being killed by the rebellious group, which occurred at the Battle of Ṣiffīn and also indicates the caliphate of 'Ali. Each example is briefly explained as having actually occurred. The author uses these fulfilled prophecies as clear evidence that the Prophet (peace be upon him) spoke the truth.

Key Terms
أخباره (prophecies)
القرآن (Qur'an)
الأخبار (hadith, reports)
خلافة (caliphate)
فئة باغية (rebellious group)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which hadith indicates the caliphate of 'Ali?
A) "The caliphate after me is thirty years."
B) "Follow the two after me: Abu Bakr and 'Umar."
C) "You will be killed by the rebellious group."
D) "The Romans will be victorious."

Key Takeaway
Fulfilled prophecies from both the Quran and Sunnah provide undeniable evidence that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was truthful and his message divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every single prophecy from the Quran and Sunnah has come true exactly as foretold, what does that mean for the prophecies that have not yet been fulfilled—such as those about the end of time? Are you preparing for them with the same certainty as if they happened yesterday?
?
The Prophet promised the Companions security after fear, and it came true. Look at your own life: what fears dominate you right now? Have you truly internalized that the same Allah who fulfilled that promise is also in control of your situation? What would it look like for you to live today with trust in His promise rather than fear of your circumstances?
?
The hadith prophecies about the caliphate and the companions were so precise that they could only have come from divine knowledge. If you claim to believe that the Prophet spoke the truth in these matters, how does that belief affect your obedience to his commands in your daily life? Where is the gap between your intellectual acceptance and your actual practice, and what will you do about it before tonight?
